01

Kotlin Essentials

Language features interviewers test to gauge Kotlin maturity. Skip what you know, refresh the rest.

Q.What's the difference between val, var, and const val?
High Freq
+

val — read-only reference (runtime constant). Value assigned once but can be computed at runtime.

var — mutable reference, can be reassigned.

const val — compile-time constant. Must be a top-level or object/companion object property of a primitive or String type. Inlined at compile time.

const val API_VERSION = "v3"           // compile-time
val timestamp = System.currentTimeMillis()  // runtime
var counter = 0                        // mutable
Gotcha: A val with a custom getter can return different values per call — it's read-only, not immutable.
Q.Explain data classes, what does the compiler generate?
High Freq
+

A data class auto-generates equals(), hashCode(), toString(), copy(), and componentN() functions based on the properties in the primary constructor.

data class User(val id: String, val name: String)

val a = User("1", "Rutul")
val b = a.copy(name = "RT")  // structural copy
val (id, name) = a           // destructuring

Limits: can't be abstract, open, sealed, or inner. Properties declared in the class body (not constructor) are NOT included in generated methods.

Q.What's the difference between ?., !!, ?:, and let?
🔥
+
  • ?. — safe call. Returns null if receiver is null.
  • !! — non-null assertion. Throws NullPointerException if null. Code smell in 95% of cases.
  • ?: — Elvis operator. Returns left side if non-null, else right side.
  • let — scope function. Combined with ?., executes only if non-null and returns the result.
val name: String? = getName()
val length = name?.length ?: 0          // null-safe with default
name?.let { println("Hello $it") }     // run block only if non-null
Q.Compare the scope functions: let, run, with, apply, also
🔥
+
FunctionReceiverReturnsUse for
letitlambda resultNull checks, transforming a value
runthislambda resultObject config + computing a result
withthislambda resultCalling multiple methods on an object
applythisthe objectBuilder-style configuration
alsoitthe objectSide effects (logging, debugging)
 
val intent = Intent(ctx, MainActivity::class.java).apply {
    putExtra("id", 42)
    flags = Intent.FLAG_ACTIVITY_NEW_TASK
}  // returns intent

user?.let { println(it.name) }  // null-safe
val len = name?.run { length }  // null-safe + compute
Q.What is a sealed class? When use it over enum?
L5+
+

A sealed class restricts the class hierarchy to a known, compile-time-fixed set of subclasses defined in the same module. Used for representing restricted state with rich payloads.

sealed class UiState<out T> {
    object Loading : UiState<Nothing>()
    data class Success<T>(val data: T) : UiState<T>()
    data class Error(val msg: String, val retry: () -> Unit) : UiState<Nothing>()
}

when (state) {
    Loading -> showSpinner()
    is Success -> render(state.data)
    is Error -> showError(state.msg)
    // compiler enforces exhaustiveness — no else needed
}

vs Enum: Enums have fixed instances with the same properties. Sealed classes can carry different data per variant and have multiple instances. Use sealed for state machines, error types, and ADT-style modeling. Use enum for simple constants.

Q.Explain extension functions and how they differ from member functions.
+

Extension functions add behavior to a class without modifying it. They're resolved statically — the declared type at the call site determines which extension is called, not the runtime type.

fun String.isValidEmail(): Boolean = matches(Regex("..."))
"hello".isValidEmail()

open class A
class B : A()
fun A.greet() = "from A"
fun B.greet() = "from B"
val a: A = B()
a.greet()  // "from A" — static dispatch, NOT polymorphic
Gotcha: Extension functions don't override member functions. If a class has a member foo(), an extension with the same name and signature is shadowed.
Q.What does inline do, and when use reified?
L5+
+

inline — the compiler copies the function body (including its lambda parameters) directly into the call site. Eliminates lambda allocation overhead. Used for high-frequency higher-order functions.

reified — only available in inline functions. Lets you access the type parameter at runtime (normally erased by JVM generics).

inline fun <reified T> Bundle.getOrNull(key: String): T? {
    return when (T::class) {
        String::class -> getString(key) as T?
        Int::class -> getInt(key) as T?
        else -> null
    }
}

val name = bundle.getOrNull<String>("name")
Q.lateinit vs by lazy — when use each?
🔥
+
lateinit varby lazy
Mutabilityvar (mutable)val (immutable)
Types allowedNon-null objects (not primitives)Any type
InitializationManually, before first readOn first access (cached)
Thread safetyNone — you handle itSynchronized by default
Use forDI-injected fields, setUp() in testsExpensive computed properties
 
// lateinit — must initialize before use
@Inject lateinit var api: ApiService
override fun onCreate() { /* api injected here */ }
if (::api.isInitialized) { /* check helper */ }

// by lazy — first access triggers init
val repo by lazy { Repository(api, db) }
Q.What is a companion object?
+

The Kotlin equivalent of Java's static members. A companion object is a singleton tied to its enclosing class — used for factory methods, constants, and class-level utilities.

class User private constructor(val id: String) {
    companion object {
        const val MAX_LEN = 32
        fun create(raw: String) = User(raw.take(MAX_LEN))
    }
}

User.create("rutul")   // factory
User.MAX_LEN           // constant

Gotcha: companion object methods are NOT real static methods — they're instance methods on the companion singleton. Use @JvmStatic for true static access from Java.

Q.noinline and crossinline — what do they do?
L5+
+

Both apply to lambda parameters of inline functions:

  • noinline — opt out one lambda from inlining (e.g., to store it in a variable or pass to another function).
  • crossinline — forbids non-local returns from the lambda. Required when the lambda is invoked from another execution context (e.g., a Runnable).
inline fun doWork(
    crossinline onSuccess: () -> Unit,
    noinline onError: (Throwable) -> Unit
) {
    thread {
        try { /* work */; onSuccess() }   // crossinline: can't `return`
        catch (e: Throwable) { onError(e) } // noinline: can be stored
    }
}
Q.What are property delegates? Explain by lazy, by observable, by Delegates.notNull().
L5+
+

Delegation lets you outsource getter/setter logic to another object. Common stdlib delegates:

// Lazy — computed once, cached
val config by lazy { loadConfig() }

// Observable — callback on change
var name: String by Delegates.observable("init") { _, old, new ->
    log("$old -> $new")
}

// Vetoable — can reject a change
var age: Int by Delegates.vetoable(0) { _, _, new -> new >= 0 }

// notNull — non-null val that's set later (alternative to lateinit for primitives)
var count: Int by Delegates.notNull()

// Map delegation
class User(map: Map<String, Any?>) {
    val name: String by map
    val age: Int by map
}

Custom delegate = implement ReadWriteProperty<T, V> (or ReadOnlyProperty).

Q.Higher-order functions and lambdas — what are they?
+

Higher-order function = function that takes a function as parameter or returns one.

Lambda = anonymous function literal. In Kotlin, the last lambda argument can be moved outside parentheses (trailing lambda syntax).

// Higher-order — takes a function
inline fun <T> List<T>.firstMatching(predicate: (T) -> Boolean): T? {
    for (item in this) if (predicate(item)) return item
    return null
}

users.firstMatching { it.isActive }  // trailing lambda

// Higher-order — returns a function
fun multiplier(factor: Int): (Int) -> Int = { it * factor }
val double = multiplier(2)
double(5)  // 10
Q.What are inline classes (now value class)?
L5+
+

A class with a single value that's compiled away at runtime — represented as the underlying type, not a wrapper object. Used for type safety without allocation overhead.

@JvmInline
value class UserId(val raw: String)

@JvmInline
value class Email(val raw: String)

fun lookup(id: UserId): User? { ... }
lookup(UserId("123"))  // type-safe
lookup(Email("x"))     // compile error — different type

Limits: exactly one property in constructor, no init blocks, no backing fields beyond the wrapped value.

Q.What's the difference between open, final, and abstract in Kotlin?
+
  • Default — classes and methods are final (can't be subclassed/overridden). Opposite of Java.
  • open — explicitly allows subclassing or overriding.
  • abstract — must be overridden; cannot be instantiated. Implicitly open.

Why default-final: better design discipline. Inheritance is opt-in, not default. Reduces fragile-base-class problems.

Q.What are visibility modifiers in Kotlin?
+
ModifierTop-level (file)Class member
public (default)EverywhereEverywhere
internalSame moduleSame module
privateSame fileSame class
protectedN/AClass + subclasses

internal is critical for multi-module projects — keeps APIs encapsulated to the module without going public.

Q.How do you create a Singleton in Kotlin?
+

Use object — Kotlin's built-in singleton primitive. Thread-safe, lazy-initialized on first access.

object Analytics {
    fun track(event: String) { ... }
}

Analytics.track("screen_view")

For singletons that need a constructor or Context, use a regular class with DI (Hilt @Singleton) instead of double-checked locking.

Q.What do @JvmStatic, @JvmField, @JvmOverloads do?
+

JVM interop annotations — change how Kotlin generates bytecode so Java callers see expected APIs.

  • @JvmStatic — generate a true static method (for companion object/object methods)
  • @JvmField — expose the property as a public field, no getter/setter generated
  • @JvmOverloads — generate overloads for each default-parameter combination, so Java callers can use them
class Snackbar(
    val view: View,
    val text: String,
    val duration: Int = 2000,
    val action: String? = null
) {
    @JvmOverloads
    fun show() { ... }
    
    companion object {
        @JvmStatic
        fun make(view: View, text: String) = Snackbar(view, text)
    }
}
02

Jetpack Compose

The single highest-weight technical topic at L5 Android interviews in 2026. Recomposition rules, state, side effects are the hot zone.

Q.How does recomposition work? What triggers it?
🔥
+

Recomposition is Compose re-executing composable functions when their inputs change. Triggered when a state read inside a composable observes a value change.

Triggers:

  • Reading a MutableState whose value changes
  • Reading a StateFlow via collectAsState() that emits
  • A new key for remember(key) or LaunchedEffect(key)

Compose tracks per-read dependencies — only composables that actually read changed state recompose. Smart skipping means stable, unchanged params skip the function entirely.

Q.Explain remember vs rememberSaveable vs derivedStateOf.
🔥
+
  • remember — stores a value across recompositions but NOT across configuration changes or process death.
  • rememberSaveable — stores across config changes (rotation) and process death via Bundle. Works with primitives, Parcelable, or custom Saver.
  • derivedStateOf — derives a state from other state, triggering recomposition only when the derived result actually changes. Avoids unnecessary recomp when intermediate state changes don't affect output.
// remember — recreated on rotation
val scrollState = rememberScrollState()

// rememberSaveable — survives rotation
var text by rememberSaveable { mutableStateOf("") }

// derivedStateOf — only recomposes when isFirstVisible changes,
// not on every scroll position update
val showFab by remember {
    derivedStateOf { listState.firstVisibleItemIndex > 0 }
}
Q.Explain all the side-effect APIs: LaunchedEffect, DisposableEffect, SideEffect, rememberCoroutineScope, produceState, snapshotFlow.
🔥Tricky
+
APIWhen it runsUse case
LaunchedEffect(key)Composition enters; restarts when key changes; cancels on leaveRun suspending work tied to composition
DisposableEffect(key)Same as LaunchedEffect; has onDispose for cleanupSubscribe to non-coroutine listeners
SideEffectAfter every successful recompositionSync Compose state to non-Compose objects
rememberCoroutineScopeReturns a scope tied to composition lifetimeLaunch coroutines from event handlers
produceStateProduces a State from coroutine workConvert async results to State
snapshotFlowConverts State reads to a FlowObserve state changes as a stream
 
// LaunchedEffect — one-shot suspend work
LaunchedEffect(userId) {
    user = repository.fetchUser(userId)
}

// DisposableEffect — listener cleanup
DisposableEffect(lifecycleOwner) {
    val observer = LifecycleEventObserver { _, e -> ... }
    lifecycleOwner.lifecycle.addObserver(observer)
    onDispose { lifecycleOwner.lifecycle.removeObserver(observer) }
}

// rememberCoroutineScope — event-driven
val scope = rememberCoroutineScope()
Button(onClick = { scope.launch { saveUser() } }) { ... }

// snapshotFlow — react to state as a flow
LaunchedEffect(listState) {
    snapshotFlow { listState.firstVisibleItemIndex }
        .distinctUntilChanged()
        .collect { trackScroll(it) }
}
Q.What is state hoisting and why does it matter?
🔥
+

State hoisting moves state OUT of a composable to its caller, making the composable stateless. The caller passes value down and onValueChange up — the unidirectional data flow pattern.

// ❌ Stateful — hard to test, can't be controlled by parent
@Composable
fun Counter() {
    var count by remember { mutableStateOf(0) }
    Button(onClick = { count++ }) { Text("$count") }
}

// ✅ Stateless — hoisted state
@Composable
fun Counter(count: Int, onIncrement: () -> Unit) {
    Button(onClick = onIncrement) { Text("$count") }
}

Benefits: Single source of truth, easier to share state across UI, testable, supports snapshotting and undo.

Q.What are @Stable and @Immutable annotations?
🔥
+

Both are contracts with the Compose compiler that let it skip recomposition for parameters that haven't changed structurally.

  • @Immutable — promises all public properties are immutable AND equals() is well-behaved. Strongest guarantee.
  • @Stable — promises that changes to public properties will be communicated to Compose (mutable but observable).
Why care: If Compose can't prove a param is stable (e.g., List<T> from kotlin stdlib is unstable), it recomposes every time, even when nothing changed. Wrap with ImmutableList from kotlinx.collections.immutable or use @Immutable wrapper classes for perf-critical screens.
Q.How do you collect a StateFlow in Compose? Lifecycle-aware?
+
// ✅ Lifecycle-aware — pauses when app backgrounded
val state by viewModel.uiState.collectAsStateWithLifecycle()

// ❌ Not lifecycle-aware — keeps collecting in background
val state by viewModel.uiState.collectAsState()

collectAsStateWithLifecycle() (from androidx.lifecycle.compose) pauses collection when the lifecycle is below STARTED, preventing wasted work and crashes from background UI updates. Always prefer this for ViewModel-exposed flows.

Q.What does key() composable do?
+

Forces Compose to treat a sub-tree as a distinct identity. By default, Compose identifies composables by their position in the call tree. key(id) overrides this with explicit identity.

// Without key — reordering causes state loss/swap
items.forEach { item ->
    EditableRow(item)  // remember state tied to position
}

// With key — state follows the item
items.forEach { item ->
    key(item.id) {
        EditableRow(item)
    }
}

Critical for: reorderable lists, animated insertions/removals, preserving scroll state in LazyColumn (use the key param, not key() composable).

Q.Why does Modifier order matter?
L5+
+

Modifiers are applied left-to-right (in the order chained) and each wraps the next. The result depends on order.

// Padding is OUTSIDE the background
Modifier.padding(16.dp).background(Red)
// → padding around a smaller red area

// Padding is INSIDE the background
Modifier.background(Red).padding(16.dp)
// → red fills full area, content inset by padding

Same applies to clickable, border, clip, size. Rule of thumb: size/layout first, then decorations.

Q.How do you optimize a LazyColumn?
+
  • Stable keys: items(list, key = { it.id }) — preserves state across reorders
  • ContentType: items(list, contentType = { it::class }) — enables view recycling across types
  • Avoid heavy work in item composables — hoist expensive computations
  • Stable item models — use @Immutable data classes
  • Don't read state in the item lambda if it changes frequently — read it inside the leaf
  • Defer reads with lambda params for hot animations: Modifier.offset { ... } instead of Modifier.offset(x.dp)
Q.What's the Compose phase model? (Composition → Layout → Drawing)
L5+
+

Every frame, Compose runs three phases:

  1. Composition — execute composable functions, build/update the UI tree
  2. Layout — measure and place each node (Measure → Place)
  3. Drawing — paint to the canvas

Why it matters: if you can defer reads to a later phase, fewer composables recompose. Reading state in Modifier.offset { x.value } (lambda, runs in Layout phase) skips Composition entirely when only x changes — huge perf win for animations.

Q.How does CompositionLocal work? When use it?
+

An implicit way to pass values down the composition tree without threading them through every parameter. Examples: LocalContext, LocalDensity, MaterialTheme.colorScheme.

val LocalUser = compositionLocalOf<User> { error("No user") }

CompositionLocalProvider(LocalUser provides currentUser) {
    Screen()  // anywhere inside: LocalUser.current
}
Use sparingly. Implicit deps are hard to track. Reach for it for cross-cutting concerns (theme, density, user session). Don't use it as a DI replacement for screen logic.
Q.How do you write a Compose unit test?
+
@get:Rule
val composeTestRule = createComposeRule()

@Test
fun counter_incrementsOnClick() {
    composeTestRule.setContent {
        Counter()
    }
    composeTestRule.onNodeWithText("0").assertExists()
    composeTestRule.onNodeWithText("+").performClick()
    composeTestRule.onNodeWithText("1").assertExists()
}

For ViewModels/state, use createAndroidComposeRule if you need an Activity. For isolation, prefer createComposeRule.

Q.How does Compose Navigation work?
+

A single NavHost contains the navigation graph. Each route is a composable. NavController manages the back stack.

val nav = rememberNavController()
NavHost(nav, startDestination = "home") {
    composable("home") { HomeScreen(onItem = { nav.navigate("detail/$it") }) }
    composable("detail/{id}", arguments = listOf(navArgument("id") { type = NavType.StringType })) {
        DetailScreen(id = it.arguments?.getString("id")!!)
    }
}

Type-safe Navigation (since 2.8.0) uses @Serializable data classes as routes — eliminates string-based args. Prefer this in 2026.

Q.How would you debug excessive recomposition?
L5+
+
  • Layout Inspector — counts recompositions per composable
  • Compose Compiler Metrics — emits a report showing which params are unstable and which composables are skippable
  • Compose Compiler Reports in build.gradle — kotlinOptions { freeCompilerArgs += ... }
  • Macrobenchmark + baseline profiles — measure scroll perf in production-like conditions
Common fixes: wrap lists in ImmutableList, hoist state higher, defer reads to layout phase, split large composables into smaller ones to localize recomp.
Q.Compose State APIs — quick cheatsheet
+
APISurvives Recomp?Survives Rotation?Survives Process Death?
mutableStateOf❌❌❌
remember { ... }✅❌❌
rememberSaveable { ... }✅✅✅
ViewModel state✅✅❌ (use SavedStateHandle for ✅)
03

Coroutines & Flow

Asynchronous code is where senior interviews dig deep. Structured concurrency, dispatchers, exception propagation are the foundation.

Q.What is structured concurrency?
🔥
+

The principle that every coroutine has a parent scope, forms a tree, and the parent doesn't complete until all children complete. Eliminates leaked coroutines and ensures cancellation propagates correctly.

The 3 guarantees:
  • A scope completes only when all its children complete
  • Cancelling a scope cancels all its children
  • An unhandled exception in a child cancels the scope (default)

The opposite is GlobalScope — coroutines launched there have no parent and live until the process dies. Almost always wrong.

Q.launch vs async — when use each?
🔥
+
  • launch — fire and forget. Returns a Job. Use when you don't need a result.
  • async — concurrent computation. Returns a Deferred<T> you await() on. Use for parallel work that returns a value.
// Parallel fetch
coroutineScope {
    val user = async { api.getUser() }
    val prefs = async { db.getPrefs() }
    combine(user.await(), prefs.await())
}
Exception gotcha: exceptions in async are stored in the Deferred and rethrown on await(). But the exception ALSO immediately cancels the parent scope (unless the scope is a SupervisorJob). Don't rely on try/catch around await() alone.
Q.coroutineScope vs supervisorScope — what's the difference?
🔥
+
  • coroutineScope — failure in any child cancels all siblings and the scope itself.
  • supervisorScope — failure in one child does NOT cancel siblings. Each child fails independently.
// Independent operations — one failure shouldn't kill others
supervisorScope {
    launch { fetchUser() }       // if this fails...
    launch { fetchPrefs() }      // ...this still completes
    launch { fetchNotifs() }
}

// Tightly coupled — all or nothing
coroutineScope {
    val a = async { stepA() }
    val b = async { stepB() }
    combine(a.await(), b.await())  // one fails → both cancelled
}

Same applies to Job vs SupervisorJob for long-lived scopes like viewModelScope.

Q.How does cancellation propagate?
🔥
+

Cooperative cancellation: code must check isActive or call suspending functions that throw CancellationException. CPU-bound loops won't be cancelled unless you check.

// ❌ Not cancellable
while (true) { heavyCompute() }

// ✅ Cancellable
while (isActive) { heavyCompute() }
// or call yield() periodically
Q.What are the standard Dispatchers and when use each?
🔥
+
DispatcherBacking threadsUse for
Dispatchers.Main1 (UI thread)UI updates, calls to LiveData/State
Dispatchers.IOUp to 64 (elastic pool)Network, disk, DB — blocking I/O
Dispatchers.Default= CPU coresCPU-intensive: sorting, parsing, image transforms
Dispatchers.Main.immediateMain threadAlready on Main? Skip the dispatch
Dispatchers.UnconfinedCaller's thread initiallyTests, niche cases. Avoid in production.
 
// Switch contexts cleanly with withContext
suspend fun loadImage(url: String): Bitmap = withContext(Dispatchers.IO) {
    val bytes = api.download(url)
    withContext(Dispatchers.Default) {
        BitmapFactory.decodeByteArray(bytes, 0, bytes.size)
    }
}
Q.Cold vs Hot flows — what's the difference?
🔥
+
  • Cold flow (Flow) — produces values only when collected. Each collector gets its own emission sequence. Like a function being called.
  • Hot flow (StateFlow, SharedFlow) — emits regardless of collectors. New collectors get the current value (StateFlow) or replay cache (SharedFlow).
TypeInitial valueConflated?Use for
StateFlowRequiredYes (only latest)UI state
SharedFlowOptional replayConfigurableOne-shot events (errors, navigation)
FlowNone — coldNoData streams from sources
Q.What does flatMapLatest do vs flatMapConcat vs flatMapMerge?
+
  • flatMapLatest — cancels previous inner flow when a new outer value arrives. Use for search-as-you-type.
  • flatMapConcat — waits for previous inner flow to complete before starting next. Use for sequential operations.
  • flatMapMerge — runs all inner flows concurrently. Use for parallel work.
// Search as you type — cancel old query when input changes
searchQueryFlow
    .debounce(300)
    .flatMapLatest { query -> api.search(query) }
    .collect { showResults(it) }
Q.Explain combine, zip, and merge.
+
  • combine — emits when ANY source emits, using latest values from all. Reactive UI state from multiple sources.
  • zip — pairs emissions by index. Waits for both. Stops when either completes.
  • merge — interleaves emissions from multiple flows. No pairing.
// UI state from user + prefs + connectivity
combine(userFlow, prefsFlow, connectivityFlow) { user, prefs, online ->
    UiState(user, prefs, online)
}.stateIn(viewModelScope, SharingStarted.WhileSubscribed(5000), UiState.Loading)
Q.What does stateIn / shareIn do? What's SharingStarted.WhileSubscribed(5000)?
L5+
+

Both convert a cold flow into a hot flow, sharing emissions across collectors. stateIn produces a StateFlow; shareIn produces a SharedFlow.

SharingStarted controls upstream lifecycle:

  • Eagerly — start immediately, never stop
  • Lazily — start on first collector, never stop
  • WhileSubscribed(stopTimeout) — start on first collector, stop after timeout when last collector leaves. 5000ms is the standard for ViewModel state — survives config changes without restarting upstream.
val uiState = repository.userStream()
    .map { /* transform */ }
    .stateIn(
        scope = viewModelScope,
        started = SharingStarted.WhileSubscribed(5_000),
        initialValue = UiState.Loading
    )
Q.How do you handle exceptions in coroutines?
+

Two mechanisms:

  • try/catch around suspending calls — works inside coroutines for direct throws.
  • CoroutineExceptionHandler — installed in the scope's context, catches uncaught exceptions from launch. NOT triggered by async (those go to await()).
val handler = CoroutineExceptionHandler { _, e ->
    logCrash(e)
}
val scope = CoroutineScope(SupervisorJob() + Dispatchers.Default + handler)

scope.launch {
    try {
        api.call()
    } catch (e: IOException) {
        showError()
    }
}
Don't catch CancellationException — it's how coroutines propagate cancellation. If you must catch broadly, rethrow it.
Q.What is viewModelScope and how is it set up?
+

An extension property on ViewModel that returns a scope tied to the ViewModel's lifecycle. Coroutines launched in it are cancelled when ViewModel.onCleared() runs.

class MyViewModel : ViewModel() {
    fun loadData() {
        viewModelScope.launch {
            val data = repository.fetch()
            _state.value = UiState.Success(data)
        }
    }
}

Backed by SupervisorJob + Dispatchers.Main.immediate — child failures don't cancel siblings.

Q.Why is GlobalScope almost always wrong?
+
  • No parent — violates structured concurrency
  • Lives until process death — easy to leak
  • Can't be cancelled together with related work
  • Hard to test (can't substitute a test dispatcher)

Acceptable exceptions: truly app-scoped fire-and-forget work, like analytics flush during app shutdown. Use a custom CoroutineScope with a SupervisorJob and inject it.

Q.How do you make a callback API into a suspending function?
+
// One-shot callback
suspend fun loadUser(): User = suspendCancellableCoroutine { cont ->
    val task = api.getUser(
        onSuccess = { cont.resume(it) },
        onError = { cont.resumeWithException(it) }
    )
    cont.invokeOnCancellation { task.cancel() }
}

// Stream of callbacks → Flow
fun locationUpdates(): Flow<Location> = callbackFlow {
    val listener = LocationListener { trySend(it) }
    locationManager.requestUpdates(listener)
    awaitClose { locationManager.removeUpdates(listener) }
}
Q.What's backpressure and how does Flow handle it?
L5+
+

Backpressure = when upstream produces faster than downstream consumes. Flow handles it via suspending the producer (the default — slow consumer slows producer).

Operators to manage:

  • buffer(n) — buffer up to N items, decouple upstream/downstream
  • conflate() — keep only the latest value, drop intermediate
  • collectLatest — cancel previous collect block when new value arrives
  • sample(period) — emit at most one value per period
  • debounce(time) — emit only after quiet period
Q.runBlocking — what is it and when use it?
+

Runs a coroutine on the current thread and blocks it until completion. Bridges blocking code to suspending code.

Use for: tests, main() in CLI tools, integration with synchronous APIs.

NEVER use in Android production code — blocks the calling thread; on Main, freezes UI.

// ✅ Tests
@Test fun example() = runBlocking {
    val result = repository.fetch()
    assertEquals(expected, result)
}

// ❌ Don't do this in a ViewModel
fun loadSync() = runBlocking { repository.fetch() }
Q.withTimeout vs withTimeoutOrNull
+
// Throws TimeoutCancellationException on timeout
withTimeout(5_000) { api.slowCall() }

// Returns null on timeout (cleaner for many flows)
val result = withTimeoutOrNull(5_000) { api.slowCall() }
result?.let { use(it) } ?: showTimeout()

Both rely on cooperative cancellation — the inner work must hit a suspension point to be cancellable.

Q.Thread.sleep() vs delay() — what's the difference?
+
  • Thread.sleep(ms) — blocks the current thread. Other coroutines on the same dispatcher are stuck.
  • delay(ms) — suspends the coroutine. Frees the thread for other work. Cancellable.
// ❌ Blocks the Main dispatcher's thread
viewModelScope.launch { Thread.sleep(1000); update() }

// ✅ Suspends only this coroutine
viewModelScope.launch { delay(1000); update() }
Q.What's the difference between collect and collectLatest?
+
  • collect — processes every emission. If the collector block is slow, upstream waits (back-pressure).
  • collectLatest — when a new emission arrives, cancels the current collector block and starts over with the new value.
// Search — only care about latest input
queryFlow.collectLatest { q ->
    val results = api.search(q)  // cancelled if q changes
    show(results)
}
Q.What is yield() and when use it?
+

A suspension point that gives other coroutines on the same dispatcher a chance to run, and checks for cancellation. Useful in long CPU-bound loops.

suspend fun process(items: List<Item>) = coroutineScope {
    items.forEach { item ->
        heavyTransform(item)
        yield()  // cancellable + cooperative
    }
}
Q.What happens if an exception is thrown in async but await() is never called?
L5+Tricky
+

The exception is stored in the Deferred. It still propagates to the parent scope at the point of failure (unless the scope is a SupervisorJob) — cancelling siblings. Calling await() rethrows the same exception.

Common mistake: assuming async { ... } without await() silently absorbs exceptions. It doesn't — the parent still tears down. Use try/catch around the scope or a CoroutineExceptionHandler.
Q.How do you debounce input in a Flow?
+
queryFlow
    .debounce(300)              // wait 300ms of silence
    .filter { it.length >= 2 }  // skip too-short queries
    .distinctUntilChanged()
    .flatMapLatest { q ->        // cancel previous query
        api.search(q).catch { emit(emptyList()) }
    }
    .collect { showResults(it) }

Standard pattern for search-as-you-type. Combines debounce + distinctUntilChanged + flatMapLatest.

Q.callbackFlow vs channelFlow
L5+
+
  • callbackFlow — wraps callback-based APIs. MUST call awaitClose to register cleanup, or it throws.
  • channelFlow — general-purpose for emitting from multiple coroutines concurrently. No awaitClose requirement.
fun gpsUpdates(): Flow<Location> = callbackFlow {
    val listener = LocationListener { loc -> trySend(loc) }
    gps.register(listener)
    awaitClose { gps.unregister(listener) }  // required
}
04

Android Internals

OS-level mechanics that distinguish a senior Android engineer from a framework user.

Q.Walk through the Activity lifecycle.
🔥
+
  • onCreate — initialize, restore state from savedInstanceState
  • onStart — visible but not interactive
  • onResume — in foreground, interactive
  • onPause — losing focus (dialog over, multi-window). Keep brief — blocks next activity's resume.
  • onStop — no longer visible
  • onDestroy — being destroyed (config change OR finishing)
Q.What's the difference between onSaveInstanceState and ViewModel for state preservation?
🔥
+
onSaveInstanceStateViewModel
Config change (rotation)✅✅
System-initiated process death✅❌
User backs out of Activity❌❌
Size limitYes (~500KB Bundle)No (in-memory)

Best practice: ViewModel for transient UI state during config changes (large objects, in-flight requests); SavedStateHandle (or onSaveInstanceState) for small UI state surviving process death (current screen, scroll position, form input).

Q.What's an ANR? How do you debug one?
+

Application Not Responding — triggered when the main thread is blocked for too long:

  • Input events: 5 seconds
  • Broadcast receivers: 10 seconds (foreground), 60s (background)
  • Services: 20 seconds (foreground), 200s (background)

Debug with:

  • /data/anr/traces.txt on device — main thread stack
  • Play Console Vitals → ANRs
  • Perfetto / Systrace for timeline visualization
  • StrictMode in debug builds catches main thread I/O

Common causes: synchronous network/disk I/O, holding a lock the UI needs, deadlocks, slow content provider queries.

Q.What causes memory leaks in Android? How does LeakCanary detect them?
+

Common causes:

  • Anonymous inner classes / non-static handlers holding outer (Activity) reference
  • Singletons holding Context (should hold ApplicationContext only)
  • Long-lived listeners not unregistered
  • Static fields holding View / Context
  • Coroutines launched in GlobalScope still referencing Activity

LeakCanary: hooks into lifecycle, waits 5s after expected destruction, forces GC, walks the heap to find the still-referenced object, traces the ref chain back to a GC root, and tells you the chain.

Q.What is Binder IPC and how does it work?
L5+
+

Android's primary IPC mechanism. Allows processes to call methods on objects in other processes via a kernel driver.

  • Defined via AIDL — generates Proxy (client) and Stub (server) Java/Kotlin code
  • Synchronous by default — caller blocks until callee returns
  • Transaction buffer limit is ~1MB per process — large data must be chunked or use shared memory
  • Used by all system services (ActivityManager, WindowManager, etc.)
Q.Service vs IntentService vs JobScheduler vs WorkManager — when use what?
+
APIUse for
Foreground ServiceUser-visible ongoing task: music playback, navigation, file upload with notification
IntentServiceDeprecated. Use WorkManager or coroutines.
JobSchedulerOlder API. Direct system scheduling; WorkManager wraps it.
WorkManagerDeferrable, guaranteed-to-run background work: sync, upload, periodic. Default for background.
Coroutines + ViewModelUI-bound async (network calls tied to screen lifetime)
Q.How does the Android Process Lifecycle work?
+

Android assigns each process a priority. When memory is needed, lowest-priority processes are killed first:

  1. Foreground — has a visible Activity in onResume, foreground Service, etc. Almost never killed.
  2. Visible — partially visible (paused dialog above)
  3. Service — running a background Service
  4. Background — Activities in onStop. LRU list.
  5. Empty — no active components. Killed first.

Implications: never trust your process stays alive in background. Persist anything important. Use WorkManager for guaranteed completion.

Q.Explicit vs implicit Intent?
+
  • Explicit — targets a specific component by class name. Used for in-app navigation.
  • Implicit — declares an action; Android finds an app/component that can handle it. Used for cross-app (share, view URL).
// Explicit
startActivity(Intent(this, DetailActivity::class.java))

// Implicit
val intent = Intent(Intent.ACTION_VIEW, Uri.parse(url))
startActivity(intent)

Security note: Implicit intents can be intercepted. Don't put sensitive data (tokens, PII) in extras of an implicit intent.

Q.What is PendingIntent and what are its mutability flags?
+

A token that grants another app/system permission to fire your Intent on your behalf — used by notifications, alarms, widgets.

Required flags (since API 31):

  • FLAG_IMMUTABLE — recipient cannot modify intent extras. Use this by default.
  • FLAG_MUTABLE — needed only for things like inline notification replies that need to add data
Q.Application Context vs Activity Context — when use each?
🔥
+
Application ContextActivity Context
LifecycleApp process lifetimeActivity lifetime
Use forSingletons, DI graph, app-scoped resourcesUI, dialogs, themed inflation
Leak riskSafe to hold long-termHolding causes leaks
Themed?No (app theme only)Yes (activity theme)
Rule: if it outlives an Activity, use ApplicationContext. UI work (Toast, Dialog, layout inflater with theme) needs Activity Context.
Q.Activity launchMode — standard / singleTop / singleTask / singleInstance
+
  • standard (default) — new instance every time
  • singleTop — if already at top of task, reuse it (calls onNewIntent)
  • singleTask — only one instance per task; if exists anywhere in task, clear above and reuse
  • singleInstance — only one instance system-wide, lives in its own task

Use case examples: singleTop for notification entry points; singleTask for main launchers; singleInstance rarely needed (in-call screens, etc.).

Q.Explain Handler, Looper, MessageQueue.
+

The classic threading primitives that power Android's main thread:

  • Looper — a thread-local loop that processes Messages one at a time
  • MessageQueue — the queue Looper drains; ordered by time
  • Handler — used to post Messages or Runnables to a Looper's queue from any thread
// Post to main thread from any thread
val handler = Handler(Looper.getMainLooper())
handler.post { updateUi() }
handler.postDelayed({ check() }, 1000)

Why it matters: Coroutines, RxJava, LiveData, Compose — all eventually post to a Handler on the Main looper. Understanding this explains why "what thread am I on" matters.

Q.What is Zygote and how does it speed up app launch?
+

Zygote is a system process that starts at boot, preloads the Android framework and core libraries into memory, then sits idle. When a new app launches, Android forks Zygote — the child process inherits the preloaded memory via copy-on-write (COW).

Result: app startup skips loading common framework code from disk every time. Launch typically takes 100s of ms instead of seconds.

Q.How does RecyclerView work? Why is it better than ListView?
🔥
+

Components:

  • Adapter — produces ViewHolders from data
  • ViewHolder — wraps a row view; holds references to avoid findViewById on scroll
  • LayoutManager — arranges rows (Linear, Grid, Staggered)
  • ItemAnimator — animates insertions/removals/changes
  • RecycledViewPool — pool of recycled ViewHolders shared across RecyclerViews

vs ListView:

  • Enforced ViewHolder pattern (ListView made it optional)
  • Pluggable LayoutManager for non-linear layouts
  • Built-in item animations
  • DiffUtil + ListAdapter for efficient diffing on data updates
  • Multiple view types via getItemViewType
Q.What is DiffUtil and how does it improve RecyclerView performance?
+

DiffUtil computes the minimal set of changes between two lists (Myers diff algorithm) and dispatches granular updates (insert/remove/move/change) — instead of calling notifyDataSetChanged which redraws everything.

class UserDiff : DiffUtil.ItemCallback<User>() {
    override fun areItemsTheSame(old: User, new: User) = old.id == new.id
    override fun areContentsTheSame(old: User, new: User) = old == new
}

class UserAdapter : ListAdapter<User, UserVH>(UserDiff()) { ... }
adapter.submitList(newList)  // diff computed off main, granular updates dispatched

For large lists (1000+), run DiffUtil on a background thread (AsyncListDiffer / ListAdapter does this automatically).

Q.How does the Android Push Notification (FCM) flow work?
+
  1. App registers with FCM SDK on first launch; gets a unique FCM token
  2. App sends token to your backend and stores it associated with user
  3. Backend wants to push → sends payload to FCM API with target token(s)
  4. Google FCM servers route to device's persistent connection (Play Services keeps a socket)
  5. Play Services wakes your app and delivers to your FirebaseMessagingService.onMessageReceived()
  6. App builds a Notification and posts it

Two payload types:

  • Notification messages — system displays automatically if app backgrounded
  • Data messages — always delivered to onMessageReceived; you build the notification (more control)
Q.What is ContentProvider? When use it?
+

An Android component that exposes structured data via a CRUD URI-based API. Designed for cross-app data sharing.

Use for:

  • Sharing data with other apps (e.g., contacts, media store)
  • Letting another app access yours via URI permissions
  • Initializing libraries at app startup (App Startup library uses ContentProviders)

Don't use for: in-app data access — that's just Room/DataStore.

Q.View vs SurfaceView vs TextureView
+
  • View — standard UI element. Drawn by the UI thread via onDraw.
  • SurfaceView — has its own Surface, drawn on a separate thread. Used for video, camera preview, games. Cannot be transformed (rotated/animated) easily.
  • TextureView — like SurfaceView but rendered to a hardware texture you CAN transform. Slightly more memory/CPU.

Modern recommendation: use SurfaceView (or Compose's AndroidExternalSurface) for video; CameraX abstracts most of this.

Q.View.GONE vs View.INVISIBLE vs alpha 0
+
  • GONE — view is removed from layout. No space taken. Not measured/drawn.
  • INVISIBLE — view is laid out and takes space; just not drawn.
  • alpha = 0 — view is laid out, drawn, and accepts input — just transparent. Still clickable.
Q.What is Multidex?
+

A workaround for Android's 65,536 method reference limit per DEX file (a JVM/Dalvik historical limit). Multidex splits your app into multiple .dex files.

When you hit it: medium-to-large apps with many dependencies. Enable with multiDexEnabled true in Gradle.

Note: with API 21+ minSdk, ART loads multi-dex natively and this is mostly transparent. Pre-21 needs MultiDex.install() in Application.

Q.How are Fragments different from Activities? When prefer one?
+
  • Activity — top-level entry point. Has its own task affinity.
  • Fragment — modular UI piece hosted by an Activity. Has its own lifecycle (nested under host's).

Use Fragment for: tablet/phone layout adaptation (multi-pane), reusing UI across screens, navigation graphs (Navigation Compose still uses fragment-style destinations in non-Compose apps).

Modern Compose apps often skip Fragments entirely — single Activity, Compose Navigation, composable destinations. Simpler.

Q.What is the default constructor rule for Fragments?
+

Fragments MUST have a no-arg public constructor. The system re-creates them on configuration changes / process death via FragmentFactory, which calls the default constructor.

Pass arguments via Bundle:

class DetailFragment : Fragment() {
    companion object {
        fun newInstance(id: String) = DetailFragment().apply {
            arguments = bundleOf("id" to id)
        }
    }
}

Custom constructors won't survive recreation — your data is lost.

Q.Memory leak vs OutOfMemory — what's the difference?
+
  • Memory leak — objects no longer needed are still referenced, so GC can't collect them. Heap grows over time.
  • OutOfMemoryError — heap is full, GC can't free enough space, allocation fails. Often the consequence of a leak, but can also be caused by a single huge allocation (decoding a 4K bitmap).

Fix order: profile heap → find dominators → trace ref chain → break ownership cycle.

Q.How does Garbage Collection work in ART?
L5+
+

ART (Android Runtime, replaced Dalvik in 5.0) uses a generational concurrent GC:

  • Young generation — short-lived objects collected frequently (cheap)
  • Old generation — long-lived objects, collected less often
  • Concurrent — most GC work happens on a background thread; pauses are short (sub-millisecond on recent ART)

You can't force GC — System.gc() is a hint, no guarantee. Fix the underlying allocation pressure instead.

Q.AIDL — define and implement a bound service with IPC
+
// 1. Define: src/main/aidl/com/example/IMyService.aidl
interface IMyService {
    int add(int a, int b);
    void enqueue(String task);
}

// 2. Implement the service (runs in its own process if android:process=":remote")
class MyService : Service() {
    private val binder = object : IMyService.Stub() {
        override fun add(a: Int, b: Int) = a + b
        override fun enqueue(task: String) {
            // runs on Binder thread pool, not main thread!
            synchronized(queue) { queue.add(task) }
        }
    }
    override fun onBind(intent: Intent) = binder
}

// 3. Connect from client Activity/Fragment
private var service: IMyService? = null
private val conn = object : ServiceConnection {
    override fun onServiceConnected(name: ComponentName, binder: IBinder) {
        service = IMyService.Stub.asInterface(binder)
    }
    override fun onServiceDisconnected(name: ComponentName) { service = null }
}

override fun onStart() {
    super.onStart()
    bindService(Intent(this, MyService::class.java), conn, Context.BIND_AUTO_CREATE)
}
override fun onStop() { super.onStop(); unbindService(conn) }

// 4. Call
val result = service?.add(3, 4)  // may throw RemoteException — always catch
Threading: AIDL method calls arrive on a Binder thread pool, NOT the main thread. Any UI update must be posted to the main thread. Never hold the Binder thread with a slow operation.
Q.Messenger — simpler IPC when you don't need concurrent calls
+

A Handler-backed IPC mechanism. Simpler than AIDL; messages are serialized and processed sequentially on the handler's thread.

// Service side
class MessengerService : Service() {
    private val handler = object : Handler(Looper.getMainLooper()) {
        override fun handleMessage(msg: Message) {
            when (msg.what) {
                MSG_ADD -> {
                    val result = msg.arg1 + msg.arg2
                    msg.replyTo?.send(Message.obtain(null, MSG_RESULT, result, 0))
                }
            }
        }
    }
    private val messenger = Messenger(handler)
    override fun onBind(intent: Intent) = messenger.binder
}

// Client side
var serviceMessenger: Messenger? = null
val replyMessenger = Messenger(Handler(Looper.getMainLooper()) { msg ->
    val result = msg.arg1
    true
})
val msg = Message.obtain(null, MSG_ADD).apply {
    arg1 = 3; arg2 = 4; replyTo = replyMessenger
}
serviceMessenger?.send(msg)

Messenger vs AIDL: Messenger is simpler (no AIDL file, no stub) and sequential (no threading concerns). AIDL supports concurrent calls and is more performant for high-frequency calls.

Q.FileProvider — sharing files between apps securely
+

Directly sharing file:// URIs fails on Android 7+ (FileUriExposedException). FileProvider grants temporary content:// URI permissions.

<!-- AndroidManifest.xml -->
<provider
    android:name="androidx.core.content.FileProvider"
    android:authorities="${applicationId}.fileprovider"
    android:grantUriPermissions="true"
    android:exported="false">
    <meta-data
        android:name="android.support.FILE_PROVIDER_PATHS"
        android:resource="@xml/file_paths" />
</provider>
<!-- res/xml/file_paths.xml -->
<paths>
    <cache-path name="shared_images" path="shared/" />
    <files-path name="downloads" path="downloads/" />
</paths>
// Share a file
val file = File(cacheDir, "shared/photo.jpg")
val uri = FileProvider.getUriForFile(this, "${packageName}.fileprovider", file)
val intent = Intent(Intent.ACTION_SEND).apply {
    type = "image/jpeg"
    putExtra(Intent.EXTRA_STREAM, uri)
    addFlags(Intent.FLAG_GRANT_READ_URI_PERMISSION)  // temporary grant
}
startActivity(Intent.createChooser(intent, "Share"))
Q.Package visibility (Android 11+) — querying installed apps
+

Android 11 (API 30) restricted apps from seeing all installed apps. You must declare which packages you need to see in the manifest.

<!-- Need to open this specific app -->
<queries>
    <package android:name="com.example.otherhapp" />
    <intent>
        <action android:name="android.intent.action.SEND" />
        <data android:mimeType="image/jpeg" />
    </intent>
    <!-- Or for a specific category of apps -->
    <intent><action android:name="android.media.browse.MediaBrowserService" /></intent>
</queries>
// Check if an app can handle an intent
val intent = Intent(Intent.ACTION_VIEW, Uri.parse("http://example.com"))
val canHandle = intent.resolveActivity(packageManager) != null
// On API 30+ without <queries>, resolveActivity returns null even if app exists
Deep links: always check resolveActivity before startActivity. Custom scheme URIs (myapp://) → ACTION_VIEW with your URI in <queries>.
05

Architecture & Patterns

Pattern fluency is table stakes at L5. Interviewers probe how you'd structure a large codebase, not just toy apps.

Q.Explain MVVM. How is it different from MVI?
🔥
+

MVVM — Model (data), View (UI), ViewModel (state + business logic). View observes ViewModel state. Multiple state variables are common.

MVI — Model-View-Intent. State is a single immutable object. Events (Intents) are reduced into new state. Pure unidirectional dataflow.

MVVMMVI
StateMultiple flows / LiveDataSingle state object
EventsMethod calls on VMReduced via reducer/intent
VerbosityLowerHigher (boilerplate)
Best forMost screensComplex state, replay/undo, debugging

In practice: "MVI-flavored MVVM" is dominant — single UiState in StateFlow, events handled by ViewModel methods. Best of both.

Q.What is the Repository pattern and why use it?
🔥
+

An abstraction over data sources. ViewModels depend on a Repository interface, not on Retrofit, Room, or DataStore directly. Repository decides what to fetch from cache vs network.

class UserRepository(
    private val api: UserApi,
    private val db: UserDao,
    private val ioDispatcher: CoroutineDispatcher
) {
    fun userStream(id: String): Flow<User> = db.observeUser(id)
        .onStart { refresh(id) }

    private suspend fun refresh(id: String) = withContext(ioDispatcher) {
        try {
            val fresh = api.getUser(id)
            db.upsert(fresh)
        } catch (e: IOException) {
            // stale data is fine; just don't refresh
        }
    }
}

Benefits: single source of truth (DB), offline-first, testable (swap fakes), caching strategy lives in one place.

Q.What is single source of truth (SSOT)?
+

One authoritative store for any piece of data. UI observes it; writes go through it.

Typical setup: Room database is SSOT. Network fetches write into Room. UI observes Room flows. No path where UI reads network response directly bypassing the cache.

Benefits: offline works automatically, consistency across screens, simpler invalidation.

Q.How does Hilt / Dagger work at a high level?
+

Compile-time dependency injection. You declare bindings (how to create types) and inject them into classes. Hilt generates code that wires everything; no reflection at runtime.

  • Scopes — control instance lifetime: @Singleton, @ActivityRetainedScoped, @ViewModelScoped
  • Components — Hilt-managed containers that match Android lifecycles
  • Entry points — Activities/Fragments/ViewModels annotated with @AndroidEntryPoint / @HiltViewModel
@Module @InstallIn(SingletonComponent::class)
object NetworkModule {
    @Provides @Singleton
    fun provideRetrofit(): Retrofit = Retrofit.Builder()...build()
}

@HiltViewModel
class UserViewModel @Inject constructor(
    private val repository: UserRepository
) : ViewModel()
Q.How would you structure a multi-module Android project?
L5+
+

Common pattern: by feature + by layer.

:app                         (entry point, DI graph wiring)
:core
    :core:ui                 (Compose theme, common UI components)
    :core:data               (Repository contracts, models)
    :core:network            (Retrofit, OkHttp, interceptors)
    :core:database           (Room + DAOs)
    :core:testing            (test fixtures, fakes)
:feature
    :feature:login           (login Compose screen + VM)
    :feature:home
    :feature:profile

Rules:

  • Features don't depend on each other
  • Features depend on core modules via interfaces
  • :app wires everything via DI graph

Benefits: parallel builds, build cache reuse, faster Gradle, enforces architectural boundaries.

Q.What is Clean Architecture? Use cases / domain layer?
+

Layered separation: Presentation (ViewModel) → Domain (UseCases) → Data (Repository). Domain has no Android dependencies. UseCases are single-purpose classes (e.g., GetUserUseCase).

Pragmatic note: Clean Architecture works at scale but adds boilerplate. For small/medium apps, ViewModels can call Repositories directly. Add UseCases when logic spans multiple repos or is reused.
Q.How do you handle one-shot events (errors, navigation) vs state in ViewModel?
L5+
+

State = reproducible by re-collecting (StateFlow). Events = should be consumed exactly once (SharedFlow or channels).

private val _events = MutableSharedFlow<UiEvent>(extraBufferCapacity = 1)
val events: SharedFlow<UiEvent> = _events.asSharedFlow()

fun onSave() {
    viewModelScope.launch {
        if (saveResult().isSuccess) _events.emit(UiEvent.NavigateBack)
        else _events.emit(UiEvent.ShowError("Save failed"))
    }
}

Alternative: include the event in state and clear it after handling — works but error-prone. SharedFlow with replay 0 is cleaner.

Q.What is VIPER architecture?
+

View - Interactor - Presenter - Entity - Router. Originally iOS-flavored. Stricter than MVP/MVVM: business logic in Interactor, navigation in Router, UI in View, glue in Presenter.

  • View — passive, dumb. Tells presenter about events.
  • Presenter — handles UI logic, asks Interactor for data.
  • Interactor — business logic. Pure.
  • Entity — data models.
  • Router — navigation.

In practice on Android: rarely used in pure form; concepts borrowed into MVVM + UseCases setups.

Q.Modularization — by-feature vs by-layer strategy
🔥
+
By LayerBy FeatureHybrid (recommended)
Structure:data, :domain, :ui:feature-home, :feature-profileCore layers + feature modules
Dependency graphFlatter, fewer modulesMore modules, cleaner boundariesBest of both
Parallel buildsModerateMaximumGood
Team boundariesCross-team per featureTeam owns a moduleTeam owns a feature module
Over-engineering riskLowHigh (for small apps)Start small, grow into it

Recommended structure (Now In Android / NOWINANDROID pattern):

:app                         // entry point, DI wiring only
:core:ui                     // Compose theme, shared components
:core:data                   // Repository interfaces + implementations
:core:network                // Retrofit, OkHttp
:core:database               // Room
:core:testing                // Fakes, test utilities
:feature:home                // HomeScreen composable + ViewModel
:feature:profile
:feature:settings

Rules: features depend on core, not on each other. :app depends on all features. Core modules never depend up on features.

Build speed win: with 5+ modules, Gradle can build non-dependent modules in parallel. :feature:home and :feature:profile build simultaneously.
Q.Type-safe Navigation Compose (2.8+)
🔥
+

Pre-2.8: routes were stringly-typed strings — typos only caught at runtime. Type-safe routes use @Serializable Kotlin objects/data classes as destinations.

// Define destinations
@Serializable object Home
@Serializable data class Detail(val id: String, val showHeader: Boolean = true)
@Serializable object Settings

// NavHost
val navController = rememberNavController()
NavHost(navController, startDestination = Home) {
    composable<Home> { HomeScreen(onItem = { id -> navController.navigate(Detail(id)) }) }
    composable<Detail> { backStackEntry ->
        val detail: Detail = backStackEntry.toRoute()
        DetailScreen(id = detail.id)
    }
    composable<Settings> { SettingsScreen() }
}

// Navigate — fully type-safe, compile error if args wrong
navController.navigate(Detail(id = "123", showHeader = false))

Scoped ViewModels to NavGraph:

// ViewModel shared across all destinations in a nested graph
@Serializable object CheckoutGraph
navigation<CheckoutGraph>(startDestination = CartDestination) {
    composable<CartDestination> {
        val sharedVm: CheckoutViewModel = it.sharedViewModel(navController)
    }
    composable<PaymentDestination> {
        val sharedVm: CheckoutViewModel = it.sharedViewModel(navController)
    }
}
Q.MVI architecture — Intent → Reducer → State
+

Strict unidirectional data flow. All state changes go through a single reducer function.

// State — single immutable snapshot
data class CounterState(val count: Int = 0, val loading: Boolean = false)

// Intent — all possible user actions
sealed class CounterIntent {
    data object Increment : CounterIntent()
    data object Decrement : CounterIntent()
    data class Set(val value: Int) : CounterIntent()
}

// ViewModel — reducer maps Intent → new State
class CounterVm : ViewModel() {
    private val _state = MutableStateFlow(CounterState())
    val state = _state.asStateFlow()

    fun process(intent: CounterIntent) {
        _state.update { current ->
            when (intent) {
                CounterIntent.Increment -> current.copy(count = current.count + 1)
                CounterIntent.Decrement -> current.copy(count = current.count - 1)
                is CounterIntent.Set -> current.copy(count = intent.value)
            }
        }
    }
}

// UI
val state by vm.state.collectAsStateWithLifecycle()
Button(onClick = { vm.process(CounterIntent.Increment) }) { Text("${state.count}") }

MVI vs MVVM: MVI enforces single state object and explicit intent types — easier to log, replay, time-travel debug. MVVM is simpler for straightforward screens. Use MVI where state complexity or debugging needs justify the boilerplate.

Q.Process death recovery — SavedStateHandle and what survives
🔥
+

Android can kill your app's process in the background (low memory). When user returns, the system recreates the Activity — but your ViewModel and in-memory state is gone. Only SavedStateHandle (backed by the Bundle) survives.

@HiltViewModel
class SearchViewModel @Inject constructor(
    private val savedState: SavedStateHandle,
    private val repo: SearchRepository
) : ViewModel() {

    // Survives process death (stored in Bundle, size limit ~1MB total)
    val query: StateFlow<String> = savedState.getStateFlow("query", "")

    fun setQuery(q: String) {
        savedState["query"] = q  // automatic persistence
        search(q)
    }

    // This does NOT survive process death — loaded lazily on demand
    private val _results = MutableStateFlow<List<Result>>(emptyList())
    val results = _results.asStateFlow()
}
MechanismConfig changeProcess deathUser back press
ViewModel in-memory✅❌❌
SavedStateHandle✅✅❌
Room / DataStore✅✅✅
Common bug: storing large objects in SavedStateHandle (lists of results, bitmaps). Bundle limit is ~500KB-1MB total. Store only the query/filter/key needed to re-fetch, not the results themselves.
Q.Hilt component hierarchy and scope lifecycle
🔥
+

Hilt generates a component for each Android lifecycle. The component provides dependencies and lives for the same duration as its corresponding Android class.

ComponentScope annotationLifetime
SingletonComponent@SingletonApp process lifetime
ActivityRetainedComponent@ActivityRetainedScopedSurvives config change; dies with Activity finish
ViewModelComponent@ViewModelScopedViewModel lifetime
ActivityComponent@ActivityScopedActivity lifetime (recreated on config change)
FragmentComponent@FragmentScopedFragment lifetime
ViewComponent@ViewScopedView lifetime
ServiceComponent@ServiceScopedService lifetime
// Scoped to ViewModel — one instance per ViewModel
@ViewModelScoped
class CartRepository @Inject constructor(private val api: Api)

@HiltViewModel
class CartViewModel @Inject constructor(
    private val repo: CartRepository  // same instance for this ViewModel
) : ViewModel()

// vs @Singleton — same instance across the entire app
@Singleton
class UserRepository @Inject constructor(private val api: Api)
Q.Hilt multibindings — @IntoSet and @IntoMap
L5+
+

Multibindings let you contribute to a collection of dependencies — useful for plugin architectures, analytics providers, screen factories.

// Define the interface
interface AnalyticsTracker { fun track(event: String) }

// Each module contributes one implementation
@Module @InstallIn(SingletonComponent::class)
object FirebaseModule {
    @Provides @IntoSet
    fun provideFirebase(): AnalyticsTracker = FirebaseTracker()
}

@Module @InstallIn(SingletonComponent::class)
object MixpanelModule {
    @Provides @IntoSet
    fun provideMixpanel(): AnalyticsTracker = MixpanelTracker()
}

// Inject the full set
class Analytics @Inject constructor(
    private val trackers: Set<@JvmSuppressWildcards AnalyticsTracker>
) {
    fun track(event: String) = trackers.forEach { it.track(event) }
}

// @IntoMap — key-based plugin registry
@MapKey annotation class ScreenKey(val value: KClass<*>)

@Provides @IntoMap
@ScreenKey(HomeScreen::class)
fun provideHomeFactory(): ScreenFactory = HomeScreenFactory()
Q.ADR: When is "Over-engineering" actually a Senior design choice?
+

Seniors are often accused of over-engineering, but it's often "Proactive Complexity" to handle anticipated scale.

  • The "Toy" Approach (YAGNI): Keep it simple. Repositories call Retrofit directly. No UseCases. No Modules. Best for: Startups, MVPs, proof-of-concepts.
  • The "Senior" Approach (Scale-aware): Introduce UseCases and Modularization early. Why? Because refactoring a 100k line monolithic `ViewModel` into a clean architecture is 10x more expensive than doing it from the start.

The Interview Answer: "I choose architecture based on the Expected Lifetime and Team Size. If we are a team of 10+ devs working on a mission-critical feature, the 'boilerplate' cost of Clean Architecture is actually a productivity multiplier because it prevents merge conflicts and side effects."

Q.Scenario: Massive list performance — RecyclerView vs. LazyColumn?
+

Both are good, but they fail differently at scale.

  • LazyColumn: Simpler, but recomposition can be expensive if the state object isn't properly optimized (use derivedStateOf, key, and @Immutable).
  • RecyclerView: Still superior for memory-constrained devices or extremely complex item layouts because it uses a more mature ViewPool and manual recycling logic.

Senior Trade-off: If the list has 10,000 items, the problem isn't the UI component, it's the Data Layer. I would prioritize Paging 3 with a RemoteMediator (Room cache) to ensure we never hold more than 50 items in memory at once.

Q.Assisted injection — inject runtime parameters with Hilt
L5+
+

Assisted injection is for when some constructor params come from Hilt (dependencies) and some come from the caller at runtime (e.g., an ID from navigation args).

class ProductViewModel @AssistedInject constructor(
    private val repo: ProductRepository,  // provided by Hilt
    @Assisted val productId: String        // provided by caller at runtime
) : ViewModel() {

    @AssistedFactory
    interface Factory {
        fun create(productId: String): ProductViewModel
    }
}

// In Fragment / Composable — pass runtime param
@AndroidEntryPoint
class ProductFragment : Fragment() {
    private val productId: String by args()

    private val viewModel: ProductViewModel by viewModels {
        ProductViewModel.Factory::class  // Hilt knows how to make the factory
    }
    // Or with Navigation Compose:
}

@Composable
fun ProductScreen(productId: String, factory: ProductViewModel.Factory) {
    val vm: ProductViewModel = viewModel {
        factory.create(productId)
    }
}
Q.Testing with Hilt — @HiltAndroidTest and test doubles
+
// Replace bindings in tests using @BindValue or test modules
@HiltAndroidTest
@RunWith(AndroidJUnit4::class)
class UserFlowTest {
    @get:Rule(order = 0) val hiltRule = HiltAndroidRule(this)
    @get:Rule(order = 1) val composeRule = createAndroidComposeRule<HiltTestActivity>()

    // Replace real impl with a fake — no need for a test module
    @BindValue @JvmField
    val userRepo: UserRepository = FakeUserRepository()

    @Before fun setUp() = hiltRule.inject()

    @Test fun displaysUser() {
        composeRule.setContent { UserScreen() }
        composeRule.onNodeWithText("Rutul Thakkar").assertIsDisplayed()
    }
}

// Custom test module — replace whole module
@Module @TestInstallIn(components = [SingletonComponent::class],
    replaces = [NetworkModule::class])
object FakeNetworkModule {
    @Provides @Singleton
    fun provideApi(): Api = FakeApi()
}
HiltTestActivity: provided by hilt-android-testing artifact. Required for Compose tests with Hilt — regular Activity won't work because it needs @AndroidEntryPoint.
06

Performance & Optimization

High-leverage senior topic. Concrete tools and numbers matter — interviewers want to see you've profiled real apps.

Q.What causes app startup to be slow and how do you fix it?
🔥
+

Types of startup: Cold (process not running), Warm (process running, no Activity), Hot (Activity in memory).

Common cold-start culprits:

  • Heavy work in Application.onCreate() — DI graph, Firebase init, logging
  • Heavy work in first Activity's onCreate()
  • Synchronous I/O on the main thread
  • Many ContentProvider initializations

Fixes:

  • App Startup Library — lazy/parallel ContentProvider replacement
  • Baseline Profiles — pre-compile hot code paths; 20-40% startup improvement typical
  • Defer non-critical init to after first frame
  • Measure with Macrobenchmark — automated, repeatable timing
  • Splash screen API (API 31+) for perceived performance
Q.What's jank and how do you debug it?
+

Jank = dropped frames. At 60Hz, each frame must complete in <16.67ms; at 120Hz, <8.33ms. Miss it and you see stutter.

Tools:

  • Perfetto / Android Studio Profiler — see frame timing, main thread blocks, GC pauses
  • GPU rendering bars in Developer Options
  • Macrobenchmark with FrameTimingMetric for automated regression detection

Common fixes: move heavy work off main thread, use stable Compose params, baseline profiles, avoid overdraw, lazy image loading.

Q.What are Baseline Profiles?
L5+
+

A list of classes/methods that get pre-compiled (AOT) on install, instead of JIT-compiled the first time they run. Improves first-launch and first-use of features.

How to make one:

  • Write a Macrobenchmark test that exercises your critical paths (cold start + scroll)
  • BaselineProfileRule generates a baseline-prof.txt file
  • Ship the file as an asset — Play Store pre-compiles methods listed

Typical wins: 20-40% startup improvement, 30-50% smoother scrolling on cold sessions.

Q.How does R8 / ProGuard help, and what can go wrong?
+

R8 is Google's replacement for ProGuard. Does:

  • Shrinking — remove unused code (tree-shaking)
  • Obfuscation — rename classes/methods to short names
  • Optimization — inline, dead code elim, constant propagation
  • Desugaring — backport newer Java APIs to older Android versions

Pitfalls: reflection-based code (Gson, Retrofit + raw types) breaks if classes are obfuscated. Add -keep rules. Always test the release variant.

Q.How do you optimize image loading?
+
  • Use Coil or Glide — handle decoding, caching, lifecycle
  • Right-size on server — request thumbnails at display size, not 4K originals
  • WebP or AVIF instead of PNG/JPEG
  • Memory + disk cache tuned to working set
  • Sampling for very large bitmaps — BitmapFactory.Options.inSampleSize
  • Hardware bitmaps for unmodified display (Coil/Glide do this automatically)
Q.Cold / warm / hot startup — measure and improve each
🔥
+
TypeStateTypical target
ColdProcess not running. Most expensive. Forks Zygote, loads class loader, runs Application + first Activity.<500ms to first frame
WarmProcess alive but Activity not. Skip Application creation; rebuild Activity.<200ms
HotActivity in memory. Just bring to foreground.<100ms

Measurement:

  • Macrobenchmark with StartupTimingMetric — repeatable, CI-friendly
  • Logcat — search for Displayed tag after launch
  • Firebase Performance — production telemetry, percentiles
  • Play Console Vitals — real user startup metrics

Wins (in priority order): Baseline Profiles → defer non-critical init → lazy DI graphs → smaller Application.onCreate() → fewer ContentProviders → smaller layout tree on first screen.

Q.How do you profile and fix memory issues?
🔥
+
  1. Reproduce — navigate the suspected leak flow, then back out
  2. Force GC in Android Studio Memory Profiler
  3. Capture a heap dump (HPROF)
  4. Find dominator — what's holding the most memory
  5. Trace ref chain from the object back to a GC root
  6. Break ownership — usually a static field, listener, or coroutine scope

Tools:

  • LeakCanary — automatic leak detection in debug
  • Android Studio Memory Profiler — live allocation tracking, heap snapshots
  • StrictMode — fail-fast on main-thread I/O and similar
  • Perfetto — system-wide trace with memory views
Q.What are the network performance levers for a mobile app?
+
  • HTTP/2 (or HTTP/3) — multiplexed streams over one connection. OkHttp uses HTTP/2 automatically when server supports it.
  • Connection pooling — OkHttp reuses connections; keep OkHttpClient singleton
  • Compression — gzip / Brotli on response bodies
  • Right-size responses — server-side: thumbnails, sparse fields, GraphQL field selection
  • Batch / coalesce requests — one trip vs ten
  • Prefetch & warm caches on idle
  • Edge caching / CDN for static + cacheable responses
  • TLS session reuse — saves the handshake on repeat requests

Measure: Network Profiler in Android Studio, Charles/Proxyman for inspection, Firebase Performance for real-user latency percentiles.

Q.What kills battery on Android? How do you audit?
L5+
+

Biggest drains:

  • Frequent wake locks (preventing CPU/screen sleep)
  • High-frequency GPS / location requests
  • Persistent network (WebSocket keep-alives, unnecessary polling)
  • Wake-up alarms (setExact, setExactAndAllowWhileIdle)
  • JobScheduler / WorkManager with too-frequent constraints
  • Camera, Bluetooth scans while backgrounded

Audit tools: Battery Historian (visualizes dumpsys batterystats), Android Studio Energy Profiler, Doze/App Standby buckets behavior in Developer Options.

Defense: Doze and App Standby Buckets — Android restricts background work for less-active apps. Embrace, don't fight.

Q.How do you track app size and prevent regression?
+
  • APK Analyzer in Android Studio — drill into method count, resource size, native libs
  • Play Console — historical app size graph + download size breakdown
  • CI gates — track AAB size per PR; fail the build if it grows beyond a threshold without justification
  • Bundletool — generate device-specific APKs locally to verify size

When size creeps up: 90% of the time it's a new dep or unshrunk resource. Audit with ./gradlew :app:dependencies and shrinkResources true.

Q.What is overdraw and how do you fix it?
+

Overdraw = the GPU drawing the same pixel multiple times in one frame (e.g., a background under another background). Adds rendering cost.

Inspect: Developer Options → "Debug GPU overdraw" → screen tints show overdraw count (blue: 1x, green: 2x, red: 4x+).

Fixes: remove unnecessary backgrounds, use android:windowBackground="@null" when you cover the whole window, prefer ConstraintLayout over nested layouts that each set a background.

07

Testing

Senior interviews always touch testability and how to test async/Compose code.

Q.Unit vs Instrumented test — when use each?
+
  • Unit tests — src/test. Run on JVM. Fast. Use for pure logic, ViewModels, Repositories with fakes.
  • Instrumented tests — src/androidTest. Run on a device/emulator. Slow. Use for Compose UI, real DB (Room), Activities, integration tests.
  • Robolectric — JVM-based, simulates Android framework. Faster than instrumented, less accurate.
Q.How do you test code that uses coroutines?
+
@OptIn(ExperimentalCoroutinesApi::class)
class UserVmTest {
    private val testDispatcher = StandardTestDispatcher()

    @Before fun setup() {
        Dispatchers.setMain(testDispatcher)
    }
    @After fun teardown() {
        Dispatchers.resetMain()
    }

    @Test fun loads_user() = runTest {
        val vm = UserViewModel(fakeRepo)
        vm.load("123")
        advanceUntilIdle()
        assertEquals(User("123"), vm.uiState.value)
    }
}

Key APIs: runTest (skips delays virtually), StandardTestDispatcher (manual scheduling), UnconfinedTestDispatcher (auto-runs eagerly), advanceUntilIdle(), advanceTimeBy().

Q.How do you test a Flow?
+
// Use Turbine library
@Test fun emits_correct_sequence() = runTest {
    repo.userStream("1").test {
        assertEquals(User.Loading, awaitItem())
        assertEquals(User("1", "Rutul"), awaitItem())
        awaitComplete()
    }
}

Turbine handles timing and back-pressure for you, way cleaner than manual collect + assertions.

Q.MockK vs Mockito for Kotlin?
+

MockK wins for Kotlin:

  • Supports mocking final classes/methods (Kotlin classes are final by default)
  • Coroutine support via coEvery / coVerify
  • Kotlin DSL feels natural
  • Object/companion mocking

Mockito works but needs mockito-kotlin + mock-maker-inline for final classes. Pick MockK for new code.

Q.Test pyramid — what's the right ratio?
+

Rough split: 70% unit / 20% integration / 10% UI/end-to-end.

  • Unit — fast, run on every save, catches logic bugs early
  • Integration — Repository + Room + fake API, run on CI
  • UI/E2E — Compose tests + Espresso, slow but catch real wiring issues

Anti-pattern: heavy on UI tests, light on units. Slow CI, brittle tests, hard to debug.

Q.Test doubles taxonomy — mock vs stub vs fake vs spy vs dummy
🔥
+
TypeWhat it doesUse for
DummyPassed but never usedFilling required constructor params
StubReturns predefined valuesControlling return values in tests
FakeWorking implementation, simplifiedIn-memory DB, fake API — realistic but no infra
MockPre-programmed, verifies interactionsVerify a method was called with specific args
SpyWraps real object, records callsVerify calls on real implementations
// Stub — control return value
val stub = mockk<UserRepo>()
every { stub.getUser("123") } returns User("123", "Rutul")

// Mock — verify interaction
val mock = mockk<Analytics>(relaxed = true)
viewModel.onButtonClick()
verify(exactly = 1) { mock.track("button_clicked") }

// Fake — real behaviour, no infrastructure
class FakeUserRepo : UserRepository {
    private val users = mutableMapOf<String, User>()
    override suspend fun getUser(id: String) = users[id] ?: throw NotFoundException()
    override suspend fun saveUser(user: User) { users[user.id] = user }
}
// Use FakeUserRepo in all tests — fast, deterministic, no DB
Prefer fakes over mocks for repositories and data sources. Mocks couple tests to implementation details (which methods are called); fakes test behaviour (what data comes out).
Q.Screenshot testing — Paparazzi and Roborazzi
+

Screenshot tests render your Composables to a bitmap and compare against a golden image. Catch visual regressions without running on a device.

Paparazzi (Square) — JVM-based, no device needed:

@RunWith(JUnit4::class)
class UserCardTest {
    @get:Rule val paparazzi = Paparazzi(
        deviceConfig = DeviceConfig.PIXEL_5,
        theme = "android:Theme.Material.Light.NoActionBar"
    )

    @Test fun userCard_default() {
        paparazzi.snapshot {
            MaterialTheme { UserCard(user = User("1", "Rutul")) }
        }
    }

    @Test fun userCard_darkMode() {
        paparazzi.snapshot {
            MaterialTheme(colorScheme = darkColorScheme()) {
                UserCard(user = User("1", "Rutul"))
            }
        }
    }
}

// Run: ./gradlew recordPaparazziDebug     ← generate goldens
//      ./gradlew verifyPaparazziDebug     ← CI comparison

Roborazzi (on top of Robolectric) — more realistic rendering:

@Config(sdk = [33]) @RunWith(RobolectricTestRunner::class)
class UserCardRoboTest {
    @get:Rule val rule = RobolectricDeviceQualifiers.Pixel5
    @Test fun snapshot() {
        composeRule.setContent { UserCard(User("1","Rutul")) }
        composeRule.onRoot().captureRoboImage("goldens/user_card.png")
    }
}
08

Mobile System Design

The make-or-break round at L5. Each case below is a quick template — clarify scope, model data, draw architecture, pick 1-2 areas to deep-dive.

Q.Universal framework — how do you structure ANY mobile system design answer?
🔥
+
  1. Clarify scope (3-5 min) — Client only, or also API/backend? What's the platform? Online/offline?
  2. Functional reqs — bullet list of capabilities. Push back on scope.
  3. Non-functional — perf, offline, battery, accessibility, scale
  4. Data model — entities, relations, what's cached locally
  5. API contract — REST/GraphQL/WebSocket; pagination strategy
  6. High-level architecture — UI layer → ViewModel → Repository → Data sources. Draw it.
  7. Deep dive (pick 1-2) — caching, sync, real-time, perf
  8. Trade-offs & what you'd do next — show seniority
Q.Design a Twitter / Instagram feed
🔥
+

1. Clarify scope

  • Chronological feed or ranked / algorithmic?
  • Image-only, video, or mixed media (Stories, Reels)?
  • Read-only or also create posts?
  • Authenticated or anonymous?
  • iOS too, or Android only? (Affects API design and reuse.)
  • Scale: ~100M MAU. Pre-fetch budget on cellular?

2. Functional requirements

  • Infinite scroll with pagination
  • Pull-to-refresh
  • Like / comment / share interactions
  • Image and short-video playback (auto-play in viewport)
  • Offline visibility of recently-viewed posts
  • Optimistic UI for likes / comments

3. Non-functional requirements

  • 60fps scroll on mid-tier device
  • <2s cold start to first post visible
  • Tolerate flaky network — degrade gracefully
  • Cellular data conscious — image right-sizing on server
  • Battery: no foreground video when offscreen

4. Data model (client-side Room)

@Entity
data class FeedPost(
    @PrimaryKey val id: String,
    val authorId: String,
    val createdAt: Long,
    val text: String?,
    val mediaJson: String,        // JSON-serialized list of MediaItem
    val likeCount: Int,
    val commentCount: Int,
    val likedByMe: Boolean,
    val cachedAt: Long
)

@Entity
data class FeedRemoteKey(
    @PrimaryKey val postId: String,
    val prevCursor: String?,
    val nextCursor: String?
)

5. API contract

GET /feed?cursor=<opaque>&limit=20
{
  "posts": [ ... ],
  "next_cursor": "abc123",   // opaque, server-defined
  "has_more": true
}

POST /posts/{id}/likes        // idempotent toggle
GET /posts/{id}/comments?cursor=...
Why cursor pagination, not offset: with offset, new inserts shift indices and you'd see duplicates or skips. Cursor (typically server-issued opaque token over (createdAt, id)) is stable.

6. Architecture (Paging 3 + Room SSOT)

  • UI: LazyColumn with stable key = { it.id }, contentType by media type for view recycling, key composable per row
  • Paging 3: Pager(PagingConfig) + RemoteMediator bridging network ↔ Room
  • Room: single source of truth — UI never reads from network directly
  • RemoteMediator handles APPEND / PREPEND / REFRESH; persists posts + RemoteKey rows for cursor
  • Coil for image loading with memory + disk cache; preload next-N as user scrolls
  • ExoPlayer pool for inline video — release players when offscreen
  • WorkManager for background refresh + retry of failed posts

7. Deep dive — Paging flow

  1. UI requests page → PagingSource reads from Room
  2. Hits "end of cached data" boundary → RemoteMediator.load(LoadType.APPEND) fires
  3. Mediator reads FeedRemoteKey.nextCursor for last post
  4. Fetches network: GET /feed?cursor=...
