  5. Inserts new posts + new RemoteKey into Room in a transaction
  6. Room flow re-emits — Paging picks up new data automatically

8. Deep dive — Optimistic likes

fun toggleLike(postId: String) {
    viewModelScope.launch {
        // 1. Optimistic Room update
        dao.toggleLike(postId)
        try {
            api.like(postId)               // 2. Network
        } catch (e: Exception) {
            dao.toggleLike(postId)         // 3. Rollback
            _events.emit(UiEvent.Error)
        }
    }
}

9. Edge cases

  • Duplicate posts on refresh — primary key on id + Upsert dedupes
  • Stale items — cap cache age (e.g., 1 hour); evict in onAppOpen
  • Memory pressure — Paging's maxSize evicts off-screen pages
  • Token expiry mid-scroll — auth interceptor refreshes silently and retries
  • Network → cache mismatch — server timestamp drift; use server clock for ordering

10. Trade-offs

  • Chronological vs ranked: chronological is simpler client-side; ranked requires server to send rankScore + client to respect it (don't re-sort)
  • Cache size: larger cache = better offline UX but more disk. Default ~250MB for images; ~50MB for post data.
  • Real-time updates: can layer WebSocket "new posts" indicator → user pulls to refresh. Avoids prepending while user scrolls.
Q.Design a chat / messaging app
🔥
+

1. Clarify scope

  • 1-1 only or groups (and up to what size)?
  • Read receipts & typing indicators?
  • Media: images / video / files? Voice messages?
  • End-to-end encryption required?
  • Multi-device or single device?
  • Edit / delete / reply / reactions?
  • Search history? How far back?

2. Functional requirements

  • Send / receive messages in real time
  • Persist conversation history offline
  • Show delivery + read status
  • Background notifications when app not open
  • Resilient to network drops — outbox queue

3. Non-functional requirements

  • Message latency <500ms when both online
  • Survive process death without losing unsent messages
  • Battery: connection only when foreground; FCM when backgrounded
  • Consistent ordering across devices

4. Data model

@Entity(primaryKeys = ["clientId"])
data class Message(
    val clientId: String,         // UUID generated on send
    val serverId: String?,        // assigned by server on ack
    val conversationId: String,
    val senderId: String,
    val body: String,
    val createdAt: Long,          // server time if synced, client time if pending
    val status: Status,           // SENDING / SENT / DELIVERED / READ / FAILED
    val replyToId: String? = null
)

@Entity
data class Conversation(
    @PrimaryKey val id: String,
    val title: String,
    val lastMessageId: String?,
    val unreadCount: Int,
    val updatedAt: Long
)

@Entity
data class Outbox(
    @PrimaryKey val clientId: String,
    val payloadJson: String,
    val attempts: Int,
    val nextRetryAt: Long
)

5. API contract

WS: wss://chat.example.com/v1?token=...
  → outbound: { "type": "send", "clientId": "uuid", "convId": "...", "body": "..." }
  ← inbound:  { "type": "ack", "clientId": "uuid", "serverId": "...", "ts": 1700... }
  ← inbound:  { "type": "msg", "serverId": "...", "convId": "...", ... }
  ← inbound:  { "type": "read", "convId": "...", "byUserId": "...", "upTo": "serverId" }

REST fallback:
  POST /conversations/{id}/messages   (idempotent via clientId)
  GET  /conversations/{id}/messages?since=<serverId>
  POST /conversations/{id}/read       { "upTo": "serverId" }

6. Architecture

  • WebSocket connection manager (singleton) — reconnects with exponential backoff, joins user-scoped channels
  • Room as SSOT — UI observes message flows per conversation
  • Outbox table — local writes go here first, drained by sync worker
  • WorkManager — schedules retries; survives process death
  • FCM — wakes app on new message when backgrounded; data payload triggers fetch
  • Foreground Service — keep WebSocket alive while user is in chat (optional, helps active call/live chat use cases)

7. Deep dive — Sending a message

  1. User taps send
  2. Generate clientId = UUID() — client-side identity
  3. Insert row in Message table with status SENDING and timestamp = client time
  4. Insert row in Outbox
  5. UI re-renders immediately (optimistic)
  6. WebSocket connected? Send. Else WorkManager picks up on connectivity
  7. Server ack arrives → update Message row: serverId, server time, status SENT; delete Outbox row
  8. Server-side fanout → other devices receive via their WebSocket / FCM
  9. Read receipt: when message visible > 500ms in viewport, post read event

8. Deep dive — Receiving a message

  • Foreground (app open): WebSocket pushes → write Room → UI Flow re-emits
  • Background: FCM data payload → FirebaseMessagingService.onMessageReceived → WorkManager job pulls missed messages via GET /messages?since=lastServerId → writes to Room → shows notification
  • Killed: FCM wakes process; same flow

9. Edge cases

  • Duplicate delivery — server ack arrives twice. UPSERT on clientId + serverId dedupes.
  • Out-of-order WebSocket frames — sort UI by server timestamp, NOT arrival order
  • Reconnect after long offline — sync via GET /messages?since=<lastServerId>; cap to e.g. 1000 messages, prompt "load older" past that
  • Clock skew between devices — use server time; never trust client clock for ordering
  • Failed send after many retries — mark FAILED in UI, let user manually retry or delete
  • Token expiry — WebSocket reconnect uses refreshed token

10. Trade-offs

  • WebSocket vs MQTT vs polling: WebSocket is simplest if you only need bidirectional msgs. MQTT shines for IoT or really tight battery budgets. Polling is fine for 30s+ refresh tolerance.
  • E2E encryption: Signal Protocol library; significantly increases complexity (key exchange, multi-device, group ratchet). Out-of-scope for most interviews unless asked.
  • Storage cap: messages grow forever. Either prune older than N days locally and refetch on demand, or let user explicitly clear conversations.
Q.Design an image caching library (like Coil)
+

1. Clarify scope

  • Just images, or also GIF / SVG / video frames?
  • Lifecycle awareness (auto-cancel on view detach)?
  • Transformations (round corners, blur, crop)?
  • Animated formats (animated WebP, AVIF)?
  • Diskless mode for sensitive content (encrypted view)?

2. Functional requirements

  • Load image from URL into ImageView / Compose AsyncImage
  • Memory + disk caching with LRU eviction
  • Request deduplication (same URL → share result)
  • Cancellation when target detaches
  • Transformations applied off main thread
  • Placeholder + error fallback

3. Non-functional requirements

  • Decode never blocks main thread
  • Bitmap memory bounded — don't OOM on long scrolls
  • Hardware acceleration where possible (hardware bitmaps for unmodified images on API 26+)
  • Predictable behavior under memory pressure

4. Layered cache architecture

  1. Memory cache — LRU keyed by (URL + transformation hash + targetSize). Size = 10-25% of app memory budget (ActivityManager.getMemoryClass()).
  2. Disk cache — LRU on internal storage. Default ~250MB. Reuses OkHttp's DiskLruCache or its own.
  3. Network — OkHttp with HTTP cache headers, conditional GETs (ETag).

5. Request pipeline

load("https://...")
   |
   v
[1] Compute cache key (url + size + transforms)
   |
   v
[2] Memory cache hit? --yes--> return bitmap
   | no
   v
[3] Pending request for same key? --yes--> await it
   | no
   v
[4] Disk cache hit? --yes--> decode off-main --> transform --> memory cache --> return
   | no
   v
[5] Network fetch (OkHttp) --> write disk --> decode --> transform --> memory cache --> return
   |
   v
[cancel] At any step: if target detached, cancel coroutine

6. Key design decisions

  • Decode off main thread — Dispatchers.Default (CPU-bound) or limited Dispatchers.IO if disk-bound.
  • Size bitmaps to target — BitmapFactory.Options.inSampleSize; never decode 4K for a 200dp thumbnail.
  • Hardware bitmaps — for unmodified images on API 26+, decode into hardware texture; saves CPU memory.
  • Request deduplication — keep a map of in-flight Deferred<Bitmap> keyed by cache key; concurrent requests await the same result.
  • Cancellation — bind request scope to view lifecycle. View detach triggers job.cancel(); pipeline aborts.
  • Memory budget under pressure — listen to ComponentCallbacks2.onTrimMemory; evict aggressively on TRIM_MEMORY_RUNNING_CRITICAL.

7. Compose integration

@Composable
fun AsyncImage(url: String, modifier: Modifier) {
    val context = LocalContext.current
    val request = remember(url) { ImageRequest(url) }
    val state by produceState<BitmapState>(BitmapState.Loading, request) {
        value = try { BitmapState.Success(imageLoader.execute(request)) }
        catch (e: Exception) { BitmapState.Error }
    }
    when (val s = state) {
        BitmapState.Loading -> Placeholder()
        is BitmapState.Success -> Image(s.bitmap.asImageBitmap(), null, modifier)
        BitmapState.Error -> ErrorIcon()
    }
}

8. Edge cases

  • Same image, different sizes — separate cache entries (the size is part of the key)
  • GIF / animated — decode frame by frame via Movie (legacy) or ImageDecoder (API 28+)
  • Recyclable bitmaps — pool of reusable bitmaps for non-hardware caches; reduces GC churn
  • Long-running scroll — viewport prefetch limited to N pages ahead, no further
  • Network error mid-decode — fall back to placeholder; surface to caller for retry

9. Trade-offs

  • Memory size: bigger cache = faster scroll, more OOM risk. Default 25% of available memory.
  • Disk size: too large bloats app; too small thrashes network. 250MB is a common default.
  • Hardware bitmaps save CPU memory but can't be transformed or accessed; use only for final display path.
Q.Design Uber rider — real-time location tracking
+

1. Clarify scope

  • Just rider, or also driver matching?
  • Map provider — Google Maps SDK or Mapbox?
  • Single ride or multi-stop?
  • Battery vs accuracy preference?
  • What's the expected latency for driver position updates? (1-2 Hz typical)

2. Functional requirements

  • Show rider's own location on map
  • Show nearby drivers in idle state
  • Once matched: show assigned driver moving toward pickup, then to destination
  • ETA updates
  • Live trip status (en route, arrived, in progress, completed)
  • Geofencing for pickup proximity alerts

3. Non-functional requirements

  • Driver marker animates smoothly between updates (no jerky jumps)
  • Battery efficient — adaptive GPS polling
  • Survive flaky 4G; degrade to polling if WebSocket drops
  • Foreground-only — battery use ends when trip ends

4. Architecture

  • Fused Location Provider — combined GPS/WiFi/cell for battery-efficient location
  • WebSocket for driver position updates (server pushes 1-2 Hz during ride)
  • Map SDK (Google Maps / Mapbox) — render driver as marker; route polyline; rider marker
  • Foreground Service during active ride — keeps location active with persistent notification
  • Geofence client — register at pickup; fires when within 100m
  • ViewModel + Repository wraps SDK + WebSocket; emits a single RideState flow

5. Data flow

WebSocket: ← { "type": "driver_pos", "lat": ..., "lng": ..., "bearing": ..., "ts": ... }
   |
   v
Repository: emit DriverPosition to StateFlow
   |
   v
ViewModel: aggregate (rider pos, driver pos, route) into MapUiState
   |
   v
UI: pass new position to marker → interpolate from current to new over 1-2s using
    AnimationSpec.tween(1000, easing = LinearEasing)

6. Deep dive — Smooth movement

Driver position arrives at 1-2 Hz but you want 60fps animation. Solution: between updates, animate the marker linearly using ObjectAnimator or Compose Animatable from current to next position. When next update arrives, snap-then-animate or chain.

val animatable = remember { Animatable(initialLatLng, LatLngVectorConverter) }
LaunchedEffect(driverPos) {
    animatable.animateTo(driverPos, tween(1000, easing = LinearEasing))
}

7. Deep dive — Battery-aware GPS

val request = LocationRequest.Builder(Priority.PRIORITY_BALANCED_POWER_ACCURACY, 4000L)
    .setMinUpdateIntervalMillis(2000L)
    .setMinUpdateDistanceMeters(5f)
    .build()
  • While idle: BALANCED priority, 10s interval, >50m delta
  • During ride: HIGH_ACCURACY, 2-4s interval
  • Stationary detection (activity recognition): pause GPS, resume on motion

8. Edge cases

  • App backgrounded mid-ride — foreground service notification keeps location active; WebSocket may downgrade to FCM-triggered polling
  • WebSocket disconnect — degrade to polling; show "reconnecting" indicator
  • Permission revoked mid-ride — surface persistent banner; can't end trip cleanly without location
  • Location spoofing — server-side detection (impossible-speed jumps)
  • Stale driver position — if no update in 15s, dim marker + show "Reconnecting"
  • Map gestures while marker animating — don't fight user gestures; pause auto-follow when user pans

9. Trade-offs

  • WebSocket vs MQTT vs server-sent events: SSE works for one-way (server → client) and is simpler. Push back if asked — WebSocket fits because rider also sends location for ETA refinement.
  • Update frequency: 1 Hz is enough for smooth animation when interpolated; 5 Hz wastes battery
  • Local map rendering vs server-rendered tiles: map SDKs handle this; rarely need to roll your own
Q.Design Spotify offline downloads
+

1. Clarify scope

  • Single tracks, full playlists, or both?
  • Quality options (lossy 96kbps to lossless FLAC)?
  • DRM-protected (Widevine) content?
  • How long can downloads remain offline before license refresh?
  • User-configurable storage quota?
  • Cellular vs Wi-Fi only?

2. Functional requirements

  • Mark a playlist for offline; system downloads all tracks
  • Resume on interruption (resumable HTTP downloads)
  • Persist encrypted at rest
  • Daily license refresh while online
  • Auto-evict expired or LRU tracks when over quota
  • Show per-playlist download progress

3. Non-functional requirements

  • Survive process death; resume where it left off
  • Respect Wi-Fi-only setting
  • Battery efficient — use WorkManager with constraints
  • Storage capped (user setting); LRU eviction when full

4. Data model

@Entity
data class OfflineTrack(
    @PrimaryKey val trackId: String,
    val filePath: String,
    val totalBytes: Long,
    val downloadedBytes: Long,
    val state: State,                 // QUEUED / DOWNLOADING / READY / EXPIRED / FAILED
    val licenseExpiresAt: Long,
    val lastPlayedAt: Long,
    val quality: Quality
)

@Entity
data class OfflinePlaylist(
    @PrimaryKey val playlistId: String,
    val trackIds: String,             // CSV
    val createdAt: Long
)

5. Architecture

  • WorkManager — chained workers: LicenseFetch → FileDownload → VerifyChecksum → WriteIndex
  • OkHttp with HTTP Range requests for resumable downloads
  • ExoPlayer + Widevine DRM for protected playback
  • Encryption: AES-256, key in Android Keystore (hardware-backed)
  • Foreground Service with notification during active downloads (Android 14+ requires this)
  • Storage manager — tracks quota; LRU eviction; clears on app uninstall (internal storage)

6. Deep dive — Resumable download

suspend fun downloadChunk(track: OfflineTrack) = withContext(Dispatchers.IO) {
    val from = track.downloadedBytes
    val request = Request.Builder()
        .url(track.url)
        .addHeader("Range", "bytes=$from-")
        .build()
    okhttp.newCall(request).execute().use { response ->
        val out = File(track.filePath).outputStream(append = true).buffered()
        response.body!!.source().use { source ->
            val sink = out.sink().buffer()
            var total = from
            val buffer = Buffer()
            while (true) {
                val read = source.read(buffer, 8192L)
                if (read == -1L) break
                sink.write(buffer, read)
                total += read
                dao.updateProgress(track.trackId, total)  // every chunk
                ensureActive()  // cooperative cancellation
            }
            sink.flush()
        }
    }
    verifyChecksum(track)
}

7. Deep dive — License management

  • Each track has a Widevine license fetched on download (offline license)
  • Licenses have an expiry (typically 30 days)
  • Background worker runs daily: refreshes licenses for tracks whose expiry is <3 days away (while online)
  • Expired tracks marked EXPIRED in DB; playback attempt shows "Reconnect to refresh"
  • Worker tries to refresh on next connectivity event via NetworkType.CONNECTED constraint

8. Deep dive — Storage management

  • User-set quota (e.g., 5GB)
  • On every download, check total used. If over quota, evict LRU tracks (by lastPlayedAt)
  • Atomic eviction: delete file → delete DB row → resync index. If app dies between, reconcile on next launch.
  • Don't evict tracks the user has explicitly pinned

9. Edge cases

  • Partial download then user unmarks — cancel worker, delete partial file
  • App killed mid-download — WorkManager re-runs from last downloadedBytes
  • Server URL expired — re-fetch fresh download URL via API, then resume
  • Device runs out of storage — check available before each chunk; surface to user
  • User signs out — wipe all encrypted offline content (per ToS / DRM contract)
  • License server unreachable — show clear "Reconnect to refresh license" UI
  • Wi-Fi only setting — NetworkType.UNMETERED constraint; tracks queued, no progress on cellular

10. Trade-offs

  • Quality vs storage: let user choose. Default to high-quality lossy.
  • Aggressive prefetch (auto-download recommended): nice but consumes user data; opt-in only.
  • WorkManager vs Foreground Service: WorkManager is the modern path; Foreground Service notification required while downloading on Android 14+.
Q.Design a video streaming app (YouTube / Netflix)
🔥
+

Clarify scope: live vs VOD? Offline downloads? Adaptive quality? Captions? Multiple resolutions? Platform: Android TV too?

Functional: browse catalog, play video (adaptive quality), seek, controls, auto-play next, offline download (Premium).

Architecture components:

  • ExoPlayer (Media3) — handles HLS/DASH adaptive bitrate streaming, DRM, buffering, seeking
  • DASH/HLS manifest — server sends manifest listing available quality levels; ExoPlayer picks based on bandwidth
  • Widevine DRM — license request in DASH MPD; ExoPlayer handles natively via DefaultDrmSessionManager
  • MediaSession — integrates with system media controls (lock screen, notification, Android Auto)
  • Foreground Service — for background playback; required on Android 8+

Data model:

data class Video(val id: String, val title: String, val dashUrl: String,
                 val thumbnailUrl: String, val durationMs: Long, val qualities: List<Quality>)
data class PlaybackState(val videoId: String, val positionMs: Long,
                         val quality: Quality, val bufferedPercent: Int)

ExoPlayer setup:

val player = ExoPlayer.Builder(context).build().apply {
    val drmConfig = MediaItem.DrmConfiguration.Builder(C.WIDEVINE_UUID)
        .setLicenseUri(licenseUrl).build()
    val mediaItem = MediaItem.Builder()
        .setUri(dashManifestUrl)
        .setDrmConfiguration(drmConfig)
        .build()
    setMediaItem(mediaItem)
    prepare()
    playWhenReady = true
}

Adaptive bitrate: ExoPlayer's DefaultTrackSelector monitors network bandwidth and picks quality. Override with trackSelector.setParameters(trackSelector.buildUponParameters().setMaxVideoSize(width, height)).

Deep dives:

  • Seeking performance: keyframe indexing in the manifest; seek to nearest keyframe, decode forward
  • Pre-buffering next video: 10s before end, prepare second ExoPlayer instance; swap on completion
  • Thumbnail preview on seek bar: server generates thumbnail sprite images; client interpolates from seek position
  • Background playback: Foreground Service + MediaSession; release ExoPlayer on service destroy

Edge cases: network switch mid-playback (WiFi → cellular) → ExoPlayer adapts automatically; audio focus loss → pause; output device change (Bluetooth connect) → continue or pause based on type.

Trade-offs: DASH vs HLS — DASH is more flexible (multiple periods, side-loaded subtitles); HLS has wider CDN support. For new products, DASH + Widevine is the modern choice.

Q.Design Google Maps — offline tiles, routing, real-time navigation
+

Clarify scope: display map only, or also routing + navigation? Offline maps? Custom markers/overlays? How much offline storage?

Functional: render map tiles, search for places, get directions, turn-by-turn navigation with voice, offline maps for a region.

Architecture layers:

  • Map rendering: vector tiles (Google Maps SDK) or raster tiles (Mapbox). Vector tiles: smaller, scalable, smooth rotation/tilt. Raster: faster decoding for complex tiles.
  • Tile cache: disk-cached by zoom+x+y key. LRU eviction, ~500MB for a metro area.
  • Routing engine: server-side (Directions API) for complex multi-modal routes. For offline: on-device graph + A* or Dijkstra (OSMDroid, Valhalla).
  • Location: Fused Location Provider; HIGH_ACCURACY mode during navigation.
  • Geometry: polyline encoding for route paths (Google Encoded Polyline). Decode client-side to coordinates.

Data model:

data class MapTile(val zoom: Int, val x: Int, val y: Int, val data: ByteArray, val cachedAt: Long)
data class Route(val polyline: List<LatLng>, val steps: List<Step>, val durationSec: Int, val distanceM: Int)
data class Step(val instruction: String, val distanceM: Int, val maneuver: Maneuver, val endLatLng: LatLng)

Turn-by-turn navigation flow:

  1. Route polyline displayed on map
  2. Location updates at 1-2s intervals via Fused Location
  3. Snap to road: project GPS point onto nearest route segment
  4. Monitor distance to next maneuver; trigger voice instruction at configured distance (e.g., "in 500m, turn left")
  5. Off-route detection: if user deviates >50m from polyline → re-route

Offline maps deep dive:

  • User selects a region to download
  • Server generates a tile package (MBTiles format — SQLite containing tiles) for that bounding box + zoom levels 0-15
  • Download via WorkManager with range requests (resumable)
  • Client queries local SQLite first; falls back to network on miss
  • Vector tiles preferred for offline (much smaller than raster)

Edge cases: GPS signal loss in tunnel → dead reckoning from last known heading + speed; map rotation with heading → smooth bearing animation using SphericalUtil.computeHeading; large route polylines (10,000+ points) → simplify with Ramer–Douglas–Peucker algorithm.

Q.Design Google Photos — camera upload pipeline
+

Clarify: auto-backup only or also manual select? Video + photos? What compression? How long is original stored?

Architecture:

  • Capture detection: observe MediaStore via ContentObserver on MediaStore.Images.Media.EXTERNAL_CONTENT_URI — triggers when camera saves a new photo
  • Upload pipeline: WorkManager PeriodicWorkRequest + OneTimeWorkRequest chained: Compress → Encrypt → Upload → Register → UpdateDB
  • Resumable upload: chunk file into 256KB–10MB segments; send Range header; store offset in Room; resume from last confirmed chunk after failure
  • Dedup: hash file (SHA-256) before upload; server rejects if already exists, returns existing URL
  • Constraints: WiFi-only by default; pause on battery saver; stop on low storage
// ContentObserver watching for new photos
contentResolver.registerContentObserver(
    MediaStore.Images.Media.EXTERNAL_CONTENT_URI,
    true,
    object : ContentObserver(Handler(Looper.getMainLooper())) {
        override fun onChange(selfChange: Boolean, uri: Uri?) {
            WorkManager.getInstance(context)
                .enqueueUniqueWork("upload", ExistingWorkPolicy.KEEP, uploadWork)
        }
    }
)

Edge cases: user deletes photo before upload completes → cancel work, delete queue entry; network switches mid-upload → resume with Range header; duplicate photo (same hash) → point to existing, don't re-upload; very large video (4GB+) → multipart upload with server-assigned uploadId.

Q.Design Tinder-style swipe deck — card stack with gestures
+

Clarify: infinite feed or finite batch? Preload how many? Undo swipe? Rewind? Match animations?

Architecture:

  • Data prefetch: preload a deck of N cards (10-20) from API on launch. When deck drops below threshold (3-5 cards remaining), silently fetch more. Users should never see a spinner.
  • Image prefetch: load images for top 3-5 cards eagerly; defer rest. Use Coil's ImagePreloader or manual prefetch via imageLoader.enqueue().
  • Gesture handling: detectDragGestures in Compose; track X/Y offset; rotate card proportional to X drag; at threshold, animate to exit and remove from stack.
@Composable
fun SwipeCard(onSwipe: (Direction) -> Unit) {
    val offsetX = remember { Animatable(0f) }
    val rotation = offsetX.value / 20f  // rotate up to ~15 degrees

    Box(Modifier
        .offset { IntOffset(offsetX.value.roundToInt(), 0) }
        .rotate(rotation)
        .pointerInput(Unit) {
            detectDragGestures(
                onDragEnd = {
                    if (abs(offsetX.value) > 300f) {
                        // Commit swipe
                        val dir = if (offsetX.value > 0) Direction.Right else Direction.Left
                        scope.launch {
                            offsetX.animateTo(if (dir == Direction.Right) 1000f else -1000f)
                            onSwipe(dir)
                        }
                    } else {
                        scope.launch { offsetX.animateTo(0f, spring()) }  // snap back
                    }
                }
            ) { _, drag -> scope.launch { offsetX.snapTo(offsetX.value + drag.x) } }
        }
    ) { ProfileCard() }
}

Edge cases: empty deck (all profiles exhausted) → show "come back later" state; match occurs → overlay match animation before removing; offline → show cached deck, queue swipe actions in outbox.

Q.Design a stock trading / real-time price app
+

Clarify: view-only or actual trading? How many symbols tracked simultaneously? Price update frequency? After-hours data?

Non-functional: prices must be accurate (staleness = financial risk); sub-second latency when market open; battery-aware (WebSocket connection management).

Architecture:

  • WebSocket for live prices during market hours (9:30am–4pm ET)
  • REST polling for pre/after-market and when app backgrounded (reduce battery)
  • Room for portfolio positions, watchlist, price history
  • WorkManager for end-of-day price snapshots (scheduled for 4:05pm ET)
// Price stream — debounce rapid updates, only render changes
webSocketFlow
    .filter { it.symbol in watchlist }
    .groupBy { it.symbol }
    .flatMapMerge { symbolFlow ->
        symbolFlow.sample(250)  // max 4 updates/sec per symbol
    }
    .collect { price -> updatePriceDisplay(price) }

// Chart rendering — defer expensive path calculation
val chartPath = remember(priceHistory) {
    derivedStateOf { buildCandlestickPath(priceHistory) }
}
Canvas(Modifier.fillMaxSize()) { drawPath(chartPath.value, paint) }

Key design decisions: display bid/ask spread or mid-price? How handle market halt (circuit breaker)? Show change % from previous close or session open? Time zone display for international markets?

Edge cases: WebSocket disconnect mid-session → reconnect with exponential backoff; stale price (last update > 5s) → grey out price, show "delayed" indicator; user places order → optimistic portfolio update, confirm on server ack; stock split → recalculate historical prices.

09

Networking & Storage

Networking layer mechanics + persistence options. Often probed alongside system design.

Q.Retrofit vs OkHttp — what's the relationship?
🔥
+

OkHttp is the HTTP client (low-level). Retrofit is a type-safe REST wrapper built ON TOP of OkHttp.

  • OkHttp: sockets, connection pool, HTTP/2, caching, interceptors
  • Retrofit: annotation-based API definitions, serialization (Moshi/Gson/kotlinx), coroutine/Flow support, error handling
interface Api {
    @GET("users/{id}")
    suspend fun user(@Path("id") id: String): User
    
    @POST("users")
    suspend fun create(@Body user: User): Response<User>
}

When use raw OkHttp: file uploads/downloads with progress, WebSockets, non-REST APIs, custom protocols.

Q.How do OkHttp Interceptors work?
+

A chain of pluggable handlers that see every request/response. Two types:

  • Application Interceptors — see what your app wrote/read. Don't see redirects/retries. Add once.
  • Network Interceptors — see actual on-wire data including redirects. Closer to the socket.
class AuthInterceptor(private val tokenProvider: () -> String) : Interceptor {
    override fun intercept(chain: Interceptor.Chain): Response {
        val req = chain.request().newBuilder()
            .addHeader("Authorization", "Bearer ${tokenProvider()}")
            .build()
        return chain.proceed(req)
    }
}

OkHttpClient.Builder()
    .addInterceptor(AuthInterceptor { authStore.token })
    .addInterceptor(HttpLoggingInterceptor())
    .build()

Common uses: auth headers, logging, request retry, response caching, response transformation.

Q.How does HTTP caching work in OkHttp?
+

OkHttp implements RFC 7234 caching. Configure a cache directory and let the server set Cache-Control headers.

val cache = Cache(File(cacheDir, "http"), 10L * 1024 * 1024)
val client = OkHttpClient.Builder().cache(cache).build()

Server tells you:

  • Cache-Control: max-age=3600 — cache 1 hour, no network
  • Cache-Control: no-cache — must revalidate (ETag/If-Modified-Since)
  • Cache-Control: no-store — don't cache at all

Override on client: use a network interceptor to inject Cache-Control if server doesn't set it.

Q.What is certificate pinning and how do you implement it?
L5+
+

Locks your app to specific server certificates (or their public key hashes). Prevents man-in-the-middle attacks even if attacker has a valid CA-signed cert.

val pinner = CertificatePinner.Builder()
    .add("api.example.com", "sha256/AAAAAA...")
    .add("api.example.com", "sha256/BBBBBB...")  // backup pin
    .build()

OkHttpClient.Builder().certificatePinner(pinner).build()

Operational risk: if you rotate the server cert without updating the app, all installs break. Always have a backup pin and a kill-switch (e.g., remote config to disable pinning).

Q.SharedPreferences vs DataStore — which to use?
🔥
+

SharedPreferences is deprecated for new code. Use DataStore.

SharedPreferencesDataStore
APISynchronous + listenerFlow-based, coroutine-first
Type safetyUntyped keysTyped (Preferences) or Proto schema
Main-thread safetyapply() async but commit() blocksAlways async, no blocking
TransactionalNo (race conditions on edit)Yes
ErrorsSilentExposed in flow
Q.Room — what is it and why use it over raw SQLite?
+

An ORM layer over SQLite providing:

  • Compile-time SQL verification — @Query strings checked at build
  • Type-safe DAOs — interface-driven, no Cursor boilerplate
  • Flow / LiveData observers — UI auto-updates on writes
  • Migrations — versioned schemas with manual or auto-migrations
  • Coroutine support — suspending DAO functions
@Entity
data class User(@PrimaryKey val id: String, val name: String)

@Dao
interface UserDao {
    @Query("SELECT * FROM User WHERE id = :id")
    fun observe(id: String): Flow<User?>
    
    @Upsert
    suspend fun upsert(user: User)
}
Q.How do Room migrations work?
L5+
+

Each schema change bumps the database version. You provide a Migration from each old version to the new one — Room runs them in sequence.

val MIGRATION_1_2 = object : Migration(1, 2) {
    override fun migrate(db: SupportSQLiteDatabase) {
        db.execSQL("ALTER TABLE User ADD COLUMN email TEXT")
    }
}

Room.databaseBuilder(ctx, AppDb::class.java, "app.db")
    .addMigrations(MIGRATION_1_2)
    .build()

Auto-migrations (since Room 2.4) handle additive changes (new columns, new tables) without writing SQL — just bump version and declare @AutoMigration(from=1, to=2).

Fallback: fallbackToDestructiveMigration() wipes DB on version bump. Use only in dev.

Q.WebSocket — when use it instead of REST polling?
+

Use WebSocket when you need server-pushed updates with low latency: chat, live scores, presence, collaborative editing, real-time location.

Tradeoffs:

  • WebSocket pros: bidirectional, persistent, low overhead per message
  • WebSocket cons: harder to scale (stateful), proxy/firewall issues, battery (keep-alives)
  • Polling pros: stateless backend, cache-friendly, simple retry
  • Polling cons: latency, wasted requests when nothing changed

Hybrid pattern: WebSocket when foreground; FCM push + REST refresh when backgrounded (battery-friendly).

Q.WorkManager — deep dive: chaining, constraints, retry, observation
🔥
+

Define work with constraints:

val constraints = Constraints.Builder()
    .setRequiredNetworkType(NetworkType.UNMETERED)
    .setRequiresCharging(false)
    .setRequiresBatteryNotLow(true)
    .build()

val uploadWork = OneTimeWorkRequestBuilder<UploadWorker>()
    .setConstraints(constraints)
    .setBackoffCriteria(BackoffPolicy.EXPONENTIAL, 10, TimeUnit.SECONDS)
    .setInputData(workDataOf("fileUri" to uri.toString()))
    .build()

Chaining — sequential pipeline:

WorkManager.getInstance(context)
    .beginUniqueWork("upload", ExistingWorkPolicy.KEEP,
        OneTimeWorkRequestBuilder<CompressWorker>().build())
    .then(OneTimeWorkRequestBuilder<EncryptWorker>().build())
    .then(uploadWork)
    .enqueue()

Observe result:

WorkManager.getInstance(context)
    .getWorkInfoByIdLiveData(uploadWork.id)
    .observe(viewLifecycleOwner) { info ->
        when (info?.state) {
            WorkInfo.State.RUNNING -> showProgress(info.progress.getFloat("progress", 0f))
            WorkInfo.State.SUCCEEDED -> showSuccess()
            WorkInfo.State.FAILED -> showError()
            else -> {}
        }
    }

Periodic work:

val periodicSync = PeriodicWorkRequestBuilder<SyncWorker>(6, TimeUnit.HOURS)
    .setConstraints(constraints).build()
WorkManager.getInstance(context).enqueueUniquePeriodicWork(
    "sync", ExistingPeriodicWorkPolicy.KEEP, periodicSync
)
Q.AlarmManager — setExact, Doze, when it beats WorkManager
+

When to use AlarmManager over WorkManager:

  • User-visible alarms (calendar reminder, medication alarm) — exact user-specified time
  • Requires wakeup even during Doze mode
  • Subsecond precision required
  • Action must trigger at a precise wall-clock time
val alarmManager = getSystemService(AlarmManager::class.java)

// User-visible alarm — shows in system alarm UI, works during Doze
val intent = PendingIntent.getBroadcast(context, 0, Intent(ACTION_ALARM),
    PendingIntent.FLAG_IMMUTABLE)

if (Build.VERSION.SDK_INT >= 31 && !alarmManager.canScheduleExactAlarms()) {
    // Request permission: Settings.ACTION_REQUEST_SCHEDULE_EXACT_ALARM
}
alarmManager.setAlarmClock(
    AlarmManager.AlarmClockInfo(triggerTimeMs, showIntent),
    intent
)
APIDoze?Exact?Use
set()DeferredNoInexact, battery-friendly
setExact()DeferredYesExact but Doze-deferred
setExactAndAllowWhileIdle()FiresYesDuring Doze (limited frequency)
setAlarmClock()FiresYesUser-visible alarms
Q.WorkManager vs AlarmManager vs JobScheduler vs Foreground Service — decision matrix
🔥
+
WorkManagerAlarmManagerForeground Service
Guaranteed exec?✅ Yes✅ Yes (with right API)✅ While running
Exact timing?❌ Approximate✅ ExactN/A (continuous)
Doze-aware?✅ Built-in⚠️ Need right API✅ Immune
Notification required?❌ No❌ No✅ Yes (API 26+)
Survives process death?✅ Yes✅ Yes❌ No
Use forBackground sync, upload, cleanupCalendar reminders, medication alarmsMusic, navigation, ongoing download
Rule of thumb: WorkManager for deferrable work that must complete; AlarmManager for exact-time user-visible alerts; Foreground Service for user-initiated, long-running, user-visible tasks; JobScheduler is the legacy API WorkManager builds on — don't use directly.
Q.Room — relations: one-to-many, many-to-many, and @Relation
+
// One-to-many: User has many Posts
@Entity
data class User(@PrimaryKey val id: String, val name: String)

@Entity
data class Post(@PrimaryKey val id: String, val userId: String, val title: String)

// POJO to hold the result
data class UserWithPosts(
    @Embedded val user: User,
    @Relation(parentColumn = "id", entityColumn = "userId")
    val posts: List<Post>
)

@Dao interface UserDao {
    @Transaction  // required for @Relation queries
    @Query("SELECT * FROM User WHERE id = :id")
    fun getUserWithPosts(id: String): Flow<UserWithPosts>
}
// Many-to-many: Students <-> Courses via junction table
@Entity(primaryKeys = ["studentId", "courseId"])
data class StudentCourseCrossRef(val studentId: String, val courseId: String)

data class StudentWithCourses(
    @Embedded val student: Student,
    @Relation(parentColumn = "id", entityColumn = "id",
              associateBy = Junction(StudentCourseCrossRef::class,
                  parentColumn = "studentId", entityColumn = "courseId"))
    val courses: List<Course>
)
Q.Room — TypeConverters, FTS, and indexes
+

TypeConverters — convert non-primitive types to/from Room-storable types:

class Converters {
    @TypeConverter fun fromTimestamp(value: Long?) = value?.let { Date(it) }
    @TypeConverter fun toTimestamp(date: Date?) = date?.time
    @TypeConverter fun fromList(value: List<String>) = value.joinToString(",")
    @TypeConverter fun toList(value: String) = value.split(",")
}

@Database(entities = [User::class], version = 1)
@TypeConverters(Converters::class)
abstract class AppDatabase : RoomDatabase()

FTS (Full-Text Search):

@Fts4   // or @Fts3
@Entity(tableName = "notes_fts")
data class NotesFts(@PrimaryKey @ColumnInfo(name = "rowid") val rowId: Int, val body: String)

@Query("SELECT * FROM notes_fts WHERE notes_fts MATCH :query")
fun search(query: String): List<NotesFts>

Indexes — speed up queries on non-primary-key columns:

@Entity(indices = [
    Index(value = ["userId"]),               // single column
    Index(value = ["userId", "status"], unique = false)  // composite
])
data class Post(@PrimaryKey val id: String, val userId: String, val status: String)
Q.Cache invalidation strategies — TTL, event-based, write-through, cache-aside
🔥
+

The hardest problem in computer science (along with naming things). Key strategies:

StrategyHowUse when
TTL (Time-To-Live)Expire after N seconds regardlessEventually-consistent data (prices, weather)
Event-based invalidationInvalidate on specific write events (WebSocket, push)Real-time apps; notifications of change available
Write-throughOn write: update cache AND backing store atomicallyRead-heavy, writes are consistent entry points
Write-behind (write-back)Write to cache; flush to store asynchronouslyHigh write throughput, eventual consistency OK
Cache-aside (lazy loading)Cache miss → load from store → populate cacheMost common Android pattern (Room + network)
Version/ETag basedInvalidate when server version changesCollaborative apps, precise invalidation

Android default pattern: cache-aside with TTL. Repository: read Room → if stale (age > TTL), fetch network → update Room → re-emit flow.

Cache stampede: on cache miss, many concurrent requests hit the backend simultaneously. Fix: mutex/lock on the fetch operation + single-flight deduplication (only one inflight request per key).
10

Offline-First & Sync

A senior-level topic. Real consumer apps work offline first, sync second. Probed often in mobile system design rounds.

Q.What does "offline-first" actually mean?
🔥
+

A design philosophy where:

  • UI reads from local storage as the single source of truth — never directly from network
  • Writes go locally first, then sync to server in the background
  • Network is treated as a refresh mechanism, not a hard dependency
  • App is fully usable without connectivity for previously-loaded data

Contrast with "online-first": spinner → request → render. Fails badly on flaky networks.

Architectural pattern: Room as SSOT; Repository decides when to refresh; UI observes flows.

Key benefit beyond offline: snappy UX even when online. Local read returns instantly; refresh happens in the background.
Q.Outbox pattern for reliable writes
🔥
+

Pattern for guaranteeing every user write eventually reaches the server, even across network drops, crashes, or process death.

Implementation:

  1. User action triggers ViewModel method
  2. In one Room transaction: write the entity AND a row to Outbox(opType, payload, attempts, nextRetryAt)
  3. UI Flow re-emits — user sees optimistic state instantly
  4. Enqueue a SyncWorker via WorkManager (idempotent — replace if already enqueued)
  5. SyncWorker drains outbox: read row → call API → on success delete row; on failure increment attempts and set nextRetryAt
  6. WorkManager retries with constraints (network required) and exponential backoff

Why it works: the local transaction is atomic — either both the entity AND the outbox row are written, or neither. Crash before sync? Outbox survives; worker picks it up on next launch.

Q.How do you handle conflict resolution when sync collides?
🔥
+

Common strategies, ranked by complexity:

  • Last-Write-Wins (LWW): server takes the latest timestamp. Simple. Use for non-critical data (settings, preferences).
  • Server-Wins / Client-Wins: one side always defers. Easy but loses data.
  • Version vectors / ETags: client sends "expected version"; server rejects if it changed. Client must re-fetch and merge.
  • Field-level merge: server merges non-conflicting fields automatically; conflicts surface for user resolution.
  • CRDTs (Conflict-free Replicated Data Types): mathematically guarantee convergence. Used by Figma, Linear, Notion. Complex; needs server + client support.
  • Operational Transform (OT): Google Docs / Notion. Transform concurrent ops to preserve intent. Hard to get right.

Pragmatic Android pattern: use ETag / version field. On conflict:

// Optimistic concurrency
PUT /tasks/{id}
If-Match: "etag-abc123"
Response: 412 Precondition Failed → client refetches, presents merge UI, retries
Don't reach for CRDTs unless you need them. For most consumer apps, LWW or ETag-based resolution is enough.
Q.How does optimistic UI work? How do you roll back?
🔥
+

UI updates immediately on user action, before server confirms. If server rejects, roll back.

fun likePost(postId: String) {
    viewModelScope.launch {
        // 1. Snapshot for rollback
        val before = dao.getPost(postId) ?: return@launch
        
        // 2. Optimistic write
        dao.updateLike(postId, liked = true, count = before.likeCount + 1)
        
        try {
            // 3. Server call
            api.like(postId)
        } catch (e: Exception) {
            // 4. Rollback to snapshot
            dao.updateLike(postId, liked = before.likedByMe, count = before.likeCount)
            _events.emit(UiEvent.LikeFailed)
        }
    }
}

Considerations:

  • Snapshot the affected state first — Room hides this naturally since you read the row
  • If a follow-up sync is queued by the outbox, treat success/failure of the worker as the source of truth
  • For multi-step flows (e.g., move task between lists), the optimistic state must be intermediate-valid
Q.How do you detect network state and act on it?
+
fun connectivityFlow(context: Context): Flow<Boolean> = callbackFlow {
    val cm = context.getSystemService(ConnectivityManager::class.java)
    val callback = object : ConnectivityManager.NetworkCallback() {
        override fun onAvailable(network: Network) { trySend(true) }
        override fun onLost(network: Network) { trySend(false) }
    }
    val request = NetworkRequest.Builder()
        .addCapability(NetworkCapabilities.NET_CAPABILITY_INTERNET)
        .addCapability(NetworkCapabilities.NET_CAPABILITY_VALIDATED)
        .build()
    cm.registerNetworkCallback(request, callback)
    awaitClose { cm.unregisterNetworkCallback(callback) }
}.distinctUntilChanged()

Use cases:

  • Banner: "You're offline — changes saved and will sync when reconnected"
  • Wake the sync worker when transitioning offline → online
  • Switch UI controls (e.g., disable "Send" if you don't want optimistic for that flow)
Don't gate writes on connectivity check. Even "online" can drop mid-request. Outbox + retry is more reliable.
Q.How do you sync large data sets efficiently?
L5+
+

Delta sync, not full re-download. Server sends only what changed since the client's last since cursor.

GET /sync?since=<serverCursor>&limit=200

{
  "changes": [
    { "op": "upsert", "entity": "task", "data": {...} },
    { "op": "delete", "entity": "task", "id": "..." }
  ],
  "next_cursor": "...",
  "has_more": true
}

Key points:

  • Cursor is opaque — server's choice (typically last change ID or timestamp)
  • Paginate — loop until has_more = false
  • Apply in transaction — atomic; either all changes for the page apply or none
  • Save cursor only after success — interrupted? Re-fetch the same page on retry
  • Tombstones for deletes — server keeps deleted-record markers so offline clients learn about deletions

Initial sync: for first-time install, deliver a snapshot (compressed JSON or Protobuf), then delta from there.

Q.How do you handle idempotency in writes?
L5+
+

Critical because the outbox + retry pattern means the same write may be sent multiple times (e.g., network ack lost).

Pattern: client generates a unique idempotencyKey (UUID) per operation and sends it with the request. Server stores recent keys; if it sees the same key twice, returns the original response without applying the operation again.

POST /messages
Idempotency-Key: 7c3f8e2a-...
{ "convId": "...", "body": "..." }

// First call: 201 Created, body returned
// Retry with same key: 200 OK, original body returned (no duplicate)

Without idempotency: a flaky network could cause the same payment to charge twice, the same message to appear twice, etc.

Q.What about offline-first for media (images, videos)?
+
  • Thumbnails inline in metadata — small enough to bundle with the feed response. Lets UI render placeholders instantly.
  • Full media downloaded lazily on view or via prefetch worker
  • Disk-cached with LRU bound (Coil handles this)
  • Upload via outbox + foreground service with progress notification
  • Resumable uploads for large files — server endpoints supporting tus protocol or HTTP Range
  • Compression / right-sizing on client before upload — reduces failure rate and data use
11

Security

Increasingly probed at senior level. Real production apps deal with these daily.

Q.How do you store sensitive data securely on Android?
🔥
+

Layered approach by sensitivity:

  • Tokens, keys, passwords: Android Keystore — hardware-backed key storage; keys never leave secure hardware
  • User data: EncryptedSharedPreferences / encrypted DataStore (key derived from Keystore)
  • DB: SQLCipher or Room with encryption layer
  • In memory: minimize lifetime; use CharArray not String for passwords (Strings are immutable, can't zero out)
Never: hardcode secrets in code, log them, or send unencrypted over HTTP.
Q.What is Android Keystore?
+

A system-managed key container backed by hardware (TEE — Trusted Execution Environment, or StrongBox on supported devices). Keys generated in Keystore never expose private material to your app — you only get a handle.

Operations: sign, verify, encrypt, decrypt — all happen inside secure hardware. Your code sees inputs and outputs only.

val keyGen = KeyPairGenerator.getInstance("RSA", "AndroidKeyStore")
keyGen.initialize(
    KeyGenParameterSpec.Builder("alias", KeyProperties.PURPOSE_SIGN)
        .setUserAuthenticationRequired(true)
        .setUserAuthenticationValidityDurationSeconds(30)
        .build()
)
keyGen.generateKeyPair()

Bind to biometric: setUserAuthenticationRequired(true) — key is usable only after fingerprint/face auth.

Q.How do you implement biometric authentication?
+
val biometricPrompt = BiometricPrompt(
    activity,
    ContextCompat.getMainExecutor(activity),
    object : BiometricPrompt.AuthenticationCallback() {
        override fun onAuthenticationSucceeded(result: BiometricPrompt.AuthenticationResult) {
            // result.cryptoObject contains an authenticated Cipher/Signature
        }
    }
)

val info = BiometricPrompt.PromptInfo.Builder()
    .setTitle("Unlock vault")
    .setNegativeButtonText("Cancel")
    .build()

biometricPrompt.authenticate(info, BiometricPrompt.CryptoObject(cipher))

Best practice: bind the auth to a Keystore-backed Cipher. The cipher can only be used IF the user authenticated — defends against an attacker bypassing your prompt logic.

Q.What is ProGuard / R8? How do -keep rules work?
+

R8 shrinks, obfuscates, and optimizes the app. -keep rules tell R8 NOT to remove or rename specific classes/methods.

# Keep entire class and members (e.g., for reflection)
-keep class com.app.api.** { *; }

# Keep only the class name (rename allowed)
-keepnames class com.app.api.Response

# Don't warn about missing classes from optional deps
-dontwarn javax.annotation.**

When you need rules: reflection (Gson type tokens), JNI, libraries that use class names at runtime (Retrofit interfaces, kotlinx.serialization). Many libraries ship consumer-rules.pro — included automatically.

Always test release builds. ProGuard bugs only show up after optimization.

Q.What is Network Security Config?
+

An XML file that declares your app's network security policy — cert pinning, cleartext rules, trust anchors. Loaded by the system; no code required.

<!-- res/xml/network_security_config.xml -->
<network-security-config>
    <base-config cleartextTrafficPermitted="false" />
    <domain-config>
        <domain includeSubdomains="true">api.example.com</domain>
        <pin-set>
            <pin digest="SHA-256">AAAA...</pin>
        </pin-set>
    </domain-config>
</network-security-config>

Since Android 9, cleartext (HTTP) is blocked by default — you'd need to explicitly allow it for specific domains in dev.

12

Design Patterns

Classic GoF patterns mapped to Android idioms, adapted for mobile-specific constraints: aggressive garbage collection (GC) pauses, strict RAM limits, intermittent connectivity, and sudden process termination.

Q.Creational: Singleton — pattern, Kotlin idiom, common pitfalls
+

One instance, globally accessible. In Kotlin, object handles thread-safety, lazy init, and double-check locking for you.

object Analytics {
    fun track(event: String) { ... }
}

Pitfalls:

  • Holding Activity Context → memory leak. Pass ApplicationContext explicitly.
  • Hard to test (no constructor for fakes). Prefer DI (@Singleton via Hilt) when the singleton has dependencies.
  • Hidden global state — couples unrelated code together.
Q.Creational: Object Pool — When to use it and trade-offs?
+

Mobile Use Case: Reusing expensive objects to prevent OutOfMemory (OOM) errors and GC jank during scrolling or animations.

Real Examples: RecyclerView.RecycledViewPool, Message.obtain(), Bitmap Pools in Coil.

When to UseObjects are expensive to create, instantiated frequently, and cause UI stutter.
When to AvoidLightweight objects (Kotlin data classes) — wastes more memory than it saves.
ProsEliminates allocation overhead; ensures smooth 60/120fps rendering.
ConsCan cause memory leaks if objects aren't properly reset before pooling.
Trade-offsCPU vs RAM: You trade the GC CPU overhead for permanent RAM usage and manual state-clearing logic.
 
class PathPool(private val maxSize: Int = 10) {
    private val pool = ArrayDeque<Path>(maxSize)
    fun acquire(): Path = if (pool.isNotEmpty()) pool.removeFirst() else Path()
    fun release(path: Path) {
        if (pool.size < maxSize) {
            path.reset() // CRITICAL: Clear state before pooling
            pool.addLast(path)
        }
    }
}
Q.Creational: Builder & DSL — when to use it in Kotlin?
+

Mobile Use Case: Configuring complex OS payloads (notifications, intents, dialogs) where parameters are optional but immutability is required.

In Kotlin, often replaceable by default args + named params, BUT Builder is still preferred for:

  • Java interop (Java callers can't use named args)
  • Fluent DSLs (e.g., OkHttp, Compose Modifiers)
  • Conditional construction with many optional steps
// Standard Builder Pattern
val request = Request.Builder()
    .url("https://api.example.com")
    .addHeader("Authorization", "Bearer $token")
    .build()

Modern Kotlin DSL alternative:

// DSL Builder for an Analytics Payload
class AnalyticsEvent private constructor(val name: String, val params: Map<String, String>) {
    class Builder(private val name: String) {
        private val params = mutableMapOf<String, String>()
        fun addParam(key: String, value: String) = apply { params[key] = value }
        fun build() = AnalyticsEvent(name, params)
    }
}

fun analyticsEvent(name: String, block: AnalyticsEvent.Builder.() -> Unit): AnalyticsEvent {
    return AnalyticsEvent.Builder(name).apply(block).build()
}
Q.Creational: Factory pattern — what is it?
+

Encapsulates object creation logic so callers don't need to know how to construct concrete types. Used heavily in Android to delegate object creation to the system.

In Android: ViewModelProvider.Factory, FragmentFactory, Hilt-generated factories.

sealed class Animal
class Dog : Animal()
class Cat : Animal()

object AnimalFactory {
    fun create(type: String): Animal = when (type) {
        "dog" -> Dog()
        "cat" -> Cat()
        else -> error("Unknown")
    }
}
Q.Structural: Facade (Repository Pattern) — When to use it?
+

Mobile Use Case: Abstracting the complexity of network conditions and caching behind a single "Offline-First" API.

Real Examples: The standard Android Repository pattern.

When to UseThe UI needs data but shouldn't care if it comes from Room (cache) or Retrofit (network).
When to AvoidPurely online apps with no local database and simple CRUD operations.
ProsSingle Source of Truth (SSOT); prevents duplicate network calls; saves battery by serving cached data.
Trade-offsSimplicity vs. Robustness: Adds layers of indirection, but guarantees app functions during cellular dropouts.
 
class UserRepository(private val api: UserApi, private val dao: UserDao) {
    fun getUser(id: String): Flow<User> = flow {
        val cached = dao.getUser(id)
        if (cached != null) emit(cached) // Serve fast cached data first
        
        try {
            val fresh = api.fetchUser(id)
            dao.insert(fresh) // Update cache quietly
            emit(fresh)
        } catch (e: IOException) { /* Offline? UI already has cached data */ }
    }
}
Q.Structural: Flyweight — When to use it and trade-offs?
+

Mobile Use Case: Drastically reducing RAM usage by sharing common state across thousands of objects.

Real Examples: Compose @Immutable parameters, Android String pool, sharing TextPaint objects.

When to UseLists of 1,000+ items (chat apps, feeds) that share redundant data like formatting.
ProsPrevents OutOfMemory crashes on low-end devices; speeds up rendering.
Trade-offsMemory Savings vs. Immutability: Massively reduces heap size but requires strict separation of shared and unique state.
 
object AvatarFormatFactory {
    private val formatCache = mutableMapOf<Int, Paint>()
    fun getPaint(colorRes: Int, strokeWidth: Float): Paint {
        val hash = Objects.hash(colorRes, strokeWidth)
        return formatCache.getOrPut(hash) {
            Paint().apply { color = colorRes; this.strokeWidth = strokeWidth }
        }
    }
}
Q.Structural: Adapter pattern — Android examples?
+

Converts one interface to another expected by a client.

  • RecyclerView.Adapter — adapts data list to ViewHolder API
  • Retrofit Converter.Factory — adapts JSON bytes to typed objects
  • CallAdapter in Retrofit — adapts Call to suspend / Flow
Q.Behavioral: State Pattern (State Machine / MVI) — When to use it?
+

Mobile Use Case: Modeling the complex reality of mobile networks (Loading, Success, Empty, Network Error).

Real Examples: MVI Architecture, Kotlin sealed class UiState.

When to UseRepresent mutually exclusive UI states and prevent impossible combinations (e.g., showing a progress bar AND an error simultaneously).
ProsCompiler enforces exhaustiveness (when statements); eliminates UI sync bugs.
Trade-offsVerbosity vs. Bug Prevention: Write more code upfront to guarantee the UI cannot enter an illegal state.
 
sealed interface FeedState {
    data object Loading : FeedState
    data class Success(val items: List<Post>) : FeedState
    data class Error(val message: String) : FeedState
}

// In Compose UI
when (val currentState = state.value) {
    is FeedState.Loading -> CircularProgressIndicator()
    is FeedState.Success -> FeedList(currentState.items)
    is FeedState.Error -> ErrorScreen(currentState.message)
}
Q.Behavioral: Command Pattern (Deferred Execution) — When to use it?
+

Mobile Use Case: Guaranteeing execution of a task (like uploading a photo) even if the user swipes the app away or the OS kills it.

Real Examples: WorkManager Workers, Analytics event queues.

When to UseDeferrable, guaranteed background work (syncing logs, uploading media).
When to AvoidImmediate user-facing actions (e.g., processing a credit card transaction while user waits).
ProsSurvives process death; OS optimizes battery by batching commands with other apps' commands.
Trade-offsReliability vs. Immediacy: Guarantees the command executes eventually (saving battery), but not instantly.
 
class UploadPhotoWorker(appContext: Context, params: WorkerParameters) : CoroutineWorker(appContext, params) {
    override suspend fun doWork(): Result {
        return try {
            api.uploadFile(inputData.getString("URI")!!)
            Result.success()
        } catch (e: Exception) {
            Result.retry() // OS will automatically retry when network returns
        }
    }
}
Q.Behavioral: Observer pattern in Android — where do you see it?
+

Mobile Use Case: Reactively updating the UI only when the app is in the foreground, decoupling the data source from the OS lifecycle.

Subject notifies subscribed observers on state change. Seen everywhere in Android:

  • StateFlow / SharedFlow — UI observes ViewModel state. Best practice: use collectAsStateWithLifecycle() to pause observations automatically when the app is backgrounded to save battery.
  • Compose State — composables observe state automatically and trigger recompositions.
  • RecyclerView.AdapterDataObserver — updates lists when underlying data diffs.
  • Lifecycle.LifecycleObserver — components reacting to Activity/Fragment state changes.
Q.Behavioral: Strategy pattern — example use in Android
+

Encapsulate interchangeable algorithms behind a common interface. Caller picks the strategy at runtime.

interface SortStrategy { fun sort(list: List<Item>): List<Item> }
class ByDateSort : SortStrategy { ... }
class ByPriceSort : SortStrategy { ... }

class ListVm(private val strategy: SortStrategy) { ... }

Common in RemoteMediator (different paging strategies), DI-bound implementations swapped via flavor or remote flag.

13

Gradle & Build

Build tooling shows up in senior interviews — most engineers can write code but few can debug a slow build or design a multi-module pipeline.

Q.Project build.gradle vs module build.gradle — what's the difference?
+
  • Project-level (root build.gradle.kts) — applies to all modules. Configure plugin versions, repositories common to all modules, root tasks.
  • Module-level (e.g., :app/build.gradle.kts) — applies the Android Gradle Plugin, declares this module's dependencies, build types, flavors, source sets.

Modern Android projects also have:

  • settings.gradle.kts — declares which modules exist; configures repository management
  • gradle/libs.versions.toml — version catalog (single source of truth for versions)
  • buildSrc/ or build-logic/ — shared Gradle code, convention plugins
Q.Build types vs product flavors — what's the difference?
🔥
+
  • Build types — typically debug and release. Different signing, minification, debuggable flag.
  • Product flavors — different variants of the SAME app for different audiences: free vs paid, staging vs prod, brand A vs brand B.
  • Build variants — every combination (flavor × build type). 2 flavors × 2 build types = 4 variants.
android {
    buildTypes {
        debug { ... }
        release {
            isMinifyEnabled = true
            isShrinkResources = true
            proguardFiles(...)
        }
    }
    flavorDimensions += "env"
    productFlavors {
        create("staging") {
            dimension = "env"
            applicationIdSuffix = ".staging"
            buildConfigField("String", "API_URL", "\"https://staging.example.com\"")
        }
        create("prod") {
            dimension = "env"
            buildConfigField("String", "API_URL", "\"https://api.example.com\"")
        }
    }
}
// Variants: stagingDebug, stagingRelease, prodDebug, prodRelease
Q.implementation vs api vs compileOnly — which to use?
🔥
+
ConfigVisible to consumers?On runtime classpath?Use for
implementation❌✅Default. Most deps.
api✅✅Leaks types to consumers (e.g., return types)
compileOnly❌❌Provided at runtime (e.g., generated annotations)
runtimeOnly❌✅Not needed at compile (e.g., JDBC driver)
ksp / kapt❌❌Annotation processors

Why it matters: using implementation by default massively speeds up incremental builds. Changing an implementation dep doesn't force consumers to recompile.

Q.What is a version catalog (libs.versions.toml)?
+

A TOML file declaring all dependency versions and library aliases in one place. Type-safe accessors are generated for use in any module.

# gradle/libs.versions.toml
[versions]
kotlin = "2.0.20"
compose-bom = "2024.06.00"
retrofit = "2.11.0"

[libraries]
compose-bom = { group = "androidx.compose", name = "compose-bom", version.ref = "compose-bom" }
compose-ui = { group = "androidx.compose.ui", name = "ui" }
retrofit = { group = "com.squareup.retrofit2", name = "retrofit", version.ref = "retrofit" }

[plugins]
android-application = { id = "com.android.application", version = "8.5.0" }
kotlin-android = { id = "org.jetbrains.kotlin.android", version.ref = "kotlin" }

[bundles]
compose = ["compose-ui", "compose-material3", "compose-tooling"]
// In module build.gradle.kts
dependencies {
    implementation(platform(libs.compose.bom))
    implementation(libs.bundles.compose)
    implementation(libs.retrofit)
}

Wins: single source of truth, IDE autocompletion, build catches missing libs at config time, easy to bump a version once and have it apply everywhere.

Q.How do you make Gradle builds faster?
🔥
+
  1. Modularize — independent modules build in parallel and cache
  2. Configuration cache — org.gradle.configuration-cache=true. Skips configuration phase on subsequent builds. Massive wins.
  3. Build cache — org.gradle.caching=true. Reuses task outputs across machines or branches.
  4. Parallel execution — org.gradle.parallel=true. Builds modules concurrently.
  5. Daemon — org.gradle.daemon=true (default). Keeps Gradle process alive between builds.
  6. Increase JVM heap — org.gradle.jvmargs=-Xmx4g on a beefy machine
  7. KSP over kapt — much faster annotation processing for Room, Moshi, Hilt
  8. Remove unused deps — every dep adds compile time
  9. Avoid api when not needed
  10. Disable unused build features — e.g., buildFeatures { buildConfig = false } when not used
Profile first. Run ./gradlew assembleDebug --scan and read the build scan. Don't optimize blind.
Q.How does signing config work?
+

A keystore file + alias + passwords sign your APK / AAB. Without signing, Play Store won't accept it; debug builds use a generic debug keystore.

android {
    signingConfigs {
        create("release") {
            storeFile = file("keystore.jks")
            storePassword = System.getenv("KEYSTORE_PASSWORD")
            keyAlias = "upload"
            keyPassword = System.getenv("KEY_PASSWORD")
        }
    }
    buildTypes {
        release {
            signingConfig = signingConfigs.getByName("release")
        }
    }
}

Never commit the keystore or passwords. Use environment variables or a CI secret manager. Play App Signing (recommended) holds the app signing key for you; you upload signed with a separate "upload key."

Q.R8 / ProGuard rules — when do you need them?
+

R8 shrinks and obfuscates by default in release builds. You need -keep rules when code is accessed reflectively or by name (R8 can't see those references at compile time).

Common cases:

  • Reflection-based serializers (Gson, kotlinx.serialization with TypeTokens)
  • Retrofit interfaces — annotations only visible to a code generator
  • JNI / native callbacks
  • Classes loaded by name (e.g., dynamic plugins)
  • Public APIs consumed by other apps
# Keep all Retrofit annotation interfaces
-keep,allowobfuscation interface ** {
    @retrofit2.http.* <methods>;
}

# Keep data classes used for serialization
-keep class com.app.api.dto.** { *; }

Best practice: rely on the consumer-rules.pro shipped by libraries (Retrofit, Moshi, Room ship these automatically). Only add rules for your own reflective code.

Q.What are convention plugins / how do you share build logic?
L5+
+

In multi-module projects, the same Gradle config repeats in every module — same compile SDK, same Kotlin opts, same dependencies. Convention plugins extract this into one place.

Pattern: create a build-logic Gradle subproject that defines plugins:

// build-logic/convention/src/main/kotlin/AndroidLibraryConventionPlugin.kt
class AndroidLibraryConventionPlugin : Plugin<Project> {
    override fun apply(target: Project) = with(target) {
        with(pluginManager) {
            apply("com.android.library")
            apply("org.jetbrains.kotlin.android")
        }
        extensions.configure<LibraryExtension> {
            compileSdk = 35
            defaultConfig { minSdk = 24 }
            buildFeatures.buildConfig = false
        }
    }
}
// In each library module:
plugins { id("myapp.android.library") }
// That's the whole module config.

Reference: Now in Android (NIA) by Google uses this pattern. Massively reduces config duplication.

17

Compose Animations

Heavily probed at Google and UI-forward companies. Know the full toolkit: animateAsState, AnimatedVisibility, Transition, Animatable, and performance patterns.

Q.animate*AsState — animating a single value reactively
🔥
+

The simplest animation API. The value animates to whatever the target becomes whenever the state changes.

// Animate a Float (alpha, size, progress)
val alpha by animateFloatAsState(
    targetValue = if (visible) 1f else 0f,
    animationSpec = tween(durationMillis = 300, easing = FastOutSlowInEasing),
    label = "alpha"
)

// Animate Dp
val size by animateDpAsState(if (expanded) 200.dp else 50.dp, label = "size")

// Animate Color
val color by animateColorAsState(if (selected) Primary else Surface, label = "color")

// In composable
Box(Modifier.alpha(alpha).size(size).background(color))

Variants: animateIntAsState, animateOffsetAsState, animateRectAsState, animateSizeAsState.

Always set label — used by the Animation Inspector in Android Studio to show what's animating.
Q.AnimatedVisibility — enter/exit animations for showing/hiding
🔥
+
var visible by remember { mutableStateOf(true) }

AnimatedVisibility(
    visible = visible,
    enter = fadeIn(tween(300)) + slideInVertically(initialOffsetY = { -it }),
    exit = fadeOut(tween(200)) + shrinkVertically()
) {
    Text("I animate in and out!")
}

Button(onClick = { visible = !visible }) { Text("Toggle") }

Built-in enter/exit:

  • fadeIn / fadeOut
  • slideInHorizontally / slideOutHorizontally
  • slideInVertically / slideOutVertically
  • expandHorizontally / shrinkHorizontally
  • expandVertically / shrinkVertically
  • scaleIn / scaleOut

Combine with + operator: fadeIn() + expandVertically()

Q.AnimatedContent — animate between different content states
+

When the content changes (not just visibility), AnimatedContent animates between the old and new versions.

var count by remember { mutableStateOf(0) }

AnimatedContent(
    targetState = count,
    transitionSpec = {
        // Slide up when increasing, down when decreasing
        if (targetState > initialState) {
            (slideInVertically { -it } + fadeIn()) togetherWith
            (slideOutVertically { it } + fadeOut())
        } else {
            (slideInVertically { it } + fadeIn()) togetherWith
            (slideOutVertically { -it } + fadeOut())
        }
    },
    label = "counter"
) { target ->
    Text("$target", style = MaterialTheme.typography.displayLarge)
}

Use for: tab content switches, loading→content, screen state transitions, scoreboards, step indicators.

Q.Transition — coordinating multiple properties together
L5+
+

When multiple values must animate in sync (same timeline), use updateTransition instead of multiple animate*AsState calls.

enum class CardState { Collapsed, Expanded }

@Composable
fun AnimatedCard(state: CardState) {
    val transition = updateTransition(state, label = "card")

    val height by transition.animateDp(
        transitionSpec = { tween(400) }, label = "height"
    ) { if (it == CardState.Expanded) 300.dp else 80.dp }

    val elevation by transition.animateDp(
        transitionSpec = { spring(stiffness = Spring.StiffnessLow) }, label = "elevation"
    ) { if (it == CardState.Expanded) 8.dp else 2.dp }

    val color by transition.animateColor(
        transitionSpec = { tween(300) }, label = "color"
    ) { if (it == CardState.Expanded) Primary else Surface }

    Card(modifier = Modifier.height(height), elevation = CardDefaults.cardElevation(elevation),
         colors = CardDefaults.cardColors(containerColor = color)) { ... }
}
Q.Animatable — gesture-driven and manual animation control
+

For animations you drive manually (gestures, physics-based, multi-step sequences).

// Draggable element that springs back
@Composable
fun DragAndRelease() {
    val offsetX = remember { Animatable(0f) }
    Box(Modifier
        .offset { IntOffset(offsetX.value.roundToInt(), 0) }
        .pointerInput(Unit) {
            detectHorizontalDragGestures(
                onDragEnd = {
                    // Spring back to 0
                    launch { offsetX.animateTo(0f, spring(dampingRatio = 0.4f)) }
                }
            ) { _, delta ->
                launch { offsetX.snapTo(offsetX.value + delta) }
            }
        })
}

// Chained animations
LaunchedEffect(Unit) {
    while (true) {
        offsetX.animateTo(200f, tween(800))
        offsetX.animateTo(0f, tween(800))
    }
}
Q.rememberInfiniteTransition — looping animations
+
// Pulsing loading indicator
@Composable
fun PulsingDot() {
    val infiniteTransition = rememberInfiniteTransition(label = "pulse")
    val scale by infiniteTransition.animateFloat(
        initialValue = 0.8f,
        targetValue = 1.2f,
        animationSpec = infiniteRepeatable(
            animation = tween(600, easing = FastOutSlowInEasing),
            repeatMode = RepeatMode.Reverse
        ),
        label = "scale"
    )
    val alpha by infiniteTransition.animateFloat(
        initialValue = 0.4f, targetValue = 1f,
        animationSpec = infiniteRepeatable(tween(600), RepeatMode.Reverse),
        label = "alpha"
    )
    Box(Modifier.scale(scale).alpha(alpha).size(24.dp)
        .background(MaterialTheme.colorScheme.primary, CircleShape))
}
Performance: rememberInfiniteTransition triggers recomposition on every frame if the animated value is read in composition. Prefer reading via graphicsLayer { scaleX = scale } to run animation in the Drawing phase — skips recomposition entirely.
Q.Compose animation performance — graphicsLayer vs recomposition
🔥
+

The biggest animation performance mistake: reading animated state in Composition when it only affects drawing.

// ❌ Slow — causes recomposition every frame
val alpha by animateFloatAsState(...)
Box(Modifier.alpha(alpha))  // alpha read in Composition

// ✅ Fast — deferred to Drawing phase, zero recompositions
val alpha by animateFloatAsState(...)
Box(Modifier.graphicsLayer { this.alpha = alpha })

// ✅ Even better — lambda Modifier defers read automatically
Box(Modifier.graphicsLayer(alpha = { animatable.value }))

Rule: if the animated value only changes a visual property (scale, alpha, translation, rotation), use graphicsLayer. This runs in the Drawing phase, skipping Composition and Layout entirely.

Offset example:

// ❌ Runs in Layout phase — still causes layout pass
Modifier.offset(x = anim.dp)

// ✅ Runs in Drawing phase — no layout pass
Modifier.offset { IntOffset(anim.roundToInt(), 0) }
16

Cross-Platform: RN / Flutter / KMP

Increasingly asked at L5+ as companies go multi-platform. Understand architectures, bridges, code samples, and trade-offs. Critical for your Samsara pipeline.

Q.React Native vs Flutter vs KMP — full comparison
🔥
+
React NativeFlutterKMP
LanguageJavaScript/TypeScriptDartKotlin
UI renderingNative platform widgetsCustom (Skia/Impeller)Native per platform
Code sharing~70% (logic + UI)~95% (logic + UI)~50-70% (logic only usually)
Perf (complex UI)JS bridge overhead*Excellent (custom renderer)Native = best
Native feelHigh (uses real widgets)Custom (pixel-perfect but not native)Perfect (native UI)
EcosystemHuge (npm)Good (pub.dev)Kotlin ecosystem
Production useMeta, Shopify, MicrosoftGoogle Pay, eBay, BMWNetflix, Airbnb, Cash App
Hot reload✅ Fast Refresh✅ Stateful Hot Reload✅ (Android only)
Android dev rampModerate (JS knowledge)Moderate (Dart)Low (Kotlin already known)

*RN New Architecture (JSI) eliminates the async JS bridge for TurboModules — performance gap closes significantly.

Decision framework: web team building mobile → RN. Design-system heavy, pixel-perfect UI → Flutter. Kotlin team wanting iOS too → KMP. Pure native perf required on both → KMP with native UI (Compose + SwiftUI).
Q.React Native architecture — old bridge vs new (Fabric / JSI / TurboModules)
+

Old architecture (pre-0.68):

  • JS thread ↔ Shadow thread ↔ Main thread communication via asynchronous JSON-serialized bridge
  • Every native call serialized to JSON string, sent over bridge, deserialized on native side
  • Bottleneck: can't share memory directly; large payloads slow; bridge is single-threaded

New architecture (0.68+, stable in 0.73+):

  • JSI (JavaScript Interface) — C++ layer; JS can hold references to native C++ objects directly. Zero serialization.
  • TurboModules — lazy-loaded native modules via JSI. Synchronous calls possible. Types auto-generated from TypeSpec.
  • Fabric — new C++ rendering engine. Synchronous layout; shared native trees; supports concurrent features.
  • Codegen — TypeSpec → auto-generates both JS and native type stubs. Type-safe bridge.
Q.React Native — Writing a Native Module in Kotlin (old bridge style)
+
// 1. Define the module
class ToastModule(private val reactContext: ReactApplicationContext)
    : ReactContextBaseJavaModule(reactContext) {

    override fun getName() = "ToastModule"  // JS access: NativeModules.ToastModule

    @ReactMethod
    fun show(message: String, duration: Int) {
        Toast.makeText(reactContext, message,
            if (duration == 0) Toast.LENGTH_SHORT else Toast.LENGTH_LONG
        ).show()
    }

    @ReactMethod
    fun add(a: Double, b: Double, promise: Promise) {
        try {
            promise.resolve(a + b)
        } catch (e: Exception) {
            promise.reject("ADD_ERROR", e.message)
        }
    }
}

// 2. Register in Package
class MyPackage : ReactPackage {
    override fun createNativeModules(ctx: ReactApplicationContext) =
        listOf(ToastModule(ctx))
    override fun createViewManagers(ctx: ReactApplicationContext) = emptyList()
}

// 3. Register Package in MainApplication
override fun getPackages() = listOf(MainReactPackage(), MyPackage())
// 4. Call from JavaScript
import { NativeModules } from 'react-native';
const { ToastModule } = NativeModules;
ToastModule.show('Hello from JS!', 0);
const result = await ToastModule.add(3, 4);  // 7
Q.React Native — Embedding RN inside an existing native Android app
+
// 1. Add to existing Activity / Fragment
class MyRNActivity : AppCompatActivity(), DefaultHardwareBackBtnHandler {
    private lateinit var mReactRootView: ReactRootView
    private lateinit var mReactInstanceManager: ReactInstanceManager

    override fun onCreate(savedInstanceState: Bundle?) {
        super.onCreate(savedInstanceState)
        mReactRootView = ReactRootView(this)
        mReactInstanceManager = ReactInstanceManager.builder()
            .setApplication(application)
            .setCurrentActivity(this)
            .setBundleAssetName("index.android.bundle")
            .setJSMainModulePath("index")
            .addPackage(MainReactPackage())
            .setUseDeveloperSupport(BuildConfig.DEBUG)
            .setInitialLifecycleState(LifecycleState.RESUMED)
            .build()
        // "RNModule" is the registered component name
        mReactRootView.startReactApplication(mReactInstanceManager, "RNModule", null)
        setContentView(mReactRootView)
    }
    override fun invokeDefaultOnBackPressed() { super.onBackPressed() }
}

Key decisions for brownfield integration:

  • Decide which screens stay native, which move to RN — don't mix in the same screen
  • Share state via native modules (pass initial props, RN calls native APIs for data)
  • Navigation: native stack for native screens; RN navigator for RN screens; deep links coordinate
  • One ReactInstanceManager per app — expensive to create; reuse across activities
Q.Flutter — Platform Channels with Android Kotlin code
+

Flutter communicates with native Android via Platform Channels — asynchronous, typed message passing.

// 1. Dart side (flutter)
class BatteryService {
    static const _channel = MethodChannel('com.example/battery');

    Future<int> getBatteryLevel() async {
        final int level = await _channel.invokeMethod('getBatteryLevel');
        return level;
    }
}
// 2. Android side (Kotlin) — in MainActivity or Plugin
class MainActivity : FlutterActivity() {
    private val CHANNEL = "com.example/battery"

    override fun configureFlutterEngine(flutterEngine: FlutterEngine) {
        super.configureFlutterEngine(flutterEngine)
        MethodChannel(flutterEngine.dartExecutor.binaryMessenger, CHANNEL)
            .setMethodCallHandler { call, result ->
                when (call.method) {
                    "getBatteryLevel" -> {
                        val bm = getSystemService(BATTERY_SERVICE) as BatteryManager
                        result.success(bm.getIntProperty(BatteryManager.BATTERY_PROPERTY_CAPACITY))
                    }
                    else -> result.notImplemented()
                }
            }
    }
}

Channel types:

  • MethodChannel — request/response (shown above)
  • EventChannel — stream of events from native to Dart (sensor data, location)
  • BasicMessageChannel — arbitrary message codec, lowest-level
Q.KMP architecture — expect/actual, source sets, Ktor, SQLDelight
+

Source set hierarchy:

src/
  commonMain/       ← shared Kotlin: business logic, models, repository interfaces
  androidMain/      ← Android-specific actual declarations + Android APIs
  iosMain/          ← iOS-specific actual declarations + Objective-C interop
  commonTest/
  androidTest/
  iosTest/
// expect/actual: declare in common, implement per platform
// commonMain
expect class Platform() { val name: String }
expect fun randomUUID(): String

// androidMain
actual class Platform actual constructor() {
    actual val name = "Android ${Build.VERSION.SDK_INT}"
}
