15

Concurrency & Threading

Classic threading model that coroutines are built on top of. Probed in concurrency rounds at Google. Know JVM fundamentals even though you use coroutines daily.

Q.Thread vs Coroutine — what's actually different at the JVM level?
🔥
+

Thread: OS-managed execution unit. Stack allocated (~1MB). Context switches are expensive (kernel call). JVM threads are 1:1 with OS threads.

Coroutine: application-managed, stackless continuation. Extremely lightweight (~few hundred bytes). Suspend/resume is a function call — zero kernel involvement. Thousands can coexist on a handful of threads.

ThreadCoroutine
Creation cost~1MB stack, OS callFew hundred bytes, pure Kotlin
10,000 concurrent10GB RAM, likely OOM~few MB, fine
Context switchOS scheduler, expensiveCooperative, free
BlockingBlocks the threaddelay() suspends, frees thread
Coroutines run ON threads — they don't replace threads. A dispatcher manages which thread(s) a coroutine runs on. Dispatchers.IO has a thread pool behind it.
Q.ExecutorService types — when to use each?
+
FactoryBehaviorUse for
newSingleThreadExecutor()1 thread, queueSequential tasks that must not overlap
newFixedThreadPool(n)n threads, unbounded queueKnown-concurrency batch work
newCachedThreadPool()Unlimited threads, idle timeout 60sShort-lived async tasks; dangerous for CPU-bound work
newScheduledThreadPool(n)n threads, supports delay/periodicRecurring tasks (prefer WorkManager on Android)
ForkJoinPool.commonPool()Work-stealing, shared JVM poolParallel streams, CompletableFuture
// Still used in Android for coroutine interop
val executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(4)
val dispatcher = executor.asCoroutineDispatcher()
scope.launch(dispatcher) { /* work */ }
Virtual threads (JDK 21): Android does not ship JDK 21 virtual threads. Coroutines are the Android equivalent.
Q.synchronized vs ReentrantLock — which to use?
+
synchronizedReentrantLock
SyntaxLanguage keywordExplicit lock/unlock
FairnessNoOptional fair mode
Try-acquire❌✅ tryLock(timeout)
Multiple conditions1 (wait/notify)Multiple Conditions
Interruptible acquire❌✅ lockInterruptibly()
// synchronized — simple, preferred default
@Synchronized fun counter() { count++ }

// ReentrantLock — when you need tryLock / multiple conditions
private val lock = ReentrantLock()
fun safeOp() {
    if (lock.tryLock(100, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS)) {
        try { /* critical section */ }
        finally { lock.unlock() }
    } else { /* couldn't acquire */ }
}
Coroutine alternative: use kotlinx.coroutines.sync.Mutex for suspending (non-blocking) mutual exclusion. Never hold a JVM lock across a suspend point.
Q.volatile vs AtomicInteger — what each guarantees?
🔥
+
  • volatile — guarantees visibility (writes immediately visible to all threads) but NOT atomicity. Reading + writing is still two separate ops — race condition possible.
  • AtomicInteger — guarantees visibility AND atomicity via CAS (Compare-And-Swap) hardware instruction. incrementAndGet() is a single atomic op.
// ❌ volatile doesn't protect compound ops
@Volatile var count = 0
fun broken() { count++ }  // read, increment, write — 3 steps, not atomic

// ✅ AtomicInteger is safe for compound ops
val count = AtomicInteger(0)
fun safe() { count.incrementAndGet() }

// ✅ Also safe — coroutine confined to single thread
var count = 0  // only accessed in singleThreadDispatcher
When to use each: volatile for single-write flags (started, stopped). AtomicInteger/AtomicReference for counters or state machines. Mutex/coroutine confinement for complex compound operations.
Q.Java/Kotlin Memory Model — happens-before and visibility
L5+
+

The JMM defines when one thread's writes are guaranteed to be visible to another thread.

Happens-before relationship guarantees visibility:

  • Within a single thread: each action happens-before the next
  • synchronized block release HB the next acquire of the same monitor
  • volatile write HB every subsequent volatile read of that field
  • Thread start HB all actions in the started thread
  • Thread join HB all actions after the join

Without HB: the compiler and CPU can reorder instructions. An update on Thread A may not be seen by Thread B for an indeterminate time.

// Classic double-checked locking (broken pre-Java 1.5)
class Singleton {
    @Volatile  // required! — forces HB between write and read
    private var instance: Singleton? = null
    fun get() = instance ?: synchronized(this) {
        instance ?: Singleton().also { instance = it }
    }
}

// Kotlin object is simpler and correct (class-loading guarantees HB)
object Singleton { ... }
Q.Race conditions and deadlocks — detection and prevention
+

Race condition: outcome depends on thread scheduling. Happens when shared mutable state is accessed without synchronization.

// ❌ Race — two threads can read balance simultaneously
var balance = 1000
fun withdraw(amount: Int) { balance -= amount }

// ✅ Fix — synchronized or Atomic
val balance = AtomicInteger(1000)
fun withdraw(amount: Int) { balance.addAndGet(-amount) }

Deadlock: Thread A holds lock 1, waits for lock 2. Thread B holds lock 2, waits for lock 1. Both block forever.

4 necessary conditions (all must hold): mutual exclusion, hold-and-wait, no preemption, circular wait.

Prevention:

  • Lock ordering — always acquire locks in the same order globally
  • tryLock with timeout — detect and back off
  • Lock-free structures — CAS-based AtomicReference
  • Single-threaded access — confine state to one coroutine/thread (actor pattern)
Q.Producer-Consumer pattern — implementation with coroutines
+

Producer generates items; consumer processes them; a channel decouples their rates.

// Coroutines channel approach
fun produceNumbers(): ReceiveChannel<Int> = produce {
    repeat(100) { send(it) }
}

fun CoroutineScope.consume(ch: ReceiveChannel<Int>) = launch {
    for (n in ch) processNumber(n)
}

// Usage
val producer = produceNumbers()
repeat(4) { consume(producer) }  // 4 parallel consumers

// With backpressure — bounded channel blocks producer when full
val bounded = Channel<Item>(capacity = 16)
launch { for (item in items) bounded.send(item) }
launch { for (item in bounded) process(item) }

Classic Java — BlockingQueue:

val queue: BlockingQueue<Item> = LinkedBlockingQueue(16)
// Producer thread
Thread { items.forEach { queue.put(it) } }.start()
// Consumer thread  
Thread { while (true) process(queue.take()) }.start()
Q.Mutex in Coroutines — when to use it vs synchronized?
L5+
+

kotlinx.coroutines.sync.Mutex is a coroutine-aware mutual exclusion primitive. Unlike synchronized, it suspends (not blocks) the waiting coroutine.

val mutex = Mutex()

// ✅ Correct — suspending, doesn't block thread
suspend fun criticalSection() = mutex.withLock {
    // only one coroutine here at a time
    sharedState++
}

// ❌ Wrong — holding synchronized lock across suspend point
@Synchronized
suspend fun broken() {
    delay(100)  // another coroutine on same thread can't acquire lock
    sharedState++  // but may have been mutated during delay
}

Alternatives to Mutex:

  • Single-threaded dispatcher — confine all access to one thread: Dispatchers.Default.limitedParallelism(1)
  • StateFlow — for observable state; use update { } which is CAS-based
  • Actor — actor { } pattern (experimental); channel-based sequential state machine
Q.Coroutines vs RxJava — why Coroutines won, when RxJava still relevant
+
RxJavaCoroutines + Flow
Learning curveVery steep (80+ operators)Gentler (structured)
Error handlingComplex (onError terminates stream)try/catch works naturally
BackpressureBuilt-in (Flowable)Manual (buffer/conflate)
DebuggingHard (stack traces scrambled)Readable traces with IDEA coroutine debugger
CancellationManual disposable managementStructured, automatic
Android integrationVia RxAndroid schedulersNative (viewModelScope, lifecycleScope)

RxJava still relevant when:

  • Existing large codebase written in RxJava
  • Library you depend on exposes RxJava types
  • switchMap / throttleWithTimeout heavy use (trivially translates to flatMapLatest / debounce)

Migration path: RxJava Observable → Kotlin Flow using Observable.asFlow() from kotlinx-coroutines-rx3. Incremental, room by room.

Q.Custom Dispatchers — limitedParallelism and when to create one
L5+
+

All standard dispatchers are shared. Sometimes you need dedicated concurrency control.

// Limit parallelism on IO — e.g., DB writes only 2 at a time
val dbDispatcher = Dispatchers.IO.limitedParallelism(2)

// Dedicated single-threaded dispatcher (replaces newSingleThreadExecutor)
val singleThread = Dispatchers.Default.limitedParallelism(1)

// From an existing executor (Java interop)
val myExecutor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(4)
val dispatcher = myExecutor.asCoroutineDispatcher()

Use cases:

  • Rate-limit concurrent DB writes
  • Serialize access to a non-thread-safe SDK (Bluetooth, some media APIs)
  • Dedicated dispatcher for a Foreground Service's work
Remember to close executor-backed dispatchers: dispatcher.close() when the owning scope ends. Otherwise thread pool leaks.
14

Cryptography & Encryption

Security-round fundamentals. Probed at Google, Stripe, banking apps. Every senior engineer should know AES modes, key management, and common pitfalls.

Q.Symmetric vs Asymmetric encryption — fundamental difference?
🔥
+

Symmetric: same key encrypts and decrypts. Fast, suited for bulk data. Problem: how do you securely share the key?

Asymmetric: public/private key pair. Public key encrypts; private key decrypts. Solves key distribution but ~1000× slower.

Symmetric (AES)Asymmetric (RSA/ECC)
Key count1 shared2 (public + private)
SpeedVery fast (GB/s)Slow (KB/s)
Use forBulk data encryptionKey exchange, signatures, auth
ExamplesAES-256-GCMRSA-2048, ECDSA P-256
Hybrid approach (TLS, Signal): asymmetric to exchange a one-time session key; symmetric for the actual data. Best of both.
Q.AES modes — ECB vs CBC vs GCM. Why GCM wins?
🔥
+

AES is a block cipher — it encrypts 16-byte blocks. The mode defines how multiple blocks are chained.

ModeIV needed?Auth?Use
ECB❌❌Never — identical plaintext blocks produce identical ciphertext (penguin attack)
CBC✅ random❌Encrypts only — vulnerable to padding oracle if not authenticated separately
GCM✅ random✅ built-inDefault choice. Authenticated Encryption with Associated Data (AEAD)

GCM wins because: integrity is built in (the auth tag detects tampering); no padding needed; parallelizable.

Critical: never reuse the same IV/nonce with the same key in GCM. IV must be random (12 bytes) and unique per message. Reuse breaks confidentiality and authenticity.
// Android AES-256-GCM
val keyGen = KeyGenerator.getInstance("AES").apply { init(256) }
val secretKey = keyGen.generateKey()

val cipher = Cipher.getInstance("AES/GCM/NoPadding")
val iv = ByteArray(12).also { SecureRandom().nextBytes(it) }
cipher.init(Cipher.ENCRYPT_MODE, secretKey, GCMParameterSpec(128, iv))
val ciphertext = cipher.doFinal(plaintext)
// Store: iv + ciphertext (IV is not secret, must be stored alongside)
Q.RSA vs ECC — when to use which?
+

Both asymmetric. ECC provides equivalent security with much smaller keys.

Security levelRSA key sizeECC key size
80-bit1024 (deprecated)160-bit
128-bit3072256-bit (P-256)
256-bit15360521-bit

Use ECC (ECDSA / ECDH) for: mobile, TLS 1.3 (P-256 required), Keystore keys, constrained devices.

Use RSA for: legacy system interop, certificate signing CAs.

Android Keystore: prefer EC keys with SECP256R1 curve for signing, or AES for encryption. RSA in Keystore is slower and larger.

Q.Password hashing — why SHA-256 is wrong, what to use instead?
+

SHA-256 is a general-purpose hash designed to be fast (billions/second on a GPU). For passwords, speed is the enemy.

Password hashing requirements:

  • Slow by design — makes brute force prohibitive
  • Salted — unique salt per password defeats rainbow tables
  • Tunable cost — can increase work factor as hardware improves
AlgorithmRecommended?Notes
MD5 / SHA-1❌Broken, fast, don't use
SHA-256❌ for passwordsToo fast for passwords
bcrypt✅Work factor 10-12; 2024 standard baseline
scrypt✅Memory-hard; resistant to ASICs
Argon2id✅✅ (best)Winner of Password Hashing Competition; tunable time + memory
Mobile note: you almost never hash passwords client-side. Send password to server over TLS; server hashes with Argon2id. Never store plaintext or SHA passwords server-side.
Q.What is HMAC and when do you need it?
+

Hash-based Message Authentication Code — combines a secret key with a hash function to produce an authentication tag. Provides both integrity (data wasn't altered) and authenticity (comes from key holder).

HMAC(key, message) = Hash(key XOR opad || Hash(key XOR ipad || message))

Use HMAC when:

  • Verifying an API request wasn't tampered with
  • Generating signed tokens (JWT uses HMAC-SHA256 in HS256)
  • Verifying webhook authenticity (GitHub, Stripe send HMAC of payload)
  • When you don't need encryption, just integrity + authenticity
val mac = Mac.getInstance("HmacSHA256")
mac.init(SecretKeySpec(secretKeyBytes, "HmacSHA256"))
val tag = mac.doFinal(message)  // send alongside message
vs plain Hash: a hash without a key is publicly verifiable — anyone can compute it. HMAC requires the key, so only the key holder can verify.
Q.TLS handshake — what happens when your app makes an HTTPS request?
+
  1. TCP connect — 3-way handshake to server port 443
  2. ClientHello — client sends TLS version, supported cipher suites, random nonce
  3. ServerHello — server picks cipher suite, sends its certificate + random nonce
  4. Certificate validation — client verifies cert chain against trusted CAs, checks expiry, checks hostname
  5. Key exchange (TLS 1.3) — ECDHE: both sides compute shared secret from ephemeral key pairs. Server signs with private key (proof of identity).
  6. Session keys derived — from shared secret + both nonces using HKDF
  7. Finished messages — both sides send MAC of entire handshake, verify the other's
  8. Application data — encrypted with AES-GCM session key

TLS 1.3 wins over 1.2: 1-RTT handshake (1.2 was 2-RTT); removed weak ciphers (RC4, 3DES, RSA key exchange); forward secrecy mandatory (ECDHE).

Android: OkHttp negotiates TLS and verifies certs automatically. You care about this for: certificate pinning, debug proxies (Charles), mutual TLS (mTLS), and security reviews.
Q.Common crypto pitfalls — what not to do
🔥
+
  • ECB mode — identical input blocks → identical output (the penguin attack). Always use GCM or CTR.
  • Reusing IV/nonce — in GCM, IV reuse with the same key leaks the auth key and breaks confidentiality.
  • Weak randomness — never use Random() for crypto. Always SecureRandom().
  • Hardcoded keys/IVs — extractable via APK decompilation in seconds. Use Android Keystore.
  • Rolling your own crypto — subtle errors invalidate every guarantee. Use javax.crypto + Keystore + established libraries.
  • Logging sensitive data — keys, tokens, plaintext in Logcat → extractable on debug builds via adb.
  • Ignoring TrustManager — never implement a TrustManager that accepts all certs (return null) even for testing. Use a debug-only trust manager scoped to the build variant.
  • MD5 / SHA-1 for integrity — both are broken for collision resistance. Use SHA-256+.
// ❌ Wrong — predictable
val iv = ByteArray(12)  // all zeros

// ✅ Right — random per message
val iv = ByteArray(12).also { SecureRandom().nextBytes(it) }
Q.Android Keystore — encrypt/decrypt data end to end
+

Full flow: generate an AES key in Keystore, use it to encrypt data, store ciphertext, decrypt later.

const val KEY_ALIAS = "myAppKey"

// 1. Generate key (once; check if already exists)
fun getOrCreateKey(): SecretKey {
    val ks = KeyStore.getInstance("AndroidKeyStore").also { it.load(null) }
    (ks.getKey(KEY_ALIAS, null) as? SecretKey)?.let { return it }
    return KeyGenerator.getInstance(KeyProperties.KEY_ALGORITHM_AES, "AndroidKeyStore")
        .apply {
            init(KeyGenParameterSpec.Builder(KEY_ALIAS,
                KeyProperties.PURPOSE_ENCRYPT or KeyProperties.PURPOSE_DECRYPT)
                .setBlockModes(KeyProperties.BLOCK_MODE_GCM)
                .setEncryptionPaddings(KeyProperties.ENCRYPTION_PADDING_NONE)
                .setKeySize(256)
                .build())
        }.generateKey()
}

// 2. Encrypt
fun encrypt(plaintext: ByteArray): Pair<ByteArray, ByteArray> {
    val cipher = Cipher.getInstance("AES/GCM/NoPadding")
    cipher.init(Cipher.ENCRYPT_MODE, getOrCreateKey())
    val iv = cipher.iv        // 12 bytes, generated by cipher
    val ct = cipher.doFinal(plaintext)
    return iv to ct           // store both
}

// 3. Decrypt
fun decrypt(iv: ByteArray, ciphertext: ByteArray): ByteArray {
    val cipher = Cipher.getInstance("AES/GCM/NoPadding")
    cipher.init(Cipher.DECRYPT_MODE, getOrCreateKey(), GCMParameterSpec(128, iv))
    return cipher.doFinal(ciphertext)
}
Key stays in hardware — the key material never enters your app's process. All crypto ops happen inside the Trusted Execution Environment.
18

KMP Literacy

Enough to speak fluently without overclaiming. 23% adoption and rising fast at companies like Netflix, Airbnb, Cash App.

Q.What is Kotlin Multiplatform (KMP)?
+

A technology for sharing Kotlin code across platforms (Android, iOS, web, desktop, server). Unlike Flutter/React Native, KMP keeps UI native — you share business logic, networking, data, validation.

Compiles to: JVM bytecode (Android, server), native binaries via LLVM (iOS, macOS, Linux), JavaScript/Wasm (web).

Q.Explain the expect/actual mechanism
+

The contract pattern for platform-specific implementations. expect declares a shape in common code; each platform provides an actual.

// commonMain
expect class Platform() {
    val name: String
}

// androidMain
actual class Platform actual constructor() {
    actual val name = "Android ${Build.VERSION.SDK_INT}"
}

// iosMain
actual class Platform actual constructor() {
    actual val name = "iOS ${UIDevice.currentDevice.systemVersion}"
}
Q.What's typically shared vs platform-specific in KMP?
+
LayerShared?
Networking (Ktor)✅
Data models, validation✅
Business logic / use cases✅
Database (SQLDelight)✅
State management✅ (ViewModels can be shared)
UIUsually native (Compose for Android, SwiftUI for iOS). Compose Multiplatform changes this — iOS stable in 2025.

Pragmatic 2026 pattern: share everything below UI; let each platform build native UI for the best UX. Airbnb hit 95% code share with this model.

Q.Interview answer if asked "do you know KMP?"
+

"Not in production, but I've explored the model. KMP fits well for consumer Android apps at scale — share business logic, networking, and data while keeping native UI on each platform. That's where the industry's converged in 2026 with Netflix, Airbnb, and Cash App. I'd ramp on expect/actual, Ktor for networking, and SQLDelight for persistence. Compose Multiplatform is the natural next step for shared UI on suitable surfaces."

22

Feature Flags, A/B Testing & Observability

Production engineering essentials. Your Alexa observability work maps directly here. Crash reporting, structured logging, flag architecture, and A/B test design.

Q.Feature flags — architecture and why they matter
+

Feature flags decouple code deployment from feature release. You ship code disabled, enable via config, test in production safely.

Types:

  • Release toggles — hide incomplete features. Removed once fully launched.
  • Experiment flags — A/B tests. Route % of users to variant. Measure, pick winner, clean up.
  • Ops flags — kill switches. Disable a broken feature without deploying a hotfix.
  • Permission flags — enable for specific users (beta, internal, premium).

Android implementation options:

  • Firebase Remote Config — most common. Fetch config on app start, cache, apply. Supports user properties for targeting.
  • In-house config service — custom REST endpoint returning flag JSON. More control.
  • Local hardcoded flags — for dev only. Never in production.
Q.Firebase Remote Config — implementation pattern
+
class FeatureFlagRepository(
    private val remoteConfig: FirebaseRemoteConfig
) {
    // Define defaults — always have a safe fallback
    private val defaults = mapOf(
        "new_checkout_enabled" to false,
        "max_upload_size_mb" to 10L,
        "home_banner_text" to "Welcome back"
    )

    init {
        remoteConfig.setDefaultsAsync(defaults)
    }

    // Fetch on app start — non-blocking
    suspend fun fetchAndActivate() = withContext(Dispatchers.IO) {
        try {
            remoteConfig.fetchAndActivate().await()
        } catch (e: Exception) {
            // Defaults already set — safe to fail silently
        }
    }

    fun isNewCheckoutEnabled(): Boolean =
        remoteConfig.getBoolean("new_checkout_enabled")

    fun getMaxUploadSizeMb(): Long =
        remoteConfig.getLong("max_upload_size_mb")
}

// In ViewModel
viewModelScope.launch {
    flagRepository.fetchAndActivate()
    _state.update { it.copy(showNewCheckout = flagRepository.isNewCheckoutEnabled()) }
}
Caching: Remote Config caches values locally. Default fetch interval is 12 hours in production, 0 in debug. Don't fetch on every screen load — fetch once on app start and cache for the session.
Q.A/B testing — exposure logging, assignment, and analysis
L5+
+

A/B testing requires more than just flag assignment. Three phases:

1. Assignment: user is bucketed into control or variant. Must be deterministic (same user always gets same variant). Usually user ID hashed against experiment ID.

2. Exposure logging: log the moment a user sees the feature — not when the flag is checked. Critical: if you log when the flag is checked (before the user could see it), you dilute the sample with users who never had a chance to be affected.

fun showNewCheckoutScreen() {
    if (flags.isNewCheckoutEnabled()) {
        analytics.logExposure("new_checkout", variant = "treatment")  // log HERE
        navigator.showNewCheckout()
    } else {
        navigator.showOldCheckout()
    }
}

3. Analysis: compare conversion/retention/crash metrics between control and treatment. Need statistical significance before calling a winner (typically p < 0.05, 80%+ power).

Common mistake: running multiple A/B tests on the same user population simultaneously without accounting for interaction effects. Use mutual exclusivity groups.
Q.Crash reporting and observability — Crashlytics, Sentry, custom breadcrumbs
🔥
+

Crashlytics (Firebase): most common for Android. Automatic crash capture, non-fatal exceptions, ANR detection.

// Non-fatal — for caught exceptions you want to track
try { riskyOperation() }
catch (e: Exception) {
    FirebaseCrashlytics.getInstance().recordException(e)
}

// Custom keys — appear in crash report for context
FirebaseCrashlytics.getInstance().apply {
    setCustomKey("user_id", userId)
    setCustomKey("screen", "checkout")
    setCustomKey("cart_items", cartSize)
    log("User tapped checkout with $cartSize items")  // breadcrumb
}

Breadcrumbs (your Alexa pattern): custom events logged before a crash to reconstruct what happened. Map directly to your 32-metric observability work.

class ObservabilityManager {
    fun track(event: String, props: Map<String, String> = emptyMap()) {
        val message = "$event: ${props.entries.joinToString()}"
        FirebaseCrashlytics.getInstance().log(message)  // appears in crash log
        analyticsService.track(event, props)            // also goes to dashboard
    }
}

KPIs to track (map to Samsara's SLOs): crash-free sessions %, ANR rate, non-fatal rate, app startup time (P50/P95), screen load time, API error rate.

Q.Structured logging and distributed tracing for mobile
L5+
+

Production observability goes beyond crash reports. Senior engineers instrument the full user journey.

Structured logging pattern:

data class LogEvent(
    val name: String,
    val timestampMs: Long = System.currentTimeMillis(),
    val sessionId: String,
    val userId: String,
    val properties: Map<String, Any>
)

class Logger {
    private val buffer = mutableListOf<LogEvent>()

    fun log(name: String, vararg props: Pair<String, Any>) {
        buffer.add(LogEvent(name, sessionId = session.id,
            userId = auth.userId, properties = props.toMap()))
        if (buffer.size >= 50) flush()  // batch send
    }

    suspend fun flush() = withContext(Dispatchers.IO) {
        api.sendLogs(buffer.toList())
        buffer.clear()
    }
}

Trace IDs: attach a traceId to each user action that flows through every log event, network call, and server response. Lets you reconstruct a complete journey across client + server in your logging dashboard.

Tools: Datadog Mobile, Elastic APM, New Relic, custom pipelines into BigQuery/ClickHouse.

Q.ANR detection and prevention
+

ANR (Application Not Responding) — triggered when main thread blocked for 5+ seconds (input), 10s (broadcast), 20s (service).

Common causes in mobile apps:

  • SharedPreferences commit() on main thread (use apply())
  • Database query on main thread (Room enforces this at compile time)
  • Thread.sleep() or network call on main thread
  • Holding a lock that another thread is waiting for
  • Heavy onDraw() or onMeasure() in a custom view

Detection:

  • StrictMode in debug: StrictMode.setThreadPolicy(StrictMode.ThreadPolicy.Builder().detectAll().penaltyLog().build())
  • Play Console Vitals → ANRs (real user data, symbolicated traces)
  • Crashlytics ANR reporting (automatic in recent SDK)

Prevention rule: only UI updates, simple calculations, and reads from memory on the main thread. Everything else → coroutine with IO or Default dispatcher.

21

Accessibility (a11y)

Senior-level differentiator. Google explicitly evaluates this. Covers semantic tree, Talkback, contrast, focus management, and testing — for both Compose and React Native.

Q.What is accessibility in Android and why do senior engineers care?
+

Accessibility (a11y) ensures your app works for users with visual, motor, auditory, or cognitive disabilities. On Android this means Talkback (screen reader), Switch Access (motor), and system-level font scaling, color inversion, etc.

Why it matters at senior level:

  • Google explicitly evaluates accessibility in its engineering bar
  • ADA compliance is a legal requirement for many US companies
  • Samsara's users are fleet drivers — many use apps under stress, gloves, poor visibility
  • Poor accessibility = poor UX for everyone (captions help in noisy environments, etc.)

WCAG 2.1 mobile targets: AA level is the baseline — minimum 4.5:1 contrast ratio for text, 44×44dp touch targets, all content reachable via screen reader.

Q.Semantic tree in Compose — how Talkback reads your UI
+

Compose maintains a parallel semantic tree alongside the UI tree. Talkback reads this tree, not the visual pixels. Every composable that renders content should contribute meaningful semantics.

// ❌ No semantics — Talkback says "unlabelled button"
Box(Modifier.clickable { delete(item) }) {
    Icon(Icons.Default.Delete, contentDescription = null)
}

// ✅ Clear semantics
IconButton(
    onClick = { delete(item) },
    modifier = Modifier.semantics {
        contentDescription = "Delete ${item.name}"
        role = Role.Button
    }
) { Icon(Icons.Default.Delete, contentDescription = null) }

// Merge semantics for complex components
Row(Modifier.semantics(mergeDescendants = true) { }) {
    Image(/* user avatar */contentDescription = null)
    Column {
        Text(user.name)   // Talkback reads "Rutul Thakkar, Online"
        Text("Online")
    }
}

mergeDescendants = true — combines child semantics into one announcement. Use for card-style items where you want one cohesive reading.

Q.Content descriptions, roles, and live regions
+
  • contentDescription — what Talkback reads for Images, Icons. Set to null for purely decorative elements (Talkback skips them).
  • Role — tells Talkback the purpose: Role.Button, Role.Checkbox, Role.Switch, Role.Tab.
  • Live regions — announce dynamic content changes without focus moving there.
// Live region — status message reads automatically when text changes
var statusMsg by remember { mutableStateOf("") }

Text(
    text = statusMsg,
    modifier = Modifier.semantics {
        liveRegion = LiveRegionMode.Polite   // reads when idle
        // LiveRegionMode.Assertive — interrupts immediately (use for errors)
    }
)

// Custom action — add non-visible actions for screen reader users
Box(Modifier.semantics {
    customActions = listOf(
        CustomAccessibilityAction("Add to favourites") { favourite(item); true }
    )
})
Q.Focus management and touch targets
+

Touch targets: WCAG requires minimum 44×44dp. Use Modifier.minimumInteractiveComponentSize() (Compose) or ensure your clickable area is large enough even if the visual is smaller.

// Small icon, large touch target
Box(Modifier
    .size(44.dp)          // touch target
    .clickable { like() }
) {
    Icon(
        Icons.Default.Favorite,
        contentDescription = "Like",
        modifier = Modifier.size(24.dp).align(Alignment.Center)
    )
}

Focus order: Talkback navigates by reading order (left-to-right, top-to-bottom). Use Modifier.focusProperties to override when visual layout differs from logical order.

val (a, b, c) = FocusRequester.createRefs()
// Force logical order: a → c → b
FirstField(Modifier.focusProperties { next = c })
ThirdField(Modifier.focusProperties { next = b })
SecondField(Modifier.focusProperties { next = a })

Keyboard navigation: Switch Access and physical keyboard users navigate with Tab/D-pad. Test with keyboard only — if you can't reach a control, it's inaccessible.

Q.Testing accessibility in your app
+

Manual testing (always required):

  1. Enable Talkback (Settings → Accessibility → Talkback)
  2. Navigate your app using swipe gestures only — double tap to activate
  3. Check every element has a meaningful announcement
  4. Check focus order makes sense
  5. Enable large font size (max) — does your layout break?
  6. Enable high contrast / colour inversion — is content still readable?

Automated testing:

// Compose semantics assertions
composeTestRule.onNode(hasContentDescription("Delete Rutul"))
    .assertIsDisplayed()
    .assertHasClickAction()

// Robolectric — check ViewCompat.getAccessibilityDelegate
val view = activity.findViewById<View>(R.id.btn_submit)
assertNotNull(ViewCompat.getAccessibilityDelegate(view))

Automated scanners: Accessibility Scanner app (Google) — overlays your screen with issues. Also: AccessibilityChecks.enable() in Espresso tests runs automated checks.

Q.Contrast, dynamic type, and colour-only information
+

Contrast ratio rules (WCAG AA):

  • Normal text (<18sp): minimum 4.5:1
  • Large text (≥18sp bold or ≥24sp): minimum 3:1
  • Non-text UI elements (icons, borders): minimum 3:1

Dynamic type: Never hardcode text sizes in sp that ignore system font scale. Compose respects system font scale by default via MaterialTheme.typography. Test at 200% font size.

// ❌ Fixed — ignores system font scale
Text(text, fontSize = 16.sp)

// ✅ Use MaterialTheme typography
Text(text, style = MaterialTheme.typography.bodyLarge)

Colour-only information: Don't convey meaning through colour alone. An error field that just turns red without an error message fails for colour-blind users. Always pair colour with text, icon, or pattern.

// ❌ Colour-only
TextField(colors = TextFieldDefaults.colors(
    focusedIndicatorColor = if (error) Color.Red else Color.Blue
))

// ✅ Colour + text + semantics
TextField(...)
if (error) Text("Required", color = Color.Red, ...)  // also set semantics error
Q.Accessibility in React Native
+

RN uses a similar semantic system with different prop names:

// Screen reader label
<TouchableOpacity
    accessible={true}
    accessibilityLabel="Delete item"
    accessibilityRole="button"
    accessibilityHint="Removes this item from your list"
    onPress={handleDelete}
>
    <Icon name="trash" />
</TouchableOpacity>

// Live region
<Text
    accessibilityLiveRegion="polite"
    importantForAccessibility="yes"
>{statusMessage}</Text>

// Group related elements
<View
    accessible={true}
    accessibilityLabel={`${user.name}, ${user.status}`}
>
    <Image source={avatar} accessibilityElementsHidden={true} />
    <Text>{user.name}</Text>
    <Text>{user.status}</Text>
</View>

accessibilityRole values: button, link, search, image, checkbox, radio, tab, header, text, adjustable (slider).

20

React Native — Deep Dive

Comprehensive coverage for Android engineers entering or interviewing for RN roles. Covers architecture, native bridging, hooks, navigation, performance, and debugging. Priority for Samsara pipeline.

Q.What IS React Native? How does it differ from a WebView wrapper?
🔥
+

React Native is a framework that lets you write UI in JavaScript/TypeScript using React, but renders actual native widgets — not a WebView. A button is a real android.widget.Button, a text input is a real EditText.

vs WebView (Cordova/Ionic): WebView renders HTML/CSS inside a browser engine. Looks like a website, scrolls like a website, performs like a website. React Native renders platform-native components — looks and feels native.

vs Flutter: Flutter draws its own widgets on a canvas (Skia). React Native delegates to platform widgets. This means React Native feels more native automatically; Flutter needs more work to match platform conventions but gives pixel-perfect control.

Key mental model: React Native is a JavaScript description of a UI that a native renderer interprets into actual platform views. The JS thread describes what should exist; the native thread makes it exist.
Q.React Native threading model — 3 threads, what each does
🔥
+

Communication (old arch): async JSON messages over the bridge. JS thread serializes → bridge → native thread deserializes. This is the bottleneck.

New arch (Fabric): JS thread holds C++ references directly via JSI. Shadow thread merged into C++ rendering. Synchronous layout possible. No serialization.

Performance rule: never block the JS thread. Heavy computation → offload to native module or use InteractionManager.runAfterInteractions(). A blocked JS thread = frozen UI.
Q.JSI, TurboModules, Fabric, Codegen — new architecture explained
🔥
+

The problem with old arch: every JS↔native call serializes to JSON string → async → deserialize. Large objects are slow. Bridge is a bottleneck. Layout is async = visual jitter.

New arch components:

  • JSI (JavaScript Interface) — C++ layer embedded in Hermes. JS can hold references to C++ host objects directly. Calls are synchronous, zero-copy. The async bridge is eliminated.
  • TurboModules — lazy-loaded native modules via JSI. Only loaded when first accessed (vs all upfront). Synchronous calls possible. Typed via TypeSpec.
  • Fabric — new C++ rendering engine replacing UIManager. Processes layout synchronously on the JS thread via JSI. Enables concurrent rendering (React 18 features).
  • Codegen — static analysis of TypeSpec files at build time. Auto-generates type-safe glue code for both JS and native sides. Catches type mismatches at compile time.
// TypeSpec defines the interface (Codegen generates the rest)
import type {TurboModule} from 'react-native';
import {TurboModuleRegistry} from 'react-native';

export interface Spec extends TurboModule {
  getBatteryLevel(): Promise<number>;
  readonly getDeviceName: () => string;  // synchronous
}

export default TurboModuleRegistry.getEnforcing<Spec>('DeviceInfo');
Q.Writing a Native Module — Kotlin side (New Architecture / TurboModule)
🔥
+
// 1. Implement the native module
class DeviceInfoModule(reactContext: ReactApplicationContext)
    : NativeDeviceInfoSpec(reactContext) {  // generated by Codegen from TypeSpec

    override fun getName() = NAME

    // Async method
    override fun getBatteryLevel(promise: Promise) {
        try {
            val bm = reactContext.getSystemService(Context.BATTERY_SERVICE) as BatteryManager
            promise.resolve(bm.getIntProperty(BatteryManager.BATTERY_PROPERTY_CAPACITY).toDouble())
        } catch (e: Exception) {
            promise.reject("BATTERY_ERR", e)
        }
    }

    // Synchronous (only safe with new arch / JSI)
    override fun getDeviceName(): String = Build.MODEL

    companion object {
        const val NAME = "DeviceInfo"
    }
}

// 2. Register
class DeviceInfoPackage : TurboReactPackage() {
    override fun getModule(name: String, ctx: ReactApplicationContext) =
        if (name == DeviceInfoModule.NAME) DeviceInfoModule(ctx) else null

    override fun getReactModuleInfoProvider() = ReactModuleInfoProvider {
        mapOf(DeviceInfoModule.NAME to ReactModuleInfo(
            DeviceInfoModule.NAME, DeviceInfoModule.NAME,
            false, false, false, BuildConfig.IS_NEW_ARCHITECTURE_ENABLED))
    }
}
// 3. Call from JavaScript
import DeviceInfo from '../specs/NativeDeviceInfo';
const battery = await DeviceInfo.getBatteryLevel();  // async
const name = DeviceInfo.getDeviceName();             // sync (new arch only)
Old arch equivalent: extend ReactContextBaseJavaModule, annotate methods with @ReactMethod. Everything async. No Codegen.
Q.Native UI Component — expose an Android View to React Native
+

When JS needs to render a custom native view (camera, map, signature pad, etc.) use a ViewManager.

// 1. Create the ViewManager (old arch style, still common)
class SignatureViewManager : SimpleViewManager<SignatureView>() {
    override fun getName() = "SignatureView"

    override fun createViewInstance(ctx: ThemedReactContext) = SignatureView(ctx)

    // Expose a prop JS can set
    @ReactProp(name = "strokeColor")
    fun setStrokeColor(view: SignatureView, color: Int) {
        view.setStrokeColor(color)
    }

    // Expose an event JS can listen to
    override fun getExportedCustomDirectEventTypeConstants() = mapOf(
        "onSignatureComplete" to mapOf("registrationName" to "onSignatureComplete")
    )
}

// 2. Fire an event back to JS from native
fun onSignatureDone(view: SignatureView, data: String) {
    val ctx = view.context as ThemedReactContext
    ctx.getJSModule(RCTEventEmitter::class.java)
        .receiveEvent(view.id, "onSignatureComplete",
            Arguments.createMap().apply { putString("data", data) })
}

// 3. Register
class MyPackage : ReactPackage {
    override fun createViewManagers(ctx: ReactApplicationContext) =
        listOf(SignatureViewManager())
    override fun createNativeModules(ctx: ReactApplicationContext) = emptyList()
}
// 4. Use in React Native
import { requireNativeComponent } from 'react-native';
const SignatureView = requireNativeComponent('SignatureView');
<SignatureView strokeColor={0xFF0000} onSignatureComplete={(e) => save(e.nativeEvent.data)} />
Q.Sending events from Android native to JavaScript (without a call from JS)
+

When native code needs to push data to JS proactively (sensor updates, Bluetooth events, push notification arrival).

// Old arch — DeviceEventEmitter
class BluetoothModule(reactContext: ReactApplicationContext)
    : ReactContextBaseJavaModule(reactContext) {

    override fun getName() = "BluetoothModule"

    // Call this when a BT device is found
    fun onDeviceFound(name: String, address: String) {
        val params = Arguments.createMap().apply {
            putString("name", name)
            putString("address", address)
        }
        reactApplicationContext
            .getJSModule(DeviceEventManagerModule.RCTDeviceEventEmitter::class.java)
            .emit("onBluetoothDeviceFound", params)
    }
}

// JS side — subscribe to the event
import { DeviceEventEmitter } from 'react-native';
useEffect(() => {
    const sub = DeviceEventEmitter.addListener('onBluetoothDeviceFound', (device) => {
        console.log(device.name, device.address);
    });
    return () => sub.remove();  // cleanup on unmount
}, []);
New arch equivalent: use EventEmitter from the TurboModule spec. JS registers via the module's event emitter interface. Same concept, type-safe.
Q.React hooks — what Android engineers need to know
🔥
+

Hooks are functions that let you use React state and lifecycle in functional components. The critical ones:

HookAndroid equivalentUse for
useStateMutableStateFlowLocal UI state
useEffectLaunchedEffectSide effects, subscriptions, data fetch on mount
useCallbackremember { lambda }Memoize a function reference (prevent re-render)
useMemoremember(deps) { ... }Memoize an expensive computed value
useRefremember { mutableStateOf } (no recompose)Mutable value without triggering re-render
useContextLocalContext.currentRead from React Context (implicit passing)
function SearchScreen() {
    const [query, setQuery] = useState('');             // local state
    const [results, setResults] = useState([]);

    useEffect(() => {
        if (!query) return;
        const timer = setTimeout(() => fetchResults(query).then(setResults), 300);
        return () => clearTimeout(timer);               // cleanup (like DisposableEffect)
    }, [query]);                                        // runs when query changes

    const handleClear = useCallback(() => {
        setQuery('');
        setResults([]);
    }, []);                                             // stable reference

    return <View>...</View>;
}
Q.Navigation in React Native — React Navigation
+

The de-facto navigation library. Three main navigator types:

  • Stack Navigator — push/pop screens. Android back button works. Like a Fragment back stack.
  • Tab Navigator — bottom tabs. Like BottomNavigationView.
  • Drawer Navigator — side drawer.
import { NavigationContainer } from '@react-navigation/native';
import { createNativeStackNavigator } from '@react-navigation/native-stack';

type RootStack = { Home: undefined; Detail: { id: string } };
const Stack = createNativeStackNavigator<RootStack>();

export default function App() {
    return (
        <NavigationContainer>
            <Stack.Navigator initialRouteName="Home">
                <Stack.Screen name="Home" component={HomeScreen} />
                <Stack.Screen name="Detail" component={DetailScreen} />
            </Stack.Navigator>
        </NavigationContainer>
    );
}

// Navigate from a screen
function HomeScreen({ navigation }) {
    return <Button title="Go" onPress={() => navigation.navigate('Detail', { id: '123' })} />;
}

// Receive params
function DetailScreen({ route }) {
    const { id } = route.params;  // typed via RootStack
}

createNativeStackNavigator vs createStackNavigator: Native stack uses actual platform navigation (Activity on Android) — better performance. JS stack animates in JS — more customizable but slower.

Q.State management in React Native — Context vs Redux vs Zustand
+
Context + useReducerRedux ToolkitZustand
BoilerplateLowMedium (RTK reduced it)Minimal
DevToolsLimitedExcellent (Redux DevTools)Good
PerformanceRe-renders all consumersSelector-based, efficientSubscription-based, efficient
Learning curveLowMediumVery low
Use forSimple/medium appsLarge apps, time-travel debugMost new projects in 2026
// Zustand — minimal store
import { create } from 'zustand';

interface UserStore {
    user: User | null;
    setUser: (u: User) => void;
    clear: () => void;
}

const useUserStore = create<UserStore>((set) => ({
    user: null,
    setUser: (user) => set({ user }),
    clear: () => set({ user: null }),
}));

// In component
const user = useUserStore((state) => state.user);        // only re-renders when user changes
const setUser = useUserStore((state) => state.setUser);  // stable reference

Android engineer analogy: Zustand ≈ StateFlow in ViewModel. Redux ≈ MVI with reducer. Context ≈ CompositionLocal.

Q.Performance optimization in React Native
🔥
+

FlatList vs ScrollView: always use FlatList for dynamic lists. ScrollView renders all children at once — OOM on large lists. FlatList virtualizes like RecyclerView.

<FlatList
    data={items}
    keyExtractor={(item) => item.id}          // stable keys — like key in Compose
    renderItem={({ item }) => <ItemRow item={item} />}
    getItemLayout={(_, index) => ({            // skip layout measurement
        length: ITEM_HEIGHT, offset: ITEM_HEIGHT * index, index
    })}
    windowSize={5}                              // render 5 screens worth
    removeClippedSubviews                       // detach off-screen (Android)
/>

Prevent unnecessary re-renders:

// React.memo — like @Stable in Compose
const ItemRow = React.memo(({ item }) => <View>...</View>);

// useCallback — stable function references
const handlePress = useCallback(() => navigate(item.id), [item.id]);

// useMemo — expensive derived values
const sorted = useMemo(() => items.sort(compareFn), [items]);

Animations: use Animated API or react-native-reanimated — run on UI thread via JSI, not JS thread. Never animate with setState in a loop.

Hermes: ensure Hermes is enabled — it pre-compiles JS to bytecode at build time, dramatically reducing JS parse time on cold start.

Q.React Native vs Native Android — when each wins
🔥
+
Use caseWinnerWhy
Camera / AR / VideoNativeFull Camera2/CameraX API access; RN wrappers are thin and lag
Bluetooth / BLENative (or with library)Complex callbacks; native module wrapping adds latency
Heavy animation (60fps gestures)Native or ReanimatedJS thread can't guarantee 60fps; Reanimated runs on UI thread
Complex lists (infinite, fast scroll)Native (RecyclerView)FlatList good but RecyclerView has more control
Business logic screens (forms, CRUD)RNFaster to build, shared with iOS, good enough perf
Settings / config screensRNSimple UI, code sharing benefit is high
Real-time data (WebSocket, live updates)Both fineRN handles this well with proper state management
Samsara's approach: React Native for driver workflow screens (forms, dashboards, settings); native for video safety feed, BLE device connectivity, camera-based features.
Q.Hermes engine — what it is and why it matters
+

Hermes is a JavaScript engine built by Meta, optimized specifically for React Native on mobile. Default since RN 0.70.

What it does differently from V8/JavaScriptCore:

  • Ahead-of-time bytecode compilation — JS is compiled to Hermes bytecode at build time (part of your APK/AAB), not at runtime. App parses bytecode on launch, not raw JS. Result: dramatically faster TTI (Time to Interactive).
  • Optimized for low memory — smaller heap, lazy parsing, designed for mobile constraints vs browser/server.
  • Better GC — shorter GC pauses than JavaScriptCore on Android.

Enable in Gradle:

// android/app/build.gradle
project.ext.react = [
    enableHermes: true  // default true in RN 0.70+
]

Practical impact: apps using Hermes see 40-50% improvement in TTI on mid-range Android devices. Critical for fleet/field apps where users have budget hardware.

Q.Metro bundler — what it does
+

Metro is React Native's JavaScript bundler (like webpack for RN). It:

  • Resolves all JS/TS imports and bundles into a single JS file
  • Transpiles JSX and modern JS to compatible versions
  • Serves the bundle during development (hot reload, Fast Refresh)
  • In release builds: produces a pre-bundled file included in the APK

Fast Refresh: Metro watches for file changes, patches only the changed module in the running app. State is preserved (unlike full reload). This is Metro's killer feature for dev speed.

# Start Metro
npx react-native start

# Bundle for release (embedded in APK)
npx react-native bundle --platform android --dev false     --entry-file index.js --bundle-output android/app/src/main/assets/index.android.bundle

For Android engineers: Metro output = the JS equivalent of your compiled bytecode. In debug, the app downloads the bundle from Metro server over local network (hence the Metro server must be running). In release, the bundle is in assets/.

Q.Debugging React Native apps
+

Main tools:

  • Flipper — desktop debugging platform. Shows network requests, layout inspector, React DevTools, logs, crash reports. Connect via USB or WiFi.
  • React DevTools (standalone) — inspect component tree, props, state in real time. Run npx react-devtools.
  • Chrome DevTools — JS debugging with breakpoints (when not using Hermes). Enable remote debugging in developer menu.
  • Hermes debugger — debugger for Hermes engine. Better performance profiling than Chrome DevTools for Hermes apps.
  • Logcat + console.log — native logs visible in Android Studio Logcat; console.log shows in Metro terminal and Flipper.

Performance profiling:

  • React Native Performance Monitor (in-app overlay: shows JS/UI FPS)
  • Android Studio Profiler for native-side (memory, CPU, network)
  • Flipper Performance plugin for JS thread CPU
Most common debug flow: Metro terminal for JS errors → Logcat for native crashes → Flipper for network + component tree → Android Profiler for memory.
Q.Testing in React Native — Jest, React Native Testing Library, Detox
+
ToolTypeAndroid equivalent
JestUnit / componentJUnit + MockK
React Native Testing LibraryComponent integrationRobolectric
DetoxE2E on real device/emulatorEspresso / UI Automator
// Jest + RNTL — component test
import { render, fireEvent, screen } from '@testing-library/react-native';

test('increments count on press', () => {
    render(<Counter />);
    fireEvent.press(screen.getByText('+'));
    expect(screen.getByText('1')).toBeTruthy();
});

// Jest — mock a native module
jest.mock('react-native', () => {
    const RN = jest.requireActual('react-native');
    RN.NativeModules.BluetoothModule = { scan: jest.fn() };
    return RN;
});

Mocking native modules is required in Jest — no native runtime in Node. Always mock native modules in test setup files.

Q.React Native — common interview questions from a native Android perspective
🔥
+

If asked about RN with your native background, here are the likely probes and clean answers:

"How would you approach adding BLE scanning to an RN app?"

Write a native module in Kotlin that wraps the Android BluetoothLeScanner API. Expose scan start/stop as @ReactMethods. Fire device-found events back to JS via DeviceEventEmitter. Wrap in a TypeScript class on the JS side for a clean API. On new arch: write a TurboModule with TypeSpec for type safety.

"What causes RN apps to feel laggy?"

JS thread blocked (heavy computation, synchronous API calls), bridge congestion (too many serialized messages), non-animated state updates in animation loops, unoptimized FlatList (no keyExtractor, no getItemLayout), Animated API running on JS thread instead of native driver (useNativeDriver: true).

"How does RN handle offline?"

Same patterns as native: persist to AsyncStorage (key-value) or SQLite via react-native-sqlite-storage or WatermelonDB. Outbox pattern for queued writes. NetInfo library for connectivity state. Redux Persist for state hydration on launch.

"What's the difference between AsyncStorage and MMKV?"

AsyncStorage is the built-in RN key-value store — async, JS-thread-only, slow for frequent reads. MMKV is a native key-value store (Meta's) with synchronous reads via JSI — 10-100× faster. Use MMKV for anything frequently read (user session, settings).
Q.TypeScript fundamentals for Android/Kotlin engineers
+

TS is Kotlin-like in many ways. Key mapping:

ConceptKotlinTypeScript
Null safetyString?string | null or string | undefined
Data classdata class User(val id: String)interface User { id: string } or type User = { id: string }
Sealed classsealed class Statetype State = Loading | Success | Error (discriminated union)
Extension funfun String.shorten()No direct equivalent; use utility functions
Coroutinesuspend funasync function returning Promise
FlowFlow<T>Observable<T> (RxJS) or async generators
// Discriminated union (Kotlin sealed class equivalent)
type ApiState<T> =
    | { status: 'loading' }
    | { status: 'success'; data: T }
    | { status: 'error'; message: string };

function render(state: ApiState<User[]>) {
    switch (state.status) {
        case 'loading': return <Spinner />;
        case 'success': return <List data={state.data} />;
        case 'error': return <Error msg={state.message} />;
    }
}  // compiler enforces exhaustiveness — like when(sealed) in Kotlin
Q.AsyncStorage vs MMKV vs SQLite vs WatermelonDB — storage in RN
+
OptionTypeSpeedUse for
AsyncStorageKey-value, asyncSlowSimple settings, small data. Built-in but deprecated in new projects.
MMKVKey-value, sync via JSIVery fastUser session, auth tokens, frequently-read preferences
react-native-sqlite-storageSQLite, asyncMediumStructured relational data
WatermelonDBSQLite + observable, lazyFast at scaleLarge datasets (1000+ records), reactive UI. Used by Nozbe, Ledger.
Realm (MongoDB)Object storeFastComplex object graphs, offline-first apps
Recommendation for Samsara-type apps: MMKV for user state + tokens, WatermelonDB for fleet/asset data (large datasets, needs offline querying), Keychain (iOS) / Keystore-backed (Android) for sensitive credentials via react-native-keychain.
Q.Styling in React Native — StyleSheet and Flexbox
+

RN has no CSS. Styling uses a JavaScript object API that maps to native layout. Layout engine is Yoga (Flexbox implementation in C++).

import { StyleSheet, View, Text } from 'react-native';

const styles = StyleSheet.create({
    container: {
        flex: 1,
        flexDirection: 'column',   // default in RN (vs row in web CSS)
        justifyContent: 'center',
        alignItems: 'center',
        backgroundColor: '#fff',
        padding: 16,
    },
    title: {
        fontSize: 24,
        fontWeight: '600',
        color: '#000',
    }
});

<View style={styles.container}>
    <Text style={styles.title}>Hello</Text>
</View>

Key differences from web CSS:

  • flexDirection defaults to column (not row)
  • All dimensions are density-independent units (like dp on Android)
  • No inheritance — styles don't cascade down the tree
  • No display: grid — Flexbox only
  • StyleSheet.create validates at dev time and optimizes style objects
Q.Bridging React Native and Native — when you need it and how to decide
🔥
+

You need to write native code when:

  • Accessing hardware not exposed by RN (BLE, NFC, specific sensors)
  • Needing synchronous execution (performance-critical paths on new arch)
  • Using a native Android SDK with no RN wrapper
  • Optimizing a bottleneck that can't be fixed in JS
  • Embedding a native view (video player, map, camera preview)

Decision flow:

Need something native?
    ↓
Does a well-maintained community library exist? (react-native-community, Expo SDK)
    ↓ Yes → Use it
    ↓ No
Do you need a View or just data/actions?
    View → ViewManager (native UI component)
    Data/Actions → NativeModule (TurboModule on new arch)
        ↓
Does it need to be synchronous?
    Yes + new arch available → TurboModule with JSI
    No → regular @ReactMethod with Promise/Callback
Samsara context: Their BLE connectivity work (from job postings) is exactly this — wrapping Android BluetoothLeScanner in a TurboModule, firing events to JS as devices are discovered, managing connection state in native. Classic native module pattern.
26

API Design & GraphQL

Senior engineers design APIs, not just consume them. REST conventions, pagination, idempotency, GraphQL with Apollo — table stakes at L5+.

Q.REST API design principles every mobile engineer should know
+

Mobile engineers consume APIs, but seniors also design them (or push back on bad design). Know the key principles.

Resource naming:

❌ /getUsers, /createUser, /deleteUser/123
✅ GET    /users
   POST   /users
   GET    /users/123
   PUT    /users/123        (full replace)
   PATCH  /users/123        (partial update)
   DELETE /users/123

// Nested resources
GET /users/123/posts         (posts for user 123)
POST /users/123/posts        (create post for user 123)
GET /users/123/posts/456     (specific post)

Versioning strategies:

  • URL path: /v1/users — most common, clear, easy to test
  • Header: Accept: application/vnd.api+json;version=1 — clean URLs but harder to debug
  • Query param: /users?version=1 — optional, often for backwards compat

Error envelope:

{
  "error": {
    "code": "USER_NOT_FOUND",      // machine-readable
    "message": "User 123 not found", // human-readable
    "details": { "userId": "123" },  // context
    "traceId": "abc-123-def"         // for support correlation
  }
}
Q.Pagination strategies — cursor vs offset vs keyset
🔥
+
StrategyRequestProsCons
Offset?page=2&limit=20Simple, random accessDuplicates/skips on insert; slow at high offset
Cursor?cursor=abc123&limit=20Stable, works with insertsNo random access; opaque to client
Keyset?after_id=456&limit=20Transparent, DB-indexedOnly forward; must have sortable key

Cursor is preferred for mobile feeds — offset pagination breaks when new items are inserted (you get duplicates on next page). Cursor is server-opaque, stable, and efficient.

// Response envelope
{
  "data": [ ... ],
  "pagination": {
    "next_cursor": "eyJ0cyI6MTcwMH0",  // base64(JSON) — opaque
    "has_more": true,
    "total_count": 1847               // optional — expensive to compute
  }
}

// Android — Paging 3 PagingSource handles cursor automatically
override suspend fun load(params: LoadParams<String>): LoadResult<String, Post> {
    val cursor = params.key  // null on first load
    val response = api.getPosts(cursor = cursor, limit = params.loadSize)
    return LoadResult.Page(
        data = response.data,
        prevKey = null,               // no backwards paging on feeds
        nextKey = response.pagination.nextCursor.takeIf { response.pagination.hasMore }
    )
}
Q.Idempotency, retry design, and API contracts for mobile
L5+
+

Idempotency keys — prevent duplicate actions when network fails and client retries:

POST /payments
Idempotency-Key: 7c3f8e2a-4b1c-4d3e-9f2a-8b6d2e1f5c8a
{ "amount": 999, "currency": "USD" }

// Server: first call → process + store key + result
// Retry with same key → return stored result, don't charge again

Retry strategies:

  • Exponential backoff: 1s → 2s → 4s → 8s → give up. Prevents thundering herd.
  • Jitter: add random delay to each backoff. Prevents all clients retrying simultaneously.
  • Retry on: 429 (rate limit), 503 (service unavailable), network timeout. Never retry 400/422 — client error, retrying won't help.
// OkHttp interceptor with exponential backoff + jitter
class RetryInterceptor(private val maxRetries: Int = 3) : Interceptor {
    override fun intercept(chain: Interceptor.Chain): Response {
        var attempt = 0
        var response: Response
        do {
            response = chain.proceed(chain.request())
            if (response.isSuccessful || attempt >= maxRetries) return response
            if (response.code !in listOf(429, 503)) return response  // don't retry
            response.close()
            val backoff = (2.0.pow(attempt) * 1000 + Random.nextLong(0, 500)).toLong()
            Thread.sleep(backoff)
            attempt++
        } while (true)
    }
}
Q.GraphQL on Android — Apollo Kotlin, normalized cache, offline
+

GraphQL lets clients request exactly what they need — no over-fetching. Used by GitHub, Shopify, Airbnb, Meta.

# Define query in .graphql file
query GetUser($id: ID!) {
    user(id: $id) {
        id
        name
        avatar { url width height }
        posts(first: 10) {
            edges { node { id title createdAt } }
        }
    }
}
// Apollo generates type-safe Kotlin classes from .graphql files
val apolloClient = ApolloClient.Builder()
    .serverUrl("https://api.example.com/graphql")
    .normalizedCache(MemoryCacheFactory(10 * 1024 * 1024)  // 10MB in-memory
        .chain(SqlNormalizedCacheFactory(context, "apollo.db")))  // SQLite disk
    .build()

// Execute query
val response = apolloClient.query(GetUserQuery(id = "123")).execute()
val user = response.data?.user  // fully typed

// Normalized cache — stores by id, not by query
// If same User appears in 2 queries, it's stored ONCE
// Update in one query → automatically updates all other queries = no stale data

vs REST tradeoffs:

  • GraphQL wins: no over-fetching, one endpoint, strong typing, normalized cache
  • REST wins: simpler caching (HTTP cache headers), easier CDN, no N+1 risk, wider tooling support
25

Internationalization & Localization

Critical for anyone who has shipped to international markets. Maps directly to your Type with Alexa 14-marketplace work. Probed at companies with global products.

Q.Internationalization fundamentals — strings, plurals, and quantity strings
🔥
+

Android i18n starts with resource qualifiers — alternate resource directories selected by locale at runtime.

res/
  values/strings.xml          ← default (English)
  values-fr/strings.xml       ← French
  values-ar/strings.xml       ← Arabic (RTL)
  values-ja/strings.xml       ← Japanese
  values-b+zh+Hans/strings.xml ← Simplified Chinese (BCP 47)

String formatting — use named args, not positional:

<!-- ❌ Positional — order is language-specific -->
<string name="greeting">Hello, %1$s! You have %2$d messages.</string>

<!-- ✅ Named placeholders via XLIFF annotation -->
<string name="greeting">Hello, <xliff:g id="name" example="Rutul">%1$s</xliff:g>! You have <xliff:g id="count">%2$d</xliff:g> messages.</string>

Plurals — quantity strings:

<plurals name="message_count">
    <item quantity="zero">No messages</item>
    <item quantity="one">%d message</item>
    <item quantity="other">%d messages</item>
    <!-- Arabic needs: zero/one/two/few/many/other -->
</plurals>

// Code
val msg = resources.getQuantityString(R.plurals.message_count, count, count)
Q.RTL support — BiDi and layout mirroring
+

Arabic, Hebrew, Farsi, Urdu are RTL (right-to-left). Android supports automatic layout mirroring — start/end instead of left/right.

<!-- ❌ Hardcoded left/right — won't mirror -->
android:paddingLeft="16dp"
android:layout_marginRight="8dp"

<!-- ✅ Start/end — automatically mirrors for RTL -->
android:paddingStart="16dp"
android:layout_marginEnd="8dp"
// Compose — uses start/end automatically
Modifier.padding(start = 16.dp, end = 8.dp)

// If an icon should mirror for RTL
Icon(
    imageVector = Icons.Default.ArrowForward,
    contentDescription = null,
    modifier = Modifier.mirror()  // automatically flips in RTL
)

Declare RTL support in manifest:

<application android:supportsRtl="true" ... />

Test RTL: Developer Options → Force RTL layout direction. Or set locale to Arabic on emulator. Check every screen — common issues: icons facing wrong direction, text truncation on wrong side, nested layouts not mirroring.

Q.Date, time, number, and currency formatting
+

Never hardcode date formats. Formats vary by locale (MM/DD/YYYY in US, DD/MM/YYYY in UK, YYYY年MM月DD日 in Japan).

// ❌ Hardcoded format
val formatted = "2024-12-25"
SimpleDateFormat("MM/DD/YYYY").format(date)

// ✅ Locale-aware formatting
val date = LocalDate.of(2024, 12, 25)
val formatted = DateTimeFormatter
    .ofLocalizedDate(FormatStyle.MEDIUM)
    .withLocale(Locale.getDefault())
    .format(date)  // "Dec 25, 2024" in US, "25 déc. 2024" in FR
// Currency — always use NumberFormat
val price = NumberFormat.getCurrencyInstance(Locale.getDefault())
    .format(amount)  // "$9.99" in US, "9,99 €" in France

// Number formatting (separators differ: 1,000.50 vs 1.000,50)
val number = NumberFormat.getNumberInstance(Locale.getDefault())
    .format(1234567.89)  // "1,234,567.89" (US) vs "1 234 567,89" (FR)

// Relative time ("2 hours ago", "in 3 days")
val relative = DateUtils.getRelativeTimeSpanString(
    timestampMs, System.currentTimeMillis(), DateUtils.MINUTE_IN_MILLIS
)
Q.Pseudo-localization — catch i18n bugs before translation
L5+
+

Pseudo-localization replaces strings with extended versions to simulate translated text without real translations. Catches layout bugs, hardcoded strings, and expansion issues before you pay for translation.

Enable in Gradle:

android {
    buildTypes {
        debug {
            pseudoLocalesEnabled = true
        }
    }
}

This generates two pseudo-locales:

  • en-XA — adds accents, extends string length (~30% longer). "Hello" → "[Ĥéļļö one two]". Tests Latin script support and expansion.
  • ar-XB — reverses text direction. Tests RTL layout support.

What to look for:

  • Text truncated (layout doesn't expand for longer strings)
  • Untranslated strings (appear without pseudo-characters — hardcoded)
  • Broken RTL layout (icons, margins wrong direction)
  • Missing plural support (only one form shows)
Do this before every major internationalization effort — it catches 80% of issues with zero translator cost.
Q.Translation workflow and String best practices
+

String best practices for translatability:

  • Don't split sentences across strings — word order differs between languages
    ❌ "Your account has " + count + " messages"
    ✅ getString(R.string.account_messages, count)
  • Don't concatenate gendered words — "the/la/le" depends on noun gender in French/Spanish
  • No implicit context — "Back" could mean "go back" or "the back of something". Add comments for translators.
  • Add translator comments with tools:ignore and XLIFF annotations
<!-- Add context for translators -->
<string name="action_back" tools:ignore="TypographyDashes">Back</string>
<!-- TRANSLATORS: This is the navigation back button, not a physical back -->

Translation tools:

  • Android Studio Translations Editor — visual editor for all locales
  • Google Play translation services — integrated in Play Console
  • Phrase, Lokalise, Crowdin — professional translation management platforms

What you shipped: Type with Alexa across 14 marketplaces / 20 locales — this is real experience. Mention locale-specific failures you fixed as concrete examples.

Q.Locale changes and configuration handling
+

When the user changes locale, Android typically recreates your Activity. Your app must handle this gracefully.

// Survive locale change without recreation (handle it yourself)
<activity android:configChanges="locale|layoutDirection" ... />

override fun onConfigurationChanged(newConfig: Configuration) {
    super.onConfigurationChanged(newConfig)
    // Update UI manually for new locale
    updateStrings()
}

Getting the current locale correctly:

// ❌ Deprecated
Locale.getDefault()  // may not match app locale after per-app language

// ✅ Respects per-app language (API 33+ / Appcompat backport)
val locale = AppCompatDelegate.getApplicationLocales().get(0)
    ?: ConfigurationCompat.getLocales(resources.configuration).get(0)
    ?: Locale.getDefault()

Testing locale changes:

// In tests — set locale explicitly
ActivityScenario.launch(MainActivity::class.java).use { scenario ->
    scenario.onActivity { activity ->
        val config = Configuration(activity.resources.configuration)
        config.setLocale(Locale.FRENCH)
        activity.createConfigurationContext(config)
    }
}
24

Modern Android APIs (2024–2026)

Platform APIs added in recent releases. Knowing these signals you track the platform. Comes up in 'what recent Android changes have you used?' questions.

Q.Predictive back gesture (API 33+) — what it is and how to support it
+

Predictive back lets users preview the destination before completing the back gesture — they see the previous screen peeking behind the current one. Standard gesture UX in Android 13+, fully animating in Android 14+.

Opt in (required):

<!-- AndroidManifest.xml -->
<application android:enableOnBackInvokedCallback="true" ... />

Handle back in Compose (Navigation):

// Navigation Compose handles it automatically when opted in
// For custom back handling:
BackHandler(enabled = showDialog) {
    showDialog = false  // intercept back before navigation
}

Custom back animation (API 34+):

// Register OnBackPressedCallback for custom transitions
val callback = object : OnBackPressedCallback(true) {
    override fun handleOnBackStarted(backEvent: BackEventCompat) {
        // Preview animation — user is swiping, not committed
    }
    override fun handleOnBackProgressed(backEvent: BackEventCompat) {
        // Update animation progress (0.0 → 1.0) based on swipe distance
        animationProgress = backEvent.progress
    }
    override fun handleOnBackPressed() {
        // User committed — complete the animation
        finish()
    }
    override fun handleOnBackCancelled() {
        // User cancelled — snap back to original position
        animationProgress = 0f
    }
}
What breaks without it: if you intercept back with onBackPressed() override (deprecated), predictive back won't work — users can't preview.
Q.Foreground Service types — API 34 required typing
+

Android 14 (API 34) requires you to declare the type of foreground service work. Prevents abuse of foreground services for background tasks.

TypeUse forPermission required
cameraCamera access while backgroundedCAMERA
connectedDeviceBluetooth, USB, NFC while backgroundedVaries
dataSyncUpload/download, backupNone (but limited)
healthFitness tracking, heart rateBody sensors
locationGPS / location trackingACCESS_BACKGROUND_LOCATION
mediaPlaybackMusic, podcast, audioNone
mediaProjectionScreen recording, castingFOREGROUND_SERVICE_MEDIA_PROJECTION
microphoneAudio recording while backgroundedRECORD_AUDIO
phoneCallActive phone/VoIP callsMANAGE_OWN_CALLS
remoteMessagingChat messaging apps (API 35)None
shortServiceShort tasks max 3 minutes (API 34)None
specialUseTypes not listed aboveSpecial permission + Play Store approval
systemExemptedSystem apps onlyN/A
<!-- Manifest -->
<service android:name=".MusicService"
    android:foregroundServiceType="mediaPlayback" />

// Code
ServiceCompat.startForeground(this, NOTIF_ID, notification,
    ServiceInfo.FOREGROUND_SERVICE_TYPE_MEDIA_PLAYBACK)
Q.Notification permission (API 33+) and notification channels
+

Android 13 (API 33) added a runtime permission for posting notifications: POST_NOTIFICATIONS. Apps targeting API 33+ must request it or notifications are silently dropped.

// Request permission
val launcher = rememberLauncherForActivityResult(
    ActivityResultContracts.RequestPermission()
) { granted ->
    if (granted) scheduleReminder()
    else showRationale()
}

Button(onClick = {
    launcher.launch(Manifest.permission.POST_NOTIFICATIONS)
}) { Text("Enable reminders") }

// Check before showing permission rationale
if (ActivityCompat.shouldShowRequestPermissionRationale(
        activity, Manifest.permission.POST_NOTIFICATIONS)) {
    // User denied once — show why you need it
}

Notification channels (API 26+ — always required):

// Create channels once (usually in Application.onCreate)
val channel = NotificationChannel(
    "reminders",
    "Reminders",
    NotificationManager.IMPORTANCE_DEFAULT
).apply {
    description = "Task reminder notifications"
    enableVibration(true)
    setShowBadge(true)
}
(getSystemService(NOTIFICATION_SERVICE) as NotificationManager)
    .createNotificationChannel(channel)

Importance levels: URGENT (makes sound, shows heads-up) → HIGH (makes sound) → DEFAULT (no heads-up) → LOW (no sound) → MIN (no visual interruption). Users can override per channel in Settings.

Q.Play Integrity API — replacing SafetyNet
L5+
+

SafetyNet Attestation is deprecated (removed Dec 2024). Play Integrity API is the replacement for verifying device integrity, app integrity, and licensing.

What it checks:

  • Device integrity — is this a genuine Android device passing hardware attestation?
  • App integrity — is this app the real, unmodified version from Play Store?
  • Account details — has the user recently completed CAPTCHA or account verification?
// Client side — request integrity token
val integrityManager = IntegrityManagerFactory.create(context)
val request = IntegrityTokenRequest.newBuilder()
    .setNonce(generateNonce())  // base64 nonce from YOUR server
    .build()

integrityManager.requestIntegrityToken(request)
    .addOnSuccessListener { response ->
        val token = response.token()
        // Send token to YOUR backend for verification
        api.verifyIntegrity(token)
    }
// Server side verifies with Google
// POST https://playintegrity.googleapis.com/v1/{package}:decodeIntegrityToken
// Response contains verdicts:
// MEETS_DEVICE_INTEGRITY | MEETS_BASIC_INTEGRITY | MEETS_STRONG_INTEGRITY
Don't block on verdict locally — the token must be verified server-side. Client-side verdict checking is trivially bypassed. The nonce from your server prevents replay attacks.
Q.App Startup library — faster initialization
+

Problem: many libraries use ContentProvider for auto-initialization. Each ContentProvider adds ~2ms+ to cold start. An app with 10+ libraries can lose 20-50ms at startup before your code even runs.

App Startup library consolidates all initializers into ONE ContentProvider:

// 1. Implement Initializer for each library
class TimberInitializer : Initializer<Unit> {
    override fun create(context: Context) {
        if (BuildConfig.DEBUG) Timber.plant(Timber.DebugTree())
    }
    override fun dependencies() = emptyList<Class<out Initializer<*>>>()
}

class AnalyticsInitializer : Initializer<AnalyticsClient> {
    override fun create(context: Context) = AnalyticsClient.init(context)
    // Depends on Timber being initialized first
    override fun dependencies() = listOf(TimberInitializer::class.java)
}

// 2. Declare in manifest (single ContentProvider handles all)
<provider android:name="androidx.startup.InitializationProvider"
    android:authorities="${applicationId}.androidx-startup">
    <meta-data android:name="com.example.AnalyticsInitializer"
        android:value="androidx.startup" />
    <!-- TimberInitializer added automatically via dependency -->
</provider>

// 3. Lazy init — only when first accessed
val client = AppInitializer.getInstance(context)
    .initializeComponent(AnalyticsInitializer::class.java)
Q.Per-app language and Photo Picker — modern UX APIs
+

Per-app language (API 33+): users can set a different language for your app independent of system language. Useful for multilingual users.

// Set language for your app (user's choice)
AppCompatDelegate.setApplicationLocales(
    LocaleListCompat.forLanguageTags("fr")  // French for this app only
)

// Get current app locale
val currentLocale = AppCompatDelegate.getApplicationLocales()
    .get(0)?.toLanguageTag()  // "fr", "en", etc.

Photo Picker (API 33+ / backported via Jetpack): system-provided photo picker — user grants access to specific photos, NOT all media. No READ_MEDIA_IMAGES permission needed.

// Pick a single photo
val launcher = rememberLauncherForActivityResult(
    ActivityResultContracts.PickVisualMedia()
) { uri ->
    uri?.let { processImage(it) }
}

Button(onClick = {
    launcher.launch(
        PickVisualMediaRequest(ActivityResultContracts.PickVisualMedia.ImageOnly)
    )
}) { Text("Pick photo") }

// Pick multiple
val multiLauncher = rememberLauncherForActivityResult(
    ActivityResultContracts.PickMultipleVisualMedia(maxItems = 5)
) { uris -> processImages(uris) }
Photo Picker vs READ_MEDIA_IMAGES: always prefer Photo Picker — users trust it more (system UI), you get less permission, and the backport works down to API 21.
Q.Health Connect and platform privacy APIs
+

Health Connect — centralized health data platform (steps, heart rate, sleep, nutrition). All health apps read/write to a shared store with granular user consent per data type per app.

val client = HealthConnectClient.getOrCreate(context)

// Request permissions
val permissions = setOf(
    HealthPermission.getReadPermission(StepsRecord::class),
    HealthPermission.getWritePermission(HeartRateRecord::class)
)

// Check and request
val granted = client.permissionController.getGrantedPermissions()
if (permissions.intersect(granted).size != permissions.size) {
    requestPermissions.launch(permissions)
}

// Read steps
val response = client.readRecords(ReadRecordsRequest(
    recordType = StepsRecord::class,
    timeRangeFilter = TimeRangeFilter.between(startTime, endTime)
))
val totalSteps = response.records.sumOf { it.count }

Other platform privacy APIs to know:

  • Privacy Dashboard (API 31) — system shows users which apps used camera/mic/location and when
  • Approximate location (API 31) — users can grant coarse-only when you request fine
  • Microphone/camera indicators (API 31) — status bar dot when in use
  • Data access auditing — AppOpsManager.setOnOpNotedCallback lets you audit your own sensitive data access
