Advanced Compose

Compose internals, custom layouts, performance tooling, and interoperability. L5+ differentiators asked at Google and design-forward companies.

Q.Compose snapshot system — how state actually works under the hood
L5+
+

Compose uses a snapshot system (from the androidx.compose.runtime.snapshots package) to track state reads and writes. This is what makes recomposition automatic and efficient.

How it works:

  1. Every mutableStateOf value lives in a Snapshot — an isolated view of state at a point in time
  2. During composition, Compose runs in a read observer — any state read is recorded as a dependency of the composable
  3. When a state value changes, all recorded readers are invalidated and scheduled for recomposition
  4. Structural sharing: unchanged state between snapshots is shared, not copied
// What mutableStateOf actually creates
val state: MutableState<Int> = mutableStateOf(0)
// Internally: a StateObject in the current Snapshot
// Writing to it notifies all Snapshot observers (= Compose recomposition system)

// Snapshot.takeSnapshot() — read current state atomically
val snap = Snapshot.takeSnapshot()
snap.enter { println(state.value) }  // reads isolated view
snap.dispose()

Why this matters for interviews: it explains WHY derivedStateOf works, WHY you can read state from multiple threads safely, and WHY snapshotFlow gives you a Flow from state changes — all build on this system.

Q.Compose compiler — stability, skippability, and strong skipping mode
🔥
+

The Compose compiler decides at build time which composables can be skipped (not recomposed when their parents recompose) based on whether their parameters are stable.

Stable = the compiler can know if a value changed:

  • Primitive types, String, lambdas — always stable
  • data class with only stable fields + compiler plugin — stable
  • List<T>, Map<K,V> — UNSTABLE (mutable, compiler can't tell)
  • Classes annotated @Stable or @Immutable — stable

Inspect with Compose Compiler Metrics:

// build.gradle.kts
composeCompiler {
    metricsDestination = layout.buildDirectory.dir("compose_metrics")
    reportsDestination = layout.buildDirectory.dir("compose_reports")
}
// Run: ./gradlew assembleRelease
// Check: build/compose_metrics/app_release-composables.csv
// Output tells you:
// restartable skippable — ideal (can skip if params unchanged)
// restartable              — will always recompose with parent
// restartable skippable unstable params — unstable params block skipping

// Fix unstable List:
@Immutable
data class UserList(val items: List<User>)  // wrap in stable class
// OR use kotlinx.collections.immutable.ImmutableList

Strong Skipping Mode (Compose 1.6+): makes all composables skippable by default, even with unstable params — uses equality check instead of stability for lambdas. Enable in composeCompiler block.

Q.Custom Layout in Compose — Layout composable
L5+
+

When built-in layouts (Row, Column, Box) don't fit, write a custom layout using the Layout composable.

@Composable
fun HorizontalGrid(
    columns: Int,
    modifier: Modifier = Modifier,
    content: @Composable () -> Unit
) {
    Layout(content = content, modifier = modifier) { measurables, constraints ->
        val colWidth = constraints.maxWidth / columns
        val itemConstraints = constraints.copy(maxWidth = colWidth)

        // Step 1: Measure all children
        val placeables = measurables.map { it.measure(itemConstraints) }

        // Step 2: Calculate layout size
        val rows = (placeables.size + columns - 1) / columns
        val height = placeables.chunked(columns).sumOf { row -> row.maxOf { it.height } }

        // Step 3: Place children
        layout(constraints.maxWidth, height) {
            var x = 0; var y = 0
            placeables.forEachIndexed { index, placeable ->
                if (index % columns == 0 && index != 0) { x = 0; y += placeable.height }
                placeable.placeRelative(x, y)
                x += colWidth
            }
        }
    }
}

3-step rule: every Layout must (1) measure children, (2) determine its own size, (3) place children. Children can only be measured ONCE (except with SubcomposeLayout).

Q.SubcomposeLayout — measure content before it's composed
L5+
+

Use SubcomposeLayout when you need to measure one piece of content to determine constraints for another — e.g., measure a trailing icon to know how wide the main text area should be.

@Composable
fun TextWithMeasuredSuffix(
    text: String,
    suffix: @Composable () -> Unit,
    modifier: Modifier = Modifier
) {
    SubcomposeLayout(modifier) { constraints ->
        // Step 1: Measure suffix first (even though it appears after text)
        val suffixPlaceables = subcompose("suffix", suffix)
            .map { it.measure(constraints) }
        val suffixWidth = suffixPlaceables.maxOfOrNull { it.width } ?: 0

        // Step 2: Measure text with remaining width
        val textConstraints = constraints.copy(maxWidth = constraints.maxWidth - suffixWidth)
        val textPlaceables = subcompose("text") { Text(text) }
            .map { it.measure(textConstraints) }

        val height = maxOf(
            textPlaceables.maxOfOrNull { it.height } ?: 0,
            suffixPlaceables.maxOfOrNull { it.height } ?: 0
        )

        layout(constraints.maxWidth, height) {
            textPlaceables.forEach { it.placeRelative(0, 0) }
            suffixPlaceables.forEach { it.placeRelative(constraints.maxWidth - suffixWidth, 0) }
        }
    }
}

Real-world use: BoxWithConstraints and LazyColumn are both implemented with SubcomposeLayout internally. Use it when composition depends on measured size of other composables.

Q.Compose performance tooling — Layout Inspector, Macrobenchmark, baseline profiles
+

Layout Inspector (Android Studio):

  • Live Composable tree inspection
  • Recomposition count per composable — highlighted in red when excessive
  • Shows which composables are skipped vs recomposed
  • Enable: View → Tool Windows → Layout Inspector → enable "Show Recomposition Counts"

Macrobenchmark for Compose:

@RunWith(AndroidJUnit4::class)
class ScrollBenchmark {
    @get:Rule val rule = MacrobenchmarkRule()

    @Test
    fun scrollFeed() = rule.measureRepeated(
        packageName = "com.example.app",
        metrics = listOf(FrameTimingMetric()),
        iterations = 5,
        startupMode = StartupMode.WARM
    ) {
        pressHome(); startActivityAndWait()
        // Scroll the list
        device.findObject(By.res("feed_list")).also { list ->
            repeat(5) { list.fling(Direction.DOWN) }
        }
    }
}

Baseline Profiles for Compose:

@RunWith(AndroidJUnit4::class)
class StartupProfile {
    @get:Rule val rule = BaselineProfileRule()

    @Test
    fun startup() = rule.collect("com.example.app") {
        pressHome(); startActivityAndWait()
        // Interact with key flows to warm code paths
    }
}
// Generates baseline-prof.txt — ship in AAB to pre-compile Compose hot paths
Compose cold start win: Compose-heavy apps see 20-40% startup improvement with Baseline Profiles because Compose's lambda-heavy code is expensive to JIT-compile on first run.
Q.Modifier internals — how modifiers chain and compose
L5+
+

Every Modifier is an immutable linked list of Modifier.Element nodes. When you chain modifiers with then() (the . operator), you're building that list.

// This:
Modifier.padding(16.dp).background(Red).clickable { }
// Is equivalent to:
Modifier
    .then(PaddingModifier(16.dp))
    .then(BackgroundModifier(Red))
    .then(ClickableModifier { })

Modifier types (internally):

  • LayoutModifier — affects measurement/placement (padding, size, offset)
  • DrawModifier — paints on canvas (background, border, shadow)
  • PointerInputModifier — handles touch (clickable, draggable)
  • ComposedModifier — factory that creates a modifier using composition context (allows remember/state inside)
  • GraphicsLayerModifier — draws to off-screen layer (alpha, scale, rotation, translationX)

Why order matters: the list is processed left-to-right. Modifier.padding().background() → padding wraps outside background. Modifier.background().padding() → background fills full area, content inset. Each modifier wraps the subsequent content.

Performance tip: Modifier.graphicsLayer { } uses the lambda form — the lambda runs in the Drawing phase, so reading animated state there skips Composition and Layout phases entirely.
Q.Adaptive layouts — WindowSizeClass, foldables, large screens
+

Android runs on phones, tablets, foldables, ChromeOS. WindowSizeClass is the standard API for adaptive layouts.

class MainActivity : ComponentActivity() {
    override fun onCreate(savedInstanceState: Bundle?) {
        super.onCreate(savedInstanceState)
        setContent {
            val windowSizeClass = calculateWindowSizeClass(this)
            MyApp(windowSizeClass)
        }
    }
}

@Composable
fun MyApp(windowSize: WindowSizeClass) {
    when (windowSize.widthSizeClass) {
        WindowWidthSizeClass.Compact -> {
            // Phone — single pane, bottom nav
            SinglePaneLayout()
        }
        WindowWidthSizeClass.Medium -> {
            // Foldable unfolded or small tablet — nav rail
            MediumLayout()
        }
        WindowWidthSizeClass.Expanded -> {
            // Tablet / landscape — two-pane, side nav
            TwoPaneLayout()
        }
    }
}

Foldable-specific:

val foldingFeature = WindowInfoTracker.getOrCreate(context)
    .windowLayoutInfo(activity)
    .map { it.displayFeatures.filterIsInstance<FoldingFeature>().firstOrNull() }
// FoldingFeature.state = FLAT (open) or HALF_OPENED (tent mode)

Key adaptive guidelines: don't put important content in the hinge area; support drag-and-drop on large screens; use NavigationSuiteScaffold (Material3) which auto-switches between bottom bar, nav rail, and nav drawer based on window size.

Q.Compose interoperability — embedding Compose in XML and XML in Compose
+

Compose in XML (adding Compose to legacy Views):

<!-- In your XML layout -->
<androidx.compose.ui.platform.ComposeView
    android:id="@+id/compose_view"
    android:layout_width="match_parent"
    android:layout_height="wrap_content" />
// In Fragment/Activity
binding.composeView.apply {
    setViewCompositionStrategy(ViewCompositionStrategy.DisposeOnViewTreeLifecycleDestroyed)
    setContent {
        MaterialTheme { UserCard(user = viewModel.user) }
    }
}

XML Views in Compose (using legacy views that have no Compose equivalent):

@Composable
fun MapView(modifier: Modifier = Modifier) {
    AndroidView(
        factory = { context ->
            // Creates the View once
            com.google.android.gms.maps.MapView(context).apply {
                onCreate(null); onResume()
            }
        },
        update = { mapView ->
            // Called on recomposition — update the view
            mapView.getMapAsync { map -> /* configure */ }
        },
        modifier = modifier
    )
}
Migration strategy: start with leaf components (buttons, cards) → migrate screen by screen → keep legacy screens in XML until team is Compose-confident. ComposeView is the bridge for gradual migration.

Key Libraries & SDKs

Exponential backoff, Firebase suite, analytics ecosystem, charts, Google Maps SDK, Google Pay, Stripe, ML Kit, In-App Purchases, and the 2026 industry-standard stack.

Q.Exponential backoff with jitter — the definitive implementation
🔥
+

Exponential backoff prevents overloading a struggling server. Jitter prevents all clients retrying simultaneously (thundering herd).

// Full implementation with jitter
suspend fun <T> retryWithBackoff(
    maxAttempts: Int = 5,
    initialDelay: Long = 1_000L,    // 1 second
    maxDelay: Long = 30_000L,       // 30 seconds cap
    factor: Double = 2.0,
    block: suspend () -> T
): T {
    var currentDelay = initialDelay
    repeat(maxAttempts - 1) { attempt ->
        try { return block() }
        catch (e: Exception) {
            if (!e.isRetryable()) throw e  // don't retry 400, auth errors, etc.
        }
        // Jitter: randomize between 50%-100% of computed delay
        val jitter = (currentDelay * 0.5 * Random.nextDouble()).toLong()
        val sleepTime = (currentDelay + jitter).coerceAtMost(maxDelay)
        delay(sleepTime)
        currentDelay = (currentDelay * factor).toLong().coerceAtMost(maxDelay)
    }
    return block()  // final attempt — let exception propagate
}

fun Exception.isRetryable() = when (this) {
    is IOException -> true         // network errors
    is HttpException -> code() in listOf(429, 500, 502, 503, 504)
    else -> false
}

// Usage
val data = retryWithBackoff { api.fetchData() }

Retry delays with default settings: 1s → 2.5s → 6.25s → 15.6s → give up.

WorkManager has built-in exponential backoff: setBackoffCriteria(BackoffPolicy.EXPONENTIAL, 10, TimeUnit.SECONDS). Use that for background work; the above for foreground/coroutine retries.

Q.Google Pay — integration and payment flow
+

Google Pay uses tokenized card details — the merchant never sees the real card number. Android provides the Pay API via Play Services.

// 1. Check if Google Pay is available
val paymentsClient = Wallet.getPaymentsClient(activity,
    Wallet.WalletOptions.Builder()
        .setEnvironment(WalletConstants.ENVIRONMENT_PRODUCTION).build())

val isReadyRequest = IsReadyToPayRequest.fromJson(isReadyToPayJson)
paymentsClient.isReadyToPay(isReadyRequest)
    .addOnSuccessListener { available -> showGooglePayButton(available) }

// 2. Build payment request
val paymentDataRequest = PaymentDataRequest.fromJson(paymentJson)

// 3. Launch Google Pay sheet
AutoResolveHelper.resolveTask(
    paymentsClient.loadPaymentData(paymentDataRequest),
    activity, LOAD_PAYMENT_DATA_REQUEST_CODE
)

// 4. Handle result in onActivityResult
override fun onActivityResult(requestCode: Int, resultCode: Int, data: Intent?) {
    if (requestCode == LOAD_PAYMENT_DATA_REQUEST_CODE && resultCode == RESULT_OK) {
        val paymentData = PaymentData.getFromIntent(data!!)
        val token = paymentData?.paymentMethodToken?.token  // send to your payment processor
    }
}
The token is a payment method token — send it to your backend which passes it to Stripe/Braintree/Adyen for actual charge processing. Google Pay itself doesn't charge — it just provides the token.
Q.Stripe Android SDK — payment integration
+

Stripe's Android SDK provides a pre-built Payment Sheet that handles card entry, 3D Secure, Apple/Google Pay in one composable UI.

// 1. Setup
implementation("com.stripe:stripe-android:20.x.x")

// 2. Get PaymentIntent from YOUR backend first
// POST /create-payment-intent → { clientSecret: "pi_xxx_secret_xxx" }

// 3. Initialize PaymentSheet
val paymentSheet = PaymentSheet(this) { result ->
    when (result) {
        is PaymentSheetResult.Completed -> showSuccess()
        is PaymentSheetResult.Canceled -> showCanceled()
        is PaymentSheetResult.Failed -> showError(result.error)
    }
}

// 4. Present (shows Stripe's ready-made card UI)
paymentSheet.presentWithPaymentIntent(
    clientSecret,
    PaymentSheet.Configuration(
        merchantDisplayName = "My App",
        googlePay = PaymentSheet.GooglePayConfiguration(
            environment = PaymentSheet.GooglePayConfiguration.Environment.Production,
            countryCode = "US"
        )
    )
)

Never process payments client-side. Always: client → YOUR backend → Stripe API → confirmation back to client. Your backend holds the Stripe secret key; the Android app only has the publishable key.

Q.In-App Purchases (IAP) — Google Play Billing Library
+

For selling subscriptions or one-time purchases. Play Billing is required for Android apps on Play Store — no external payment links for digital goods (Google's policy).

implementation("com.android.billingclient:billing-ktx:7.x.x")

// 1. Connect
val billingClient = BillingClient.newBuilder(context)
    .setListener { billingResult, purchases ->
        if (billingResult.responseCode == BillingClient.BillingResponseCode.OK) {
            purchases?.forEach { handlePurchase(it) }
        }
    }
    .enablePendingPurchases()
    .build()

billingClient.startConnection(object : BillingClientStateListener {
    override fun onBillingSetupFinished(result: BillingResult) { queryProducts() }
    override fun onBillingServiceDisconnected() { /* retry */ }
})

// 2. Query products
val params = QueryProductDetailsParams.newBuilder()
    .setProductList(listOf(
        QueryProductDetailsParams.Product.newBuilder()
            .setProductId("premium_monthly")
            .setProductType(BillingClient.ProductType.SUBS)
            .build()
    )).build()
billingClient.queryProductDetailsAsync(params) { _, productDetailsList ->
    showSubscriptionOptions(productDetailsList)
}

// 3. Purchase
val billingFlowParams = BillingFlowParams.newBuilder()
    .setProductDetailsParamsList(listOf(
        BillingFlowParams.ProductDetailsParams.newBuilder()
            .setProductDetails(productDetails)
            .setOfferToken(selectedOffer.offerToken)
            .build()
    )).build()
billingClient.launchBillingFlow(activity, billingFlowParams)

// 4. Verify on YOUR backend + acknowledge
suspend fun handlePurchase(purchase: Purchase) {
    if (purchase.purchaseState == Purchase.PurchaseState.PURCHASED) {
        backend.verifyPurchase(purchase.purchaseToken)  // ALWAYS verify server-side
        val ackParams = AcknowledgePurchaseParams.newBuilder()
            .setPurchaseToken(purchase.purchaseToken).build()
        billingClient.acknowledgePurchase(ackParams)  // must ack within 3 days
    }
}

27

Authentication & Authorization

OAuth 2.0, OIDC, JWT, Google Sign-In, Firebase Auth, Facebook Login, SSO, token storage, and authorization patterns. Critical for any app with user accounts.

Q.OAuth 2.0 — what it is and the 4 grant types
🔥
+

OAuth 2.0 is an authorization framework — it lets a user grant a third-party app access to their resources without sharing their password. It issues tokens, not credentials.

Key roles:

  • Resource Owner — the user
  • Client — your app
  • Authorization Server — issues tokens (Google, Facebook, your backend)
  • Resource Server — the API being accessed

4 grant types:

GrantUse forGets
Authorization Code + PKCEMobile / web apps (most common)Auth code → tokens via back-channel
Client CredentialsServer-to-server (no user)Access token directly
Device CodeSmart TV, CLI, limited inputUser authorizes on another device
ImplicitDeprecated. Never use for mobile.Token in URL fragment — insecure
Mobile always uses Authorization Code + PKCE. PKCE (Proof Key for Code Exchange) prevents auth code interception attacks — critical because mobile apps can't securely store a client secret.
Q.OAuth 2.0 Authorization Code + PKCE flow — step by step
🔥
+
  1. App generates code verifier — random 43-128 char string. Stored locally. Never sent over network.
  2. App computes code challenge — SHA-256 hash of verifier, base64url-encoded.
  3. App opens browser/Custom Tab — redirects to auth server with: client_id, redirect_uri, scope, state, code_challenge, code_challenge_method=S256
  4. User authenticates in browser (not in-app WebView — never use WebView for OAuth)
  5. Auth server redirects back to your app via deep link/custom scheme with: code, state
  6. App exchanges code for tokens — POST to token endpoint with: code, code_verifier, redirect_uri. Server verifies SHA-256(code_verifier) == code_challenge.
  7. App receives tokens — access token (short-lived) + refresh token (long-lived)
// Android AppAuth library handles this
val serviceConfig = AuthorizationServiceConfiguration(
    Uri.parse("https://auth.example.com/authorize"),
    Uri.parse("https://auth.example.com/token")
)
val codeVerifier = CodeVerifierUtil.generateRandomCodeVerifier()
val codeChallenge = CodeVerifierUtil.deriveCodeVerifierChallenge(codeVerifier)

val request = AuthorizationRequest.Builder(
    serviceConfig, CLIENT_ID, ResponseTypeValues.CODE,
    Uri.parse("com.example.app://oauth/callback")
).setScope("openid profile email")
 .setCodeVerifier(codeVerifier, codeChallenge, "S256")
 .build()

authService.performAuthorizationRequest(request, pendingIntent)
Q.OpenID Connect (OIDC) — authentication on top of OAuth 2.0
🔥
+

OAuth 2.0 is for authorization (can this app access your files?). OpenID Connect adds authentication (who IS this user?) on top of OAuth 2.0.

What OIDC adds:

  • ID Token — a JWT containing user identity claims (sub, email, name, picture)
  • UserInfo endpoint — fetch additional profile claims
  • Standardized scopes — openid (required), profile, email, phone, address
  • Standard claims in ID token: sub (unique user ID), iss (issuer), aud (audience = your client_id), exp (expiry)

Flow: same as OAuth Authorization Code + PKCE, but add openid to scope. Auth server returns both access token AND ID token.

// Parse ID token (JWT) to get user info
fun parseIdToken(idToken: String): UserInfo {
    val parts = idToken.split(".")
    val payload = String(Base64.decode(parts[1], Base64.URL_SAFE or Base64.NO_PADDING))
    val claims = JSONObject(payload)
    return UserInfo(
        id = claims.getString("sub"),
        email = claims.optString("email"),
        name = claims.optString("name"),
        picture = claims.optString("picture")
    )
    // Always verify signature and expiry on YOUR SERVER — never trust client-side verification alone
}
Google Sign-In, Sign in with Apple, Facebook Login are all OIDC implementations. When you integrate them you're using OIDC whether you know it or not.
Q.JWT — structure, validation, access vs refresh tokens
🔥
+

JSON Web Token — a self-contained signed token. Three base64url-encoded parts separated by dots: Header.Payload.Signature

// Header
{ "alg": "RS256", "typ": "JWT" }

// Payload (claims)
{
  "sub": "user_123",          // subject — unique user ID
  "iss": "https://auth.example.com",  // issuer
  "aud": "my-android-app",    // audience — must match your client_id
  "exp": 1700000000,          // expiry (Unix timestamp) — ALWAYS check this
  "iat": 1699996400,          // issued at
  "email": "r@example.com",
  "roles": ["user", "premium"]
}

// Signature — HMAC-SHA256 or RSA sign of header + payload

Access token vs refresh token:

Access TokenRefresh Token
LifetimeShort (5min–1hr)Long (days–months)
Sent toEvery API requestAuth server only (to get new access token)
If compromisedLow risk (expires soon)High risk (invalidate immediately)
StorageMemory (or secure store)Encrypted secure storage only
Never store tokens in SharedPreferences or logs. Use Android Keystore-backed EncryptedSharedPreferences or a Keystore-encrypted Room field. Access tokens can live in memory; refresh tokens must be persisted securely.
Q.Token refresh strategy — silent refresh and interceptor pattern
🔥
+

Access tokens expire. Your app must silently refresh them without logging the user out.

// Authenticator — OkHttp's built-in retry on 401
class TokenAuthenticator(
    private val tokenRepo: TokenRepository,
    private val authApi: AuthApi
) : Authenticator {

    override fun authenticate(route: Route?, response: Response): Request? {
        if (response.request.header("Authorization") == null) return null

        // Prevent concurrent refresh loops
        return synchronized(this) {
            val currentToken = tokenRepo.getAccessToken()
            // If another thread already refreshed, just retry with new token
            if (currentToken != response.request.header("Authorization")?.removePrefix("Bearer ")) {
                return response.request.newBuilder()
                    .header("Authorization", "Bearer $currentToken")
                    .build()
            }
            // Refresh
            val refreshToken = tokenRepo.getRefreshToken() ?: return null
            try {
                val newTokens = runBlocking { authApi.refresh(refreshToken) }
                tokenRepo.saveTokens(newTokens)
                response.request.newBuilder()
                    .header("Authorization", "Bearer ${newTokens.accessToken}")
                    .build()
            } catch (e: Exception) {
                tokenRepo.clear()  // refresh failed — force re-login
                null
            }
        }
    }
}

// Register with OkHttp
OkHttpClient.Builder().authenticator(TokenAuthenticator(repo, api))
Proactive refresh: also check token expiry before requests — if <60s to expiry, refresh first. Prevents most 401s rather than reacting to them.
Q.Google Sign-In / One Tap — implementation
🔥
+

Two modern Google Sign-In approaches in 2024+:

1. Credential Manager (recommended, API 28+):

// build.gradle
implementation("androidx.credentials:credentials:1.3.0")
implementation("androidx.credentials:credentials-play-services-auth:1.3.0")
implementation("com.google.android.libraries.identity.googleid:googleid:1.1.1")

// Request Google Sign-In
val googleIdOption = GetGoogleIdOption.Builder()
    .setFilterByAuthorizedAccounts(false)  // show all accounts, not just previously signed in
    .setServerClientId(WEB_CLIENT_ID)       // from Google Cloud Console
    .setAutoSelectEnabled(true)             // One Tap auto-selects if one account
    .build()

val request = GetCredentialRequest.Builder()
    .addCredentialOption(googleIdOption)
    .build()

val credManager = CredentialManager.create(context)
try {
    val result = credManager.getCredential(context, request)
    val googleCred = result.credential as GoogleIdTokenCredential
    val idToken = googleCred.idToken  // send THIS to your backend for verification
    val email = googleCred.id
    val displayName = googleCred.displayName
} catch (e: GetCredentialException) {
    // No credentials or user cancelled
}
What to do with the ID token: send it to YOUR backend. Backend verifies it with Google's public keys (or using Firebase Admin SDK / Google Auth Library). Never trust the client's claim about who they are — always verify on the server.
Q.Firebase Authentication — umbrella auth with multiple providers
+

Firebase Auth manages user sessions across all providers (Google, Facebook, Apple, email, phone) with a unified API. Tokens refresh automatically.

// Google Sign-In via Firebase
val auth = FirebaseAuth.getInstance()

// After getting googleIdToken from Credential Manager:
val credential = GoogleAuthProvider.getCredential(googleIdToken, null)
auth.signInWithCredential(credential)
    .addOnSuccessListener { result ->
        val user = result.user!!
        val uid = user.uid           // Firebase UID — stable across providers
        val email = user.email
        val idToken = user.getIdToken(false)  // Firebase ID token — send to YOUR backend
    }

// Email + password
auth.createUserWithEmailAndPassword(email, password)
auth.signInWithEmailAndPassword(email, password)

// Observe auth state
auth.addAuthStateListener { firebaseAuth ->
    val user = firebaseAuth.currentUser  // null = signed out
    updateUI(user)
}

// Sign out
auth.signOut()

// Get fresh token for API calls
auth.currentUser?.getIdToken(false)?.addOnSuccessListener { result ->
    val token = result.token  // use as Bearer token in API requests
}
Firebase ID token vs Google ID token: after signing in with Google through Firebase, use the Firebase ID token (from user.getIdToken()) for your backend — not the original Google one. Firebase re-wraps and manages refresh automatically.
Q.Facebook Login SDK — integration and user data
+
// 1. Setup — build.gradle
implementation("com.facebook.android:facebook-android-sdk:latest.release")

// 2. Manifest
<meta-data android:name="com.facebook.sdk.ApplicationId" android:value="@string/facebook_app_id"/>

// 3. Initialize
FacebookSdk.sdkInitialize(applicationContext)
AppEventsLogger.activateApp(application)

// 4. Login button (simplest)
loginButton.setPermissions("email", "public_profile")
loginButton.registerCallback(callbackManager, object : FacebookCallback<LoginResult> {
    override fun onSuccess(result: LoginResult) {
        val accessToken = result.accessToken.token
        // Option A: send to Firebase
        val credential = FacebookAuthProvider.getCredential(accessToken)
        auth.signInWithCredential(credential)
        // Option B: send to your backend for verification via Graph API
    }
    override fun onCancel() {}
    override fun onError(error: FacebookException) {}
})

// 5. Fetch user profile
GraphRequest.newMeRequest(AccessToken.getCurrentAccessToken()) { obj, _ ->
    val name = obj?.getString("name")
    val email = obj?.getString("email")
    val pictureUrl = obj?.getJSONObject("picture")?.getJSONObject("data")?.getString("url")
}.apply {
    parameters = Bundle().apply { putString("fields", "name,email,picture") }
}.executeAsync()
Token verification: never trust the client's access token claim. Always verify server-side via GET https://graph.facebook.com/me?access_token=TOKEN or Firebase.
Q.Authorization — RBAC, scopes, and permission models
🔥
+

Authentication = who are you? Authorization = what can you do?

RBAC (Role-Based Access Control): assign roles to users; roles have permissions.

// JWT claims carry roles
{ "sub": "user_123", "roles": ["admin", "editor"], "exp": ... }

// Android — check role before showing UI
val canEdit = userSession.roles.contains("editor")
if (canEdit) showEditButton() else hideEditButton()

// Server enforces INDEPENDENTLY — never trust client-side role checks alone

OAuth Scopes: fine-grained permissions requested from the user.

// Request only what you need (principle of least privilege)
scope = "openid email"          // just authentication
scope = "openid email calendar" // also Google Calendar access
scope = "read:posts write:posts" // custom API scopes

Permission models:

ModelHowUse for
RBACUser → Role → PermissionsMost apps (admin/user/guest)
ABACAttributes (time, location, dept) → decisionComplex enterprise rules
ReBACRelationship-based (owner, member)Google Drive–style sharing
Defence in depth: check authorization on BOTH client (hide UI) AND server (reject request). Client-side checks are UX; server-side checks are security. Never skip the server check.
Q.Secure token storage on Android
🔥
+

Tokens are sensitive credentials. Storage strategy by token type:

TokenStorageWhy
Access token (short-lived)In-memory (ViewModel / singleton)Expires quickly; no need to persist
Refresh token (long-lived)EncryptedSharedPreferences or Keystore-encrypted RoomMust survive app restart; never in plaintext
API keysBackend only; obfuscate if must be in APKAPK is decompilable
// EncryptedSharedPreferences — backed by Keystore
val masterKey = MasterKey.Builder(context)
    .setKeyScheme(MasterKey.KeyScheme.AES256_GCM)
    .build()

val prefs = EncryptedSharedPreferences.create(
    context, "secure_prefs", masterKey,
    EncryptedSharedPreferences.PrefKeyEncryptionScheme.AES256_SIV,
    EncryptedSharedPreferences.PrefValueEncryptionScheme.AES256_GCM
)

prefs.edit().putString("refresh_token", token).apply()
val token = prefs.getString("refresh_token", null)

Never use:

  • Plain SharedPreferences (no encryption)
  • External storage (other apps can read)
  • Logcat (tokens in logs are a common vulnerability)
  • Hardcoded in source code or BuildConfig fields exposed in APK
Q.SSO (Single Sign-On) concepts
+

SSO lets one login session grant access to multiple apps/services. Enterprise common (Okta, Azure AD, Google Workspace).

How it works on mobile:

  1. App checks for existing session (usually a SSO token in secure storage)
  2. No session → redirect to Identity Provider (IdP) via Custom Tab
  3. IdP authenticates (may show login, or reuse existing browser session)
  4. IdP issues tokens back to app via deep link
  5. App uses tokens; stores session for future launches

Common IdPs for Android:

  • Google Workspace — Google Sign-In/Credential Manager
  • Microsoft Azure AD — MSAL (Microsoft Authentication Library)
  • Okta / Auth0 — AppAuth + their SDKs; supports SAML + OIDC
  • Firebase Auth — supports SAML + OIDC providers
// MSAL for Azure AD / Microsoft 365
val msalConfig = PublicClientApplication.createSingleAccountPublicClientApplication(
    context, R.raw.msal_config  // JSON config with client_id, tenant, redirect_uri
)
msalConfig.signIn(activity, null, arrayOf("User.Read")) { result ->
    val token = result.accessToken  // use for Microsoft Graph API
}
AppAuth (open-source, by OpenID Foundation) is the recommended base library for any custom OIDC/OAuth2 SSO on Android — handles PKCE, Custom Tabs, redirect URI correctly.
Q.Biometric authentication flow — linking biometrics to session
L5+
+

Biometrics (fingerprint, face) on Android authenticate the user to the DEVICE, not to your server. To link them to your server session, use Keystore-backed cryptography as the bridge.

Pattern: Biometric → Keystore key → decrypt token → send to server

// 1. At registration: generate a key requiring biometric auth
val keyGen = KeyPairGenerator.getInstance(KeyProperties.KEY_ALGORITHM_EC, "AndroidKeyStore")
keyGen.initialize(KeyGenParameterSpec.Builder("biometric_key",
    KeyProperties.PURPOSE_SIGN or KeyProperties.PURPOSE_VERIFY)
    .setDigests(KeyProperties.DIGEST_SHA256)
    .setUserAuthenticationRequired(true)           // requires biometric
    .setUserAuthenticationParameters(0,            // valid only during auth
        KeyProperties.AUTH_BIOMETRIC_STRONG)
    .build())
val keyPair = keyGen.generateKeyPair()
// Send publicKey to server for challenge-response verification

// 2. At login: biometric unlocks the key, signs a server challenge
val ks = KeyStore.getInstance("AndroidKeyStore").also { it.load(null) }
val privateKey = ks.getKey("biometric_key", null)
val signature = Signature.getInstance("SHA256withECDSA").also { it.initSign(privateKey) }

BiometricPrompt(activity, executor, object : BiometricPrompt.AuthenticationCallback() {
    override fun onAuthenticationSucceeded(result: BiometricPrompt.AuthenticationResult) {
        val sig = result.cryptoObject?.signature!!
        sig.update(serverChallenge)
        val signedChallenge = sig.sign()
        api.verifyBiometric(signedChallenge)  // server verifies with stored publicKey
    }
}).authenticate(promptInfo, BiometricPrompt.CryptoObject(signature))
Q.Authorization best practices — what to check on mobile
+

Common authorization mistakes mobile engineers make (and what to do instead):

  • Hide UI but not API: hiding a button doesn't prevent API calls. Always enforce on server. Mobile is an untrusted client.
  • Store role in SharedPreferences: user can modify it. Roles must come from the server on each session, not be cached client-side beyond the session.
  • Trust the ID token for authorization: ID tokens prove identity; API access requires access tokens with scopes. Don't send ID tokens to your API.
  • Broad scopes: request only the scopes you actually use. Users see the permission list; unnecessary scopes reduce trust.
  • No token expiry check: always check exp claim before using a token. A valid-looking token might be expired.
// Check token expiry before using
fun isTokenValid(jwt: String): Boolean {
    return try {
        val payload = JSONObject(String(Base64.decode(jwt.split(".")[1], Base64.URL_SAFE)))
        val exp = payload.getLong("exp")
        System.currentTimeMillis() / 1000 < exp - 60  // 60s buffer
    } catch (e: Exception) { false }
}

// If expired: refresh first, then proceed
if (!isTokenValid(accessToken)) {
    accessToken = refreshTokens(refreshToken)
}
api.call(accessToken)
29

Dependency Injection

Deep dives into Dagger/Hilt under the hood, scopes, and memory implications. FAANG interviews test how it works, not just how to use it.

Q.@Provides vs @Binds — What is the difference and performance impact?
+

Both tell Dagger how to provide an instance, but @Binds is highly optimized for interfaces.

  • @Provides: Requires a method body. Dagger generates a factory class and calls the method at runtime. This causes a small allocation and method-call overhead.
  • @Binds: Abstract method that takes the concrete implementation as a parameter and returns the interface. Dagger generates NO factory and NO method call—it simply casts the implementation directly.
@Module
@InstallIn(SingletonComponent::class)
abstract class AnalyticsModule {
    // 🔥 Optimized: No generated factory, zero overhead
    @Binds
    abstract fun bindAnalytics(impl: FirebaseAnalyticsImpl): AnalyticsLogger
}
Q.Explain Scopes (@Singleton vs @ViewModelScoped) and memory leaks.
+

By default, Dagger creates a new instance every time a dependency is requested (unscoped). Scoping pins the instance to a component's lifecycle.

  • @Singleton: Lives as long as the Application. Misusing this for UI-specific data causes permanent memory leaks.
  • @ViewModelScoped: Lives as long as the ViewModel. Survives configuration changes but is destroyed when the Activity finishes.
  • @ActivityRetainedScoped: Similar to ViewModel, survives rotation.

Interview Gotcha: Injecting an unscoped object into a @Singleton makes that object effectively a singleton, regardless of its own annotations.

Q.What is Assisted Injection and when do you need it?
+

Used when a dependency requires some parameters from the DI graph, but other parameters are only known at runtime (e.g., an ID passed from a previous screen).

// 1. Annotate the class and the runtime parameter
class UserDetailViewModel @AssistedInject constructor(
    private val repository: UserRepository, // From DI graph
    @Assisted private val userId: String    // From runtime
) : ViewModel() {

    // 2. Define the factory interface
    @AssistedFactory
    interface Factory {
        fun create(userId: String): UserDetailViewModel
    }
}

You then inject the Factory into your Fragment/Activity, and call factory.create(id).

30

Classic Views & Interop

Bridging the gap between legacy XML codebases and modern Compose UI.

Q.Explain the classic View Lifecycle (onMeasure, onLayout, onDraw)
+

The OS traverses the view tree in three distinct phases:

  1. onMeasure(widthMeasureSpec, heightMeasureSpec): Parent asks children "How big do you want to be?" Children reply with measured width/height based on constraints (EXACTLY, AT_MOST, UNSPECIFIED).
  2. onLayout(changed, left, top, right, bottom): Parent assigns the actual X/Y positions and final sizes to its children.
  3. onDraw(canvas): The view paints itself (background, text, shapes) onto the provided Canvas.

Gotcha: Never allocate objects (like new Paint()) inside onDraw() because it is called 60+ times per second. It will trigger aggressive GC and drop frames.

Q.How do you embed Compose in XML and XML in Compose?
+

Compose in XML: Use ComposeView.

// In your Fragment/Activity
val composeView = findViewById<ComposeView>(R.id.compose_container)
composeView.apply {
    setViewCompositionStrategy(ViewCompositionStrategy.DisposeOnViewTreeLifecycleDestroyed)
    setContent { MyComposeScreen() }
}

XML in Compose: Use AndroidView.

@Composable
fun LegacyMapView() {
    AndroidView(
        factory = { context -> MapView(context).apply { /* init */ } },
        update = { view -> view.updateCamera(location) }
    )
}
31

Modern OS Restrictions

Android 12 through 15+ have heavily locked down background execution, alarms, and permissions to save battery and protect privacy.

Q.What changed with Foreground Services in Android 14+?
+

You can no longer launch a "generic" Foreground Service. You must explicitly declare the foregroundServiceType in the Manifest and request a specific permission for that type.

  • Types include: mediaPlayback, location, dataSync, health.
  • Enforcement: If you declare dataSync, but don't actually do network operations, the OS will throw a SecurityException.
  • Alternative: For deferrable work, Google mandates using WorkManager instead of keeping a service alive.
Q.Explain the shift to Granular Permissions and Photo Picker (Android 13/14)
+

The broad READ_EXTERNAL_STORAGE permission is dead. Apps must now request granular permissions:

  • READ_MEDIA_IMAGES
  • READ_MEDIA_VIDEO
  • READ_MEDIA_AUDIO

Best Practice (Photo Picker): Instead of requesting any permissions, use the OS-level Photo Picker (via ActivityResultContracts.PickVisualMedia). The OS renders the gallery UI out-of-process and grants your app temporary, read-only access to only the specific photos the user selects.

Q.What is the crackdown on Exact Alarms (Android 14)?
+

Apps can no longer request SCHEDULE_EXACT_ALARM by default (used via AlarmManager). It is restricted to calendar and alarm clock apps.

If you use it without the user explicitly granting it in system settings, your app will crash. For everything else (syncing, reminders), you must use inexact alarms or WorkManager, which allows the OS to batch wake-ups and save battery.

32

Advanced Memory Management

Understanding how the JVM handles memory, debugging OutOfMemory (OOM) errors, and reference types.

Q.Explain GC Roots and Reference Types (Strong, Weak, Soft, Phantom)
+

The Garbage Collector starts at "GC Roots" (active threads, static variables, JNI references) and traces every connected object. Anything not connected is garbage collected.

  • Strong Reference: Default. val user = User(). Never collected as long as it's reachable.
  • WeakReference: Collected on the very next GC cycle if no strong references point to it. (Used to avoid Context leaks).
  • SoftReference: Kept alive until the OS is absolutely desperate for memory (OOM is imminent). Used for memory-sensitive caches.
  • PhantomReference: Used purely to schedule post-mortem cleanup tasks just before an object is destroyed. Rarely used in product code.
Q.How do you read a Heap Dump? (Shallow Size vs Retained Size)
+

When analyzing a .hprof file in Android Studio Profiler to find leaks:

  • Shallow Size: The memory consumed by the object itself (just its own primitive fields and pointer addresses). Usually very small.
  • Retained Size: The total memory that would be freed if this object was garbage collected (includes the shallow size + the size of all objects only reachable through this object).

To fix a leak: Look for objects with a massive Retained Size, and trace their "Shortest Path to GC Root" to find what is erroneously holding the strong reference (often a static field or a forgotten observer).

33

Mobile CI/CD & Distribution

How code actually gets from a Pull Request to a user's device.

Q.App Bundles (.aab) vs APKs and Play Feature Delivery
+

APK: A monolithic archive containing resources for every screen density, language, and CPU architecture. Wastes massive amounts of user bandwidth and storage.

AAB (Android App Bundle): A publishing format you upload to Google Play. Google Play's servers take the AAB and dynamically generate a customized, tiny APK specifically for the user's exact device (e.g., only downloading xxhdpi drawables and es-ES strings).

Play Feature Delivery: Using AABs, you can modularize your app. E.g., a "Customer Support Chat" module is only downloaded on-demand when the user actually navigates to that screen.

Q.Explain a typical Mobile CI/CD Pipeline (Fastlane / GitHub Actions)
+

A senior engineer understands the automation gates:

  1. Pull Request (CI): GitHub Actions triggers ./gradlew lint (code quality) and ./gradlew testDebugUnitTest (JUnit). If these fail, the PR cannot be merged.
  2. Nightly Build: Runs slower UI/Espresso tests on Firebase Test Lab devices.
  3. Release (CD): Fastlane is triggered to bump the version code, build the .aab, sign it with the release keystore, upload mapping files to Crashlytics, and deploy directly to the Google Play Internal track.
34

Process Death & State Restoration

Configuration changes (rotation) are easy. Surviving process death (OS killing your app) is the true test of a senior developer.

Q.Difference between Configuration Change and Process Death?
+
  • Config Change: Activity is destroyed and recreated. ViewModel is retained (if using ViewModelProvider). Local memory (non-static) is lost.
  • Process Death: The entire application process is killed by the OS. Everything in memory is wiped, including the ViewModel. The OS will attempt to recreate your Activity stack, but your ViewModel will be fresh (re-initialized).

Senior Strategy: Use SavedStateHandle in your ViewModel. It survives process death and is automatically bundled into the system's saved instance state.

35

Advanced Kotlin & Coroutines

Q.Why did my coroutine not cancel? (The "Blocking Call" Trap)
+

Coroutines are cooperative. They only check for cancellation at suspension points (like delay() or other suspend functions).

// ❌ This loop will NOT stop on cancellation
launch(Dispatchers.Default) {
    while(isActive) { // Need to manually check isActive
        // Heavy blocking computation (e.g., Image processing)
        processLargeBitmap() 
    }
}
// ✅ Fix: use ensureActive() or yield() inside the loop
// launch { while(isActive) { ensureActive(); process() } }
36

Advanced Testing Strategies

Q.When to use Mocks vs. Fakes?
+
  • Mocks (Mockito/MockK): Verify behavior (e.g., "Was this function called 3 times?"). Brittle: if you refactor internals, tests break.
  • Fakes (Custom implementation): A lightweight, working version of a dependency (e.g., an InMemoryUserRepository). Superior for seniors: they are more stable, run faster, and model real-world state transitions rather than just "recording calls."
★

DSA & Algorithms

Coding round fundamentals. Every pattern has: recognition signals, an SVG diagram to visualize the approach, a template, and the problems where it's applied. Start with the framework question, then patterns in order.

Q.How to approach ANY coding interview problem — the UREOP framework
🔥
+

Never jump to code. A structured approach signals senior thinking and prevents wasted effort.

StepActionTime
UnderstandRestate the problem. Confirm input/output. Ask about constraints (size, type, edge cases). Are duplicates possible? Negative numbers?2-3 min
RepresentDraw examples. At least 2: normal case + edge case. Write them down, don't just think.2 min
ExploreThink aloud — brute force first (state complexity), then optimize. "Naive is O(n²), but I can do better with a hashmap..."3-5 min
OptimizeIdentify the pattern. Agree on approach before coding. "I'll use a sliding window — does that make sense?"2 min
ProgramCode clean, talk through logic. Handle edge cases explicitly. Don't code in silence.15-20 min
After coding: trace through your example by hand. Find bugs before they do. Then discuss time/space complexity. "This is O(n) time and O(1) space because..."

If you're stuck: say "Let me think about the pattern here..." then ask "Can I ask a clarifying question?" Never sit silent for more than 60 seconds.

Q.Big O notation — time and space complexity quick reference
🔥
+

Space complexity note: O(1) = constant extra space (no DS proportional to n). O(n) = storing n items (hashmap, array copy). Recursive calls add O(depth) stack space.

What interviewers want to hear: "This is O(n) time because I visit each element once, and O(n) space for the hashmap. Could I get it to O(1) space? Yes, but I'd sacrifice readability for this problem size."

Q.Two Pointers — pattern, recognition, template, problems
🔥
+

Recognize when: sorted array + find pair/triplet with target sum/difference | remove duplicates in-place | palindrome check | comparing two arrays

// Template — Two Sum (sorted array)
fun twoSum(nums: IntArray, target: Int): IntArray {
    var left = 0; var right = nums.size - 1
    while (left < right) {
        val sum = nums[left] + nums[right]
        when {
            sum == target -> return intArrayOf(left, right)
            sum < target  -> left++   // need bigger
            else          -> right--  // need smaller
        }
    }
    return intArrayOf(-1, -1)
}
Classic problemsAlways asked
  • Two Sum II (sorted)
  • Container With Most Water
  • 3Sum → fix one, two-ptr rest
  • Trapping Rain Water
Variation: Same directionRemove duplicates / Fast-slow
  • Remove Duplicates in Sorted Array
  • Move Zeroes
  • Linked List Cycle (Floyd's)
  • Find Middle of LinkedList
Q.Sliding Window — pattern, recognition, template, problems
🔥
+

Recognize when: subarray / substring + "longest/shortest/minimum" + constraint on the window content

// Template — Longest Substring Without Repeating Characters
fun lengthOfLongestSubstring(s: String): Int {
    val seen = HashMap<Char, Int>()  // char → last index
    var left = 0; var maxLen = 0
    for (right in s.indices) {
        if (s[right] in seen && seen[s[right]]!! >= left) {
            left = seen[s[right]]!! + 1  // shrink window
        }
        seen[s[right]] = right
        maxLen = maxOf(maxLen, right - left + 1)
    }
    return maxLen
}  // O(n) time, O(min(m,n)) space
Fixed size windowMax/min sum of k elements
  • Max Average Subarray I (size k)
  • Number of Subarrays of size k
  • Sliding Window Maximum
Variable size windowLongest/shortest with constraint
  • Longest Substring No Repeat
  • Minimum Window Substring
  • Longest Repeating Char Replacement
  • Fruit Into Baskets
Q.Binary Search — template, variants, and when to recognize it
🔥
+
// Universal binary search template
fun binarySearch(nums: IntArray, target: Int): Int {
    var lo = 0; var hi = nums.size - 1
    while (lo <= hi) {
        val mid = lo + (hi - lo) / 2   // avoid overflow vs (lo+hi)/2
        when {
            nums[mid] == target -> return mid
            nums[mid] < target  -> lo = mid + 1
            else                -> hi = mid - 1
        }
    }
    return -1  // or lo for insertion point
}

// Binary search on ANSWER (common pattern!)
// "Find minimum capacity such that condition is met"
fun canShip(weights: IntArray, days: Int, capacity: Int): Boolean { ... }
// lo = max(weights), hi = sum(weights)
// binary search on capacity, check if canShip(weights, days, mid)
ClassicSorted array
  • Search in Rotated Sorted Array
  • Find First/Last Position
  • Search a 2D Matrix
Binary search on ANSWERMinimize/maximize something
  • Koko Eating Bananas
  • Ship Packages Within D Days
  • Capacity to Ship (Samsara-type)
Q.Hash Map / Frequency Counter — the most powerful O(1) tool
🔥
+

Recognize when: need to count, group, look up, or check if something was seen before.

// Pattern 1: Frequency counter
fun isAnagram(s: String, t: String): Boolean {
    if (s.length != t.length) return false
    val freq = IntArray(26)
    s.forEach { freq[it - 'a']++ }
    t.forEach { freq[it - 'a']-- }
    return freq.all { it == 0 }
}

// Pattern 2: Complement lookup (Two Sum)
fun twoSum(nums: IntArray, target: Int): IntArray {
    val map = HashMap<Int, Int>()  // value → index
    for ((i, n) in nums.withIndex()) {
        val complement = target - n
        if (complement in map) return intArrayOf(map[complement]!!, i)
        map[n] = i
    }
    return intArrayOf()
}

// Pattern 3: Grouping
fun groupAnagrams(strs: Array<String>): List<List<String>> {
    val map = HashMap<String, MutableList<String>>()
    strs.forEach { s ->
        val key = s.toCharArray().sorted().joinToString("")  // sorted chars as key
        map.getOrPut(key) { mutableListOf() }.add(s)
    }
    return map.values.toList()
}
Classic HashMap problemsAll O(n) solutions
  • Two Sum (complement lookup)
  • Group Anagrams (sorted key)
  • Top K Frequent Elements
  • Longest Consecutive Sequence
Sliding window + HashMapSubstring problems
  • Longest Substring No Repeat
  • Minimum Window Substring
  • Substring with Concatenation
Q.Stack — monotonic stack, when to use it
🔥
+
// Balanced parentheses — stack classic
fun isValid(s: String): Boolean {
    val stack = ArrayDeque<Char>()
    for (c in s) {
        when (c) {
            '(', '[', '{' -> stack.addLast(c)
            ')' -> if (stack.removeLastOrNull() != '(') return false
            ']' -> if (stack.removeLastOrNull() != '[') return false
            '}' -> if (stack.removeLastOrNull() != '{') return false
        }
    }
    return stack.isEmpty()
}

// Next Greater Element — monotonic stack
fun nextGreaterElement(nums: IntArray): IntArray {
    val result = IntArray(nums.size) { -1 }
    val stack = ArrayDeque<Int>()  // stores indices
    for (i in nums.indices) {
        while (stack.isNotEmpty() && nums[stack.last()] < nums[i]) {
            result[stack.removeLast()] = nums[i]
        }
        stack.addLast(i)
    }
    return result
}
Classic stack
  • Valid Parentheses
  • Min Stack (track minimum)
  • Evaluate Reverse Polish
  • Daily Temperatures
Monotonic stack
  • Next Greater Element
  • Largest Rectangle in Histogram
  • Trapping Rain Water (stack approach)
  • Asteroid Collision
Q.BFS — breadth-first search template, shortest path, level order
🔥
+
// BFS template — finds SHORTEST path in unweighted graph/grid
fun bfs(root: TreeNode?): Int {
    if (root == null) return 0
    val queue = ArrayDeque<TreeNode>()
    queue.addLast(root)
    var depth = 0
    while (queue.isNotEmpty()) {
        val size = queue.size          // process current level
        repeat(size) {
            val node = queue.removeFirst()
            // Process node here
            node.left?.let { queue.addLast(it) }
            node.right?.let { queue.addLast(it) }
        }
        depth++
    }
    return depth
}

// BFS on grid — shortest path
fun shortestPath(grid: Array<IntArray>, start: Pair<Int,Int>, end: Pair<Int,Int>): Int {
    val queue = ArrayDeque<Triple<Int,Int,Int>>()  // row, col, steps
    queue.addLast(Triple(start.first, start.second, 0))
    val visited = HashSet<Pair<Int,Int>>()
    val dirs = arrayOf(intArrayOf(0,1), intArrayOf(0,-1), intArrayOf(1,0), intArrayOf(-1,0))
    while (queue.isNotEmpty()) {
        val (r, c, steps) = queue.removeFirst()
        if (r to c == end) return steps
        if (!visited.add(r to c)) continue
        for (d in dirs) {
            val nr = r + d[0]; val nc = c + d[1]
            if (nr in grid.indices && nc in grid[0].indices && grid[nr][nc] != 1)
                queue.addLast(Triple(nr, nc, steps + 1))
        }
    }
    return -1
}

Use BFS when: shortest path in unweighted graph, level-order traversal, minimum steps/moves, spreading from multiple sources simultaneously.

Q.DFS & Backtracking — tree/graph traversal, combinations
🔥
+
// DFS template — recursive
fun dfs(node: TreeNode?) {
    if (node == null) return   // base case
    // Pre-order: process HERE
    println(node.val)
    dfs(node.left)
    // In-order: process HERE (gives sorted order for BST)
    dfs(node.right)
    // Post-order: process HERE (children done, now process parent)
}

// Backtracking template — generate all combinations
fun combine(n: Int, k: Int): List<List<Int>> {
    val result = mutableListOf<List<Int>>()
    fun backtrack(start: Int, current: MutableList<Int>) {
        if (current.size == k) { result.add(current.toList()); return }
        for (i in start..n) {
            current.add(i)        // choose
            backtrack(i + 1, current)  // explore
            current.removeLast()  // un-choose (backtrack)
        }
    }
    backtrack(1, mutableListOf())
    return result
}
DFS on trees
  • Max depth, path sum
  • Validate BST, LCA
  • Serialize/Deserialize tree
Backtracking
  • Subsets, Combinations, Permutations
  • Word Search (grid DFS)
  • Sudoku Solver, N-Queens
Q.Dynamic Programming — recognizing it and the two approaches
🔥
+

Recognize DP when: optimization (min/max), counting (how many ways), decision (can we achieve X), and the problem has overlapping subproblems (recursive approach re-solves same states).

// Top-down (memoization) — natural recursive thinking
fun climbStairs(n: Int, memo: HashMap<Int,Int> = HashMap()): Int {
    if (n <= 2) return n
    return memo.getOrPut(n) { climbStairs(n-1, memo) + climbStairs(n-2, memo) }
}

// Bottom-up (tabulation) — usually faster (no stack overhead)
fun climbStairsDP(n: Int): Int {
    val dp = IntArray(n + 1)
    dp[1] = 1; if (n > 1) dp[2] = 2
    for (i in 3..n) dp[i] = dp[i-1] + dp[i-2]
    return dp[n]
}

// Space-optimized — O(1) space when only last 2 states needed
fun climbStairsOpt(n: Int): Int {
    var prev = 1; var curr = 2
    for (i in 3..n) { val next = prev + curr; prev = curr; curr = next }
    return if (n == 1) 1 else curr
}
1D DP
  • Climbing Stairs, House Robber
  • Coin Change (min coins)
  • Longest Increasing Subsequence
2D DP
  • Unique Paths (grid)
  • Longest Common Subsequence
  • 0/1 Knapsack
  • Edit Distance
Q.Trees — must-know traversals and BST properties
🔥
+
// All traversals of a binary tree
fun inorder(root: TreeNode?): List<Int> {    // Left → Root → Right (BST sorted order)
    val result = mutableListOf<Int>()
    fun dfs(node: TreeNode?) {
        if (node == null) return
        dfs(node.left)
        result.add(node.`val`)
        dfs(node.right)
    }
    dfs(root); return result
}

// Iterative inorder (avoids stack overflow on deep trees)
fun inorderIterative(root: TreeNode?): List<Int> {
    val result = mutableListOf<Int>()
    val stack = ArrayDeque<TreeNode>()
    var curr = root
    while (curr != null || stack.isNotEmpty()) {
        while (curr != null) { stack.addLast(curr); curr = curr.left }
        curr = stack.removeLast()
        result.add(curr.`val`)
        curr = curr.right
    }
    return result
}

// Validate BST — pass min/max range down
fun isValidBST(node: TreeNode?, min: Long = Long.MIN_VALUE, max: Long = Long.MAX_VALUE): Boolean {
    if (node == null) return true
    if (node.`val` <= min || node.`val` >= max) return false
    return isValidBST(node.left, min, node.`val`.toLong()) &&
           isValidBST(node.right, node.`val`.toLong(), max)
}

// Lowest Common Ancestor
fun lowestCommonAncestor(root: TreeNode?, p: TreeNode?, q: TreeNode?): TreeNode? {
    if (root == null || root == p || root == q) return root
    val left = lowestCommonAncestor(root.left, p, q)
    val right = lowestCommonAncestor(root.right, p, q)
    return if (left != null && right != null) root else left ?: right
}
Tree problems
  • Max/Min depth (BFS or DFS)
  • Path sum (DFS target tracking)
  • Symmetric / Same tree
  • Invert binary tree
BST specific
  • Validate BST
  • BST to sorted array (inorder)
  • LCA in BST (navigate by value)
  • Kth Smallest (inorder k-th)
Q.Heap / Priority Queue — Top K and median problems
🔥
+

Recognize when: "find K largest/smallest", "Kth largest", "median from stream", "most frequent K elements", "merge K sorted lists".

// Kotlin: PriorityQueue is min-heap by default
val minHeap = PriorityQueue<Int>()            // smallest at top
val maxHeap = PriorityQueue<Int>(reverseOrder()) // largest at top

// Pattern: Kth Largest Element — maintain min-heap of size K
fun findKthLargest(nums: IntArray, k: Int): Int {
    val heap = PriorityQueue<Int>()
    for (n in nums) {
        heap.offer(n)
        if (heap.size > k) heap.poll()  // remove smallest, keep K largest
    }
    return heap.peek()!!   // O(n log k) — better than O(n log n) sort
}

// Pattern: Top K Frequent Elements
fun topKFrequent(nums: IntArray, k: Int): IntArray {
    val freq = HashMap<Int,Int>()
    nums.forEach { freq[it] = (freq[it] ?: 0) + 1 }
    // Min-heap sorted by frequency
    val heap = PriorityQueue<Map.Entry<Int,Int>>(compareBy { it.value })
    for (entry in freq.entries) {
        heap.offer(entry)
        if (heap.size > k) heap.poll()
    }
    return heap.map { it.key }.toIntArray()
}

// Pattern: Merge K Sorted Lists
// Put first node of each list into min-heap, always extract min, push next node
Min-heap (keep K largest)
  • Kth Largest Element in Stream
  • Top K Frequent Elements
  • K Closest Points to Origin
Two heaps (median)
  • Max-heap (left half) + Min-heap (right half)
  • Median from Data Stream
  • Sliding Window Median
Q.LRU Cache — implement it (most common mobile-flavored coding question)
🔥
+

Least Recently Used cache — when full, evicts the item that was accessed the longest time ago. Needs O(1) get AND put.

Data structure: HashMap (O(1) lookup) + Doubly Linked List (O(1) remove from middle, O(1) add to front).

class LRUCache(private val capacity: Int) {
    // Doubly linked list node
    inner class Node(val key: Int, var value: Int) {
        var prev: Node? = null; var next: Node? = null
    }

    private val map = HashMap<Int, Node>(capacity)
    // Dummy head/tail — no null checks needed
    private val head = Node(-1, -1)
    private val tail = Node(-1, -1)

    init { head.next = tail; tail.prev = head }

    // Always insert right after head (most recent)
    private fun insertFront(node: Node) {
        node.next = head.next; node.prev = head
        head.next!!.prev = node; head.next = node
    }

    // Remove from current position
    private fun remove(node: Node) {
        node.prev!!.next = node.next
        node.next!!.prev = node.prev
    }

    fun get(key: Int): Int {
        val node = map[key] ?: return -1
        remove(node); insertFront(node)  // mark as recently used
        return node.value
    }

    fun put(key: Int, value: Int) {
        map[key]?.let { remove(it) }     // update if exists
        val node = Node(key, value)
        map[key] = node; insertFront(node)
        if (map.size > capacity) {       // evict LRU (just before tail)
            val lru = tail.prev!!
            remove(lru); map.remove(lru.key)
        }
    }
}  // All ops O(1)
Q.Graph algorithms — connected components, cycle detection
🔥
+
// Graph representation
val graph = HashMap<Int, MutableList<Int>>()  // adjacency list

// Number of connected components (Union-Find approach)
class UnionFind(n: Int) {
    val parent = IntArray(n) { it }
    val rank = IntArray(n) { 0 }
    var components = n

    fun find(x: Int): Int {
        if (parent[x] != x) parent[x] = find(parent[x])  // path compression
        return parent[x]
    }

    fun union(x: Int, y: Int) {
        val px = find(x); val py = find(y)
        if (px == py) return
        components--
        if (rank[px] < rank[py]) parent[px] = py          // union by rank
        else if (rank[px] > rank[py]) parent[py] = px
        else { parent[py] = px; rank[px]++ }
    }
}

// Cycle detection in directed graph (DFS + coloring)
fun hasCycle(graph: Map<Int, List<Int>>): Boolean {
    // 0=white(unvisited), 1=gray(in stack), 2=black(done)
    val color = HashMap<Int, Int>()
    fun dfs(node: Int): Boolean {
        color[node] = 1   // mark as in-progress
        for (neighbor in graph[node] ?: emptyList()) {
            if (color[neighbor] == 1) return true      // back edge = cycle!
            if (color[neighbor] != 2 && dfs(neighbor)) return true
        }
        color[node] = 2; return false  // done
    }
    return graph.keys.any { color[it] == 0 && dfs(it) }
}
BFS graphs
  • Shortest path unweighted
  • Level order / spreading
  • Bipartite check (2-coloring)
DFS graphs
  • Connected components
  • Cycle detection
  • Topological sort (Kahn's or DFS)
Q.Sorting algorithms — when to use which
+
AlgorithmTime (avg)Time (worst)SpaceStable?Use when
Merge SortO(n log n)O(n log n)O(n)✅Need stable sort; linked lists; external sort
Quick SortO(n log n)O(n²)O(log n)❌In-practice fastest for arrays; randomize pivot
Heap SortO(n log n)O(n log n)O(1)❌In-place, guaranteed O(n log n)
Counting SortO(n + k)O(n + k)O(k)✅Small integer range (k = range size)
Bucket SortO(n + k)O(n²)O(n)✅Uniformly distributed floats
// Merge Sort — classic divide and conquer
fun mergeSort(arr: IntArray): IntArray {
    if (arr.size <= 1) return arr
    val mid = arr.size / 2
    val left = mergeSort(arr.sliceArray(0 until mid))
    val right = mergeSort(arr.sliceArray(mid until arr.size))
    return merge(left, right)
}

fun merge(left: IntArray, right: IntArray): IntArray {
    val result = IntArray(left.size + right.size)
    var i = 0; var j = 0; var k = 0
    while (i < left.size && j < right.size)
        result[k++] = if (left[i] <= right[j]) left[i++] else right[j++]
    while (i < left.size) result[k++] = left[i++]
    while (j < right.size) result[k++] = right[j++]
    return result
}
Interview answer: "Kotlin's sortedBy uses TimSort (merge + insertion sort) — O(n log n) worst, stable. I'd use it for general sorting. For small integer ranges I'd consider counting sort."
Q.Mobile-flavored: Implement a debounce function
🔥
+

Debounce: delay execution until N milliseconds after the last call. Classic interview question for mobile engineers.

// Kotlin / Android implementation
class Debouncer(private val delayMs: Long = 300L) {
    private var job: Job? = null

    fun debounce(scope: CoroutineScope, action: suspend () -> Unit) {
        job?.cancel()
        job = scope.launch {
            delay(delayMs)
            action()
        }
    }
}

// Usage
val debouncer = Debouncer(300L)
searchField.doOnTextChanged { text, _, _, _ ->
    debouncer.debounce(viewModelScope) {
        viewModel.search(text.toString())
    }
}

// Flow-based debounce (cleaner for search-as-you-type)
queryFlow
    .debounce(300L)
    .distinctUntilChanged()
    .flatMapLatest { q -> api.search(q) }
    .collect { showResults(it) }

// Pure function version (no coroutines — for JavaScript-style answer)
fun debounce(fn: () -> Unit, delay: Long): () -> Unit {
    var handler: Job? = null
    return {
        handler?.cancel()
        handler = GlobalScope.launch {
            delay(delay); fn()
        }
    }
}
Q.Common patterns cheat sheet — which pattern for which problem signal
🔥
+
Problem signalPattern to try first
Sorted array + find pair/tripletTwo Pointers
Subarray/substring + constraint (longest/shortest)Sliding Window
"Search in sorted…"Binary Search
"Seen before?" or counting duplicates/frequenciesHashMap
Balanced parentheses, "next greater element"Stack / Monotonic Stack
Tree traversal, shortest path, level by levelBFS (queue)
Tree path, all combinations, backtrackingDFS / Recursion
Minimum/maximum/count with overlapping subproblemsDynamic Programming
K largest/smallest/most frequentHeap / Priority Queue
Intervals (overlap, merge, insert)Sort + Greedy
Connected components, union groupsUnion-Find or BFS/DFS
O(1) get and evict on capacityHashMap + Doubly Linked List (LRU)
Words with common prefixTrie
Divide array into equal halves, sortMerge Sort / Divide & Conquer
Interview meta: when you see a problem, say aloud "The signal here is [X], which suggests [pattern]. Let me verify: [walk through example]." This shows pattern recognition, not memorization.
★

Anti-Patterns & Best Practices

The explicit "things to avoid and why" guide. Android, Compose, architecture, security, RN, and testing anti-patterns. Plus production checklist, code review red flags, and interview meta.

Q.Android anti-patterns — top 10 things never to do
🔥
+
  1. Network/disk I/O on Main thread → ANR within 5s. Always use coroutines + IO dispatcher.
  2. Inner class holding Activity reference → memory leak. Use static nested class + WeakReference, or coroutines.
  3. God Activity / God Fragment → unmaintainable. Move logic to ViewModel + UseCases.
  4. Passing large objects in Intents/Bundles → TransactionTooLargeException (>1MB). Use a shared ViewModel or Room.
  5. notifyDataSetChanged() every update → full RecyclerView redraw. Use DiffUtil or ListAdapter.
  6. Hardcoded strings → untranslatable, no i18n. Use string resources always.
  7. Storing sensitive data in SharedPreferences → unencrypted. Use EncryptedSharedPreferences or Keystore.
  8. Registering broadcast receivers without unregistering → leak. Always unregister in onStop/onDestroy.
  9. GlobalScope.launch { } → leaked coroutines with no lifecycle. Use viewModelScope or lifecycleScope.
  10. Static context references → memory leak. Application context for long-lived; Activity context only for UI.
Q.Compose anti-patterns — common mistakes that hurt performance or correctness
🔥
+
  • Reading state in wrong phase: reading animated state in Composition causes recomposition every frame. Read in graphicsLayer { } for Drawing-phase reads.
  • Unstable parameters: passing List<T> directly causes recomposition even when unchanged. Wrap with @Immutable or use ImmutableList.
  • Creating objects in composable bodies: val brush = Brush.gradient(...) inside a composable creates a new object every recomposition. Use remember { }.
  • LaunchedEffect without proper keys: LaunchedEffect(Unit) runs once. LaunchedEffect(userId) re-runs when userId changes. Wrong key = stale or over-triggering.
  • Calling suspend fns directly in composable body: composables are not coroutines. Use LaunchedEffect or produceState.
  • Modifier.clickable without ripple handling: add indication = rememberRipple() or null explicitly.
  • Ignoring stability annotations: not annotating ViewModel-exposed data classes with @Stable prevents smart-skipping.
// ❌ Creates new list each recomposition
@Composable fun Foo(items: List<Item>) { /* unstable */ }

// ✅ Wrap or use ImmutableList
@Immutable data class Items(val list: List<Item>)
@Composable fun Foo(items: Items) { /* stable */ }
Q.Architecture anti-patterns — violations that hurt long-term
🔥
+
  • ViewModel that knows about Views: ViewModel should never hold Context, View, or Activity references. It outlives the Activity. Use SharedFlow for UI events.
  • Repository talking to UI: Repository returns raw data (or Flow). ViewModel transforms it. UI only reads ViewModel state. Never skip layers.
  • Multiple sources of truth: if Room AND a cache AND a ViewModel variable all hold "the current user", they'll diverge. SSOT = one place per piece of data.
  • Network calls in UI layer: Composables/Fragments/Activities calling APIs directly. All async work belongs in ViewModel or Repository.
  • Circular dependencies between modules: feature:home depends on feature:profile which depends on feature:home. Break via :core:navigation interfaces.
  • UseCases that just delegate: class GetUserUseCase { fun get(id) = repo.get(id) } — pure delegation with no business logic adds layers without value. Only add UseCases when there's logic to encapsulate.
Q.Performance anti-patterns — what makes apps slow
🔥
+
  • Synchronous work on Main thread: any blocking call over ~5ms is visible as jank. Move to Dispatchers.IO or Default.
  • Overdraw: multiple layers painting the same pixels. Remove unnecessary backgrounds, use clipToPadding, check with GPU overdraw overlay.
  • Not using the right Dispatcher: CPU-heavy work on Dispatchers.IO (meant for I/O wait) — uses threads unnecessarily. CPU work → Dispatchers.Default.
  • Bitmap allocated at full resolution: decoding a 4K image for a 200dp thumbnail. Always sample down with inSampleSize or let Coil/Glide size it.
  • Not using Baseline Profiles: Compose-heavy apps see 20-40% startup improvement from pre-compiling hot paths.
  • Allocating in draw/measure: creating objects in onDraw() or custom Compose draw lambdas → GC pressure during rendering. Allocate once in remember { }.
  • Blocking StateFlow/SharedFlow with slow collectors: a slow UI collecting a high-frequency StateFlow can back up. Use conflate() or collectLatest.
Q.Security anti-patterns — vulnerabilities you introduce unknowingly
🔥
+
  • Logging sensitive data: tokens, passwords, PII in Logcat. Extractable via adb on debug builds. Strip all sensitive logging before release.
  • Hardcoded secrets in source code: API keys in strings.xml or BuildConfig. APKs are decompilable — anyone can extract them. Move to backend proxy.
  • Trusting all SSL certificates: TrustManager { override fun checkServerTrusted() {} } — accepts anything, enables MITM. Never do this, even for debugging.
  • Implicit intents for sensitive actions: other apps can intercept. Always use explicit intents for sensitive operations.
  • SQL concatenation: "SELECT * WHERE id = " + userInput → SQL injection. Always parameterized queries (Room does this by default).
  • Storing tokens in SharedPreferences: unencrypted, world-readable on rooted devices. Use EncryptedSharedPreferences or Keystore.
  • android:debuggable="true" in release: allows ADB debugging of production app. Ensure buildTypes { release { isDebuggable = false } }.
  • android:allowBackup="true" for sensitive apps: allows ADB to back up your private data. Set to false for banking/health/auth apps.
Q.React Native anti-patterns — common mistakes from native Android perspective
+
  • Heavy computation on JS thread: JS thread = UI thread equivalent. Crypto, sorting 10K items, complex transforms → use native module or InteractionManager.runAfterInteractions().
  • Using ScrollView for large dynamic lists: renders ALL children at once. Always use FlatList (virtualizes like RecyclerView).
  • Anonymous functions as props: onPress={() => doSomething()} creates a new function reference every render → unnecessary re-renders. Use useCallback.
  • setState in a loop or animation frame: each setState triggers a re-render. Use Reanimated for animations (runs on UI thread via JSI).
  • Missing key prop in lists: React can't identify which items changed → full re-render. Always provide stable, unique key.
  • Not cleaning up effects: subscriptions, event listeners in useEffect without cleanup return → memory leaks surviving unmount.
  • useNativeDriver: false on animations: JS-thread animations drop frames on slow devices. Most animations can use useNativeDriver: true.
Q.Testing anti-patterns — what makes tests brittle and useless
+
  • Testing implementation, not behavior: if your test breaks every time you refactor (without changing behavior), it's testing internals. Test what the user/caller sees.
  • Mocking what you don't own: mocking Retrofit or OkHttp directly — if they change behavior, your tests won't catch it. Use fakes or integration tests for external deps.
  • Fixture bloat: tests with 50 lines of setup before the assertion. Extract a TestFixtures builder. Test readability matters.
  • Thread.sleep() in tests: brittle on slow CI, passes locally, fails in pipeline. Use TestCoroutineDispatcher and advanceTimeBy().
  • One enormous integration test instead of unit tests: slow, hard to debug, tests too many things. Prefer small, fast units.
  • Asserting on irrelevant details: test verifies 10 things when only 1 matters → maintenance burden when unrelated fields change.
  • Not testing edge cases: empty list, null, single item, max value, error state. Bugs live at boundaries.
Q.Things to always do — senior engineering habits
+
  • Write the test first (or immediately after): code written to be tested is better-designed code.
  • Read the error message fully before Googling. Most bugs are explained in the stack trace.
  • Measure before optimizing: profile first, optimize the actual bottleneck, not where you think it is.
  • Handle all error states in UI: Loading, Empty, Error, Success — all four, not just Success.
  • Use the same API you expose: write real usage code before finalizing an interface design.
  • Document the WHY, not the WHAT: the code says what. Comments explain why a non-obvious choice was made.
  • Check for memory leaks in debug builds: LeakCanary integrated always. Fix leaks before they reach production.
  • Review your own PR before requesting review: you catch 80% of comments yourself.
Q.Code review red flags — things to never merge
🔥
+
  • TODO: fix this without a linked ticket — will never be fixed
  • Thread.sleep(500) in production code — timing dependency, not a real fix
  • catch (e: Exception) { } — swallowed exception, silent failure
  • !!` on anything not provably non-null — potential NullPointerException bomb
  • Non-null assertion on user input or API response — will crash in production
  • Business logic in a Fragment or Activity — untestable, wrong layer
  • Hardcoded strings, colors, or dimensions — localization and theming break
  • New dependency added without justification — bundle size, security surface, license
  • Logging PII or tokens — security vulnerability
  • Test deleted "because it was failing" — hiding a real bug
Q.Production readiness checklist — before you ship
🔥
+
  • All error states handled (loading, empty, error, network-offline)
  • No TODO/FIXME without a tracking ticket
  • isDebuggable = false in release build type
  • Sensitive data NOT in Logcat (search for PII/token in log statements)
  • ProGuard/R8 enabled and tested on release variant
  • Network Security Config blocks cleartext traffic
  • API keys NOT in source code or BuildConfig fields
  • Crash reporting (Crashlytics) integrated and tested
  • Analytics events fired for key user flows
  • Feature flags wrapping new features (kill switch available)
  • Accessibility: all images have contentDescription, contrast 4.5:1+
  • Battery: no wake locks held unnecessarily, background work via WorkManager
  • Memory: LeakCanary clean on debug build, no OOM on large lists
  • Offline: app degrades gracefully without network
Q.State management anti-patterns
🔥
+
  • Multiple sources of truth: same data in Room AND a ViewModel variable AND a local var. Pick one — Room.
  • setState from non-main thread: StateFlow update from background thread without withContext(Dispatchers.Main) in non-coroutine code.
  • Exposing MutableStateFlow from ViewModel: UI can modify state directly. Always expose as StateFlow (immutable) from ViewModel.
  • Storing UI ephemeral state in ViewModel: scroll position, focus state, animation values belong in Compose/View layer with rememberSaveable.
  • Using SharedFlow for UI state: SharedFlow doesn't have a current value — new collectors miss previous emissions. Use StateFlow for state, SharedFlow only for one-shot events.
  • Not handling process death: ViewModel state survives rotation but not process death. Use SavedStateHandle for anything that must survive.
// ❌ Exposed mutable
val _state = MutableStateFlow(UiState())
val state = _state  // still MutableStateFlow!

// ✅ Properly sealed
val _state = MutableStateFlow(UiState())
val state: StateFlow<UiState> = _state.asStateFlow()  // read-only
Q.API consumption best practices on mobile
+
  • Always handle HTTP error codes: 401 → refresh token or re-login. 429 → back off and retry. 503 → retry with exponential backoff. 400 → don't retry, show error.
  • Validate API responses: don't trust the server to return the expected shape. Null-safe parsing, graceful degradation on unexpected fields.
  • Cache aggressively, invalidate deliberately: don't refetch on every screen open. Cache with TTL, invalidate on explicit user action or event.
  • Batch requests where possible: 10 individual calls vs 1 batch call. Design your API (or BFF) to support batching.
  • Use idempotency keys for mutations: any POST that creates or modifies should be retryable without side effects.
  • Log every outgoing request in debug: OkHttp HttpLoggingInterceptor. Strip in release builds.
  • Set timeouts: no timeout = potential infinite hang. Set connect, read, and write timeouts on OkHttpClient.
Q.How to discuss trade-offs in a technical interview
🔥
+

Senior engineers are expected to know the trade-offs, not just the "right answer". Every design choice has costs.

Framework for any trade-off question:

  1. Name both options clearly: "Option A is X, Option B is Y"
  2. State what each optimizes for: "A optimizes for speed, B for correctness"
  3. State the cost: "A trades off consistency, B trades off throughput"
  4. Give your recommendation WITH context: "For this use case (high write volume, eventual consistency OK), I'd choose A because..."

Examples you should know cold:

  • REST vs GraphQL → simplicity vs flexibility
  • Normalization vs denormalization → consistency vs read performance
