  • Optimistic UI vs pessimistic → UX speed vs correctness
  • Native vs cross-platform → performance vs development speed
  • SQL vs NoSQL → ACID vs scale/flexibility
  • Caching vs always-fresh → performance vs staleness
Red flag to avoid: "It depends" without following up. That's an incomplete answer. Always say: "It depends on X — if X is true, I'd choose A because... If Y, then B because..."
Q.Interview meta — handling tough questions and staying calm
🔥
+

When you don't know the answer:

Say: "I haven't worked with that specific technology, but let me reason through it from first principles..."

Never: sit silent, make something up, or say "I don't know" and stop there.

When you're stuck on a coding problem:

  1. Say "Let me think through this out loud..."
  2. Restate what you know and what you need
  3. Try a simpler version of the problem first
  4. Ask "Is it OK if I start with a brute-force approach and optimize?"

Time management in coding rounds (45 min):

  • 0-5 min: understand + clarify + examples
  • 5-10 min: think out loud, pick approach
  • 10-30 min: code, talk through logic
  • 30-40 min: trace through examples, fix bugs
  • 40-45 min: complexity analysis + follow-up discussion

Things that signal seniority:

  • Ask about constraints before coding (input size, time budget, device constraints)
  • State complexity before and after optimization ("this is O(n²), I can do O(n) with a hashmap")
  • Discuss trade-offs unprompted
  • Ask about production considerations ("how would this handle 10M users?")
★

API Types & Real-Time Patterns

REST vs GraphQL vs gRPC vs WebSocket vs SSE vs long polling — with code. When to choose each, rate limiting, API gateway, BFF patterns.

Q.API types — REST vs GraphQL vs gRPC vs WebSocket vs SSE vs Long Polling
🔥
+
TypeProtocolDirectionBest forAndroid
RESTHTTP/1.1-3Req → RespCRUD, most APIsRetrofit + OkHttp
GraphQLHTTPReq → RespFlexible queries, avoid over-fetchApollo Kotlin
gRPCHTTP/2Req + StreamingMicroservices, binary perfgRPC-Kotlin
WebSocketTCP upgradeBidirectionalChat, live updates, gamingOkHttp WS
SSEHTTP/1.1+Server → ClientLive feeds, progress, notificationsOkHttp + EventSource
Long PollingHTTPSimulated pushWebSocket fallbackRetrofit + loop
Decision rule: full-duplex (both sides send) → WebSocket. Server-to-client only → SSE (simpler). High-perf microservice → gRPC. Standard CRUD → REST. Mobile client needs exact fields → GraphQL.
Q.gRPC on Android — Protobuf, 4 communication patterns, implementation
+
// 1. Define service in .proto file
syntax = "proto3";
service UserService {
  rpc GetUser (UserRequest) returns (UserResponse);               // Unary
  rpc StreamUsers (StreamRequest) returns (stream UserResponse);  // Server stream
  rpc Chat (stream ChatMessage) returns (stream ChatMessage);     // Bidirectional
}
message UserRequest { string id = 1; }
message UserResponse { string id = 1; string name = 2; int32 age = 3; }
// 2. Dependencies
implementation("io.grpc:grpc-kotlin-stub:1.4.1")
implementation("io.grpc:grpc-android:1.57.0")

// 3. Create channel and stub
val channel = ManagedChannelBuilder.forAddress("api.example.com", 443)
    .useTransportSecurity().build()
val stub = UserServiceGrpcKt.UserServiceCoroutineStub(channel)

// 4. Unary call (like a REST request)
val response = stub.getUser(userRequest { id = "123" })
println(response.name)

// 5. Server streaming
stub.streamUsers(streamRequest { limit = 100 }).collect { user ->
    updateUI(user)
}

gRPC vs REST: gRPC wins for inter-service comms (microservices), binary perf, streaming, strongly-typed contracts enforced at build. REST wins for browser clients, caching, human-readable debugging, public APIs.

Q.Server-Sent Events (SSE) — one-way live streaming implementation
+

SSE streams server-to-client events over a single HTTP connection. Simpler than WebSocket when client doesn't need to send messages.

// SSE wire format — server sends text/event-stream
data: {"type":"price","symbol":"AAPL","value":189.50}

data: {"type":"price","symbol":"GOOG","value":141.20}

event: alert
data: {"message":"Market closed"}

id: 123
data: {"type":"update"}  // id enables reconnect resume
// Android — Flow-based SSE client
fun sseFlow(url: String): Flow<String> = callbackFlow {
    val request = Request.Builder().url(url)
        .addHeader("Accept", "text/event-stream")
        .addHeader("Cache-Control", "no-cache").build()
    val call = okhttp.newCall(request)
    call.enqueue(object : Callback {
        override fun onResponse(call: Call, response: Response) {
            val source = response.body?.source() ?: return
            while (!source.exhausted()) {
                val line = source.readUtf8Line() ?: break
                if (line.startsWith("data: "))
                    trySend(line.removePrefix("data: "))
            }
            // Reconnect with backoff when server closes
        }
        override fun onFailure(call: Call, e: IOException) { close(e) }
    })
    awaitClose { call.cancel() }
}

SSE vs WebSocket: SSE is simpler (standard HTTP, proxy-friendly, auto-reconnect via Last-Event-ID). WebSocket needed only when client also sends messages frequently.

Q.Long polling vs short polling — implementation and when to use
+
// Short polling — simple, wastes resources
suspend fun shortPoll(interval: Long = 5_000L) {
    while (isActive) {
        try {
            val update = api.checkForUpdates()
            if (update.hasNew) processUpdate(update)
        } catch (e: Exception) { /* ignore, retry */ }
        delay(interval)
    }
}

// Long polling — server holds request open until data available
suspend fun longPoll(timeout: Long = 30_000L) {
    while (isActive) {
        try {
            // Server blocks until something happens (or timeout)
            val response = api.waitForUpdate(timeout = timeout, lastId = lastId)
            lastId = response.id
            processUpdate(response)
            // Immediately re-request — no delay when data arrived
        } catch (e: TimeoutException) {
            // Normal — server had nothing, reconnect
        } catch (e: Exception) {
            delay(backoff)  // error — backoff before retry
        }
    }
}
Short PollLong PollWebSocket/SSE
LatencyUp to poll intervalNear real-timeReal-time
Server loadHigh (many requests)MediumLow (persistent)
Use whenSimple status checksWS blocked by firewall/proxyPreferred for real-time
Q.WebSocket lifecycle — connect, reconnect, state management
+
class WebSocketManager(private val client: OkHttpClient) {
    private var webSocket: WebSocket? = null
    private val _messages = MutableSharedFlow<String>()
    val messages = _messages.asSharedFlow()
    private var reconnectDelay = 1_000L

    fun connect(url: String, token: String) {
        val request = Request.Builder().url(url)
            .addHeader("Authorization", "Bearer $token").build()
        webSocket = client.newWebSocket(request, object : WebSocketListener() {
            override fun onOpen(ws: WebSocket, response: Response) {
                reconnectDelay = 1_000L  // reset on success
                ws.send("""{"type":"subscribe","channels":["updates"]}""")
            }
            override fun onMessage(ws: WebSocket, text: String) {
                scope.launch { _messages.emit(text) }
            }
            override fun onFailure(ws: WebSocket, t: Throwable, response: Response?) {
                scope.launch {
                    delay(reconnectDelay)
                    reconnectDelay = minOf(reconnectDelay * 2, 30_000L)  // cap at 30s
                    connect(url, token)
                }
            }
        })
    }
    fun send(msg: String) = webSocket?.send(msg) ?: false
    fun close() { webSocket?.close(1000, "done"); webSocket = null }
}
Q.Rate limiting on mobile — reactive (429) and proactive (token bucket)
L5+
+
// Reactive — handle 429 from server
class RateLimitInterceptor : Interceptor {
    override fun intercept(chain: Interceptor.Chain): Response {
        val response = chain.proceed(chain.request())
        if (response.code == 429) {
            val retryAfter = response.header("Retry-After")?.toLongOrNull() ?: 60L
            response.close()
            Thread.sleep(retryAfter * 1000)
            return chain.proceed(chain.request())
        }
        return response
    }
}

// Proactive — token bucket client-side throttle
class TokenBucket(private val capacity: Int, private val refillRatePerSec: Double) {
    private var tokens = capacity.toDouble()
    private var lastRefill = System.currentTimeMillis()
    @Synchronized fun tryConsume(): Boolean {
        val now = System.currentTimeMillis()
        tokens = minOf(capacity.toDouble(), tokens + (now - lastRefill) / 1000.0 * refillRatePerSec)
        lastRefill = now
        return if (tokens >= 1.0) { tokens -= 1.0; true } else false
    }
}

// Allow 10 req/sec, burst up to 50
val bucket = TokenBucket(capacity = 50, refillRatePerSec = 10.0)
if (bucket.tryConsume()) api.call() else queueForLater()
Q.API Gateway and BFF (Backend for Frontend) patterns
+

API Gateway — single entry point for all client requests. Handles routing, auth, rate limiting, SSL termination, caching. Your app calls one endpoint; the gateway routes to microservices.

BFF (Backend for Frontend) — a dedicated API layer built specifically for mobile. Returns exactly what the app needs — no over/under-fetching.

// Without BFF — mobile calls each service (expensive, over-fetches)
api.getUser("123")          // → User Service :8001 — returns 50 fields
api.getPosts("123")         // → Post Service :8002 — returns 20 fields each
api.getNotifications("123") // → Notification Service :8003
// 3 trips, hundreds of fields, mobile uses 10

// With BFF — one call, mobile-optimized payload
GET /mobile/v1/home/123 → {
    user: { name, avatar },           // just display fields
    unread_count: 3,                   // pre-computed
    featured_content: [...],           // pre-sorted, pre-filtered
    recent_posts: [...]                // limited to 5 items
}  // 1 trip, exactly what the screen needs
Samsara/Tesla relevance: their mobile teams almost certainly have BFF layers — dashboard endpoint returns vehicle status + alerts + map position in one call, pre-joined from 3-4 microservices server-side.
Q.Real-time pattern decision — which to use for each use case
🔥
+
Use casePatternWhy
Chat messagesWebSocketBidirectional — both sides send
Live stock pricesWebSocket or SSEServer pushes; SSE if client read-only
Background push (app killed)FCMOS-managed, battery-efficient
File upload progressSSEServer pushes progress; client already sent file
Order status trackingLong polling or SSEInfrequent updates; no need for full WebSocket
Collaborative editingWebSocket + OT/CRDTBoth sides edit; need conflict resolution
GPS tracking (active ride)WebSocketBidirectional: client sends position, server sends ETAs
IoT sensor data (Samsara)MQTT or UDPHigh volume, some loss OK, constrained devices
★

OS & Networking Fundamentals

OSI model, TCP/UDP, HTTP versions, TLS, DNS, semaphores, processes vs threads. The CS theory layer that senior interviews probe. Includes Alexa Voice SDK mapped to OSI layers.

Q.OSI 7-layer model — what each layer does (with diagram)
🔥
+

Mnemonic (bottom to top): Please Do Not Throw Sausage Pizza Away (Physical, Data Link, Network, Transport, Session, Presentation, Application)

Practical Android relevance: Layer 7 = your Retrofit/OkHttp code. Layer 6 = TLS (OkHttp handles). Layer 4 = TCP socket (OS handles). You code at 7; OS handles 1-4.

Q.Voice SDK / Alexa architecture mapped to OSI layers
🔥
+
OSI LayerAlexa Voice SDK mapping
7 — ApplicationAVS HTTP/2 API — directives and events JSON, capability negotiation, SpeechRecognizer/AudioPlayer namespaces
6 — PresentationOPUS encoding for mic audio, MP3/OPUS for TTS output, multipart HTTP bodies for audio chunks, JSON serialization
5 — SessionPersistent HTTP/2 downchannel connection, OAuth2 token management, reconnection logic, keep-alive pings
4 — TransportHTTP/2 over TLS (ALPN negotiated), multiplexed streams for simultaneous audio + directives, flow control
3 — NetworkIP routing to AVS endpoints (avs.amazon.com), DNS resolution, IPv4/IPv6
2 — Data LinkWiFi 802.11 frames or LTE/5G frames, MAC addressing
1 — PhysicalRadio waves (WiFi/cellular), microphone ADC converting sound to digital PCM
Interview angle: when asked about your Alexa/voice SDK work, framing it with OSI layers shows you understand the full stack from radio waves to application protocol — a rare depth signal that distinguishes L5 from L4.
Q.TCP vs UDP — when each is used on mobile
🔥
+
TCPUDP
ConnectionConnection-oriented (3-way handshake)Connectionless
ReliabilityGuaranteed delivery, ordering, no duplicatesBest-effort — packets can be lost or reordered
OverheadHigher (acks, retransmits, flow control)Low (8-byte header vs TCP's 20)
SpeedSlower (waits for acks)Faster (fire and forget)
Use forHTTP/HTTPS, REST APIs, WebSocket, file transferVideo streaming, VoIP, live GPS, DNS, gaming

HTTP/3 uses UDP! Via QUIC — reimplements reliability on top of UDP, eliminating TCP's head-of-line blocking. OkHttp supports HTTP/3 experimentally.

Mobile decision: Real-time voice/video → UDP (slight packet loss better than delay). REST APIs → TCP/HTTPS (correctness required). Samsara IoT telemetry → often UDP + custom reliability (high volume, some loss OK).

Q.HTTP/1.1 vs HTTP/2 vs HTTP/3 — key differences
🔥
+
HTTP/1.1HTTP/2HTTP/3 (QUIC)
TransportTCPTCPUDP (QUIC)
Multiplexing❌ 1 request/connection✅ Many streams, 1 TCP connection✅ Independent streams
Header compression❌ Repeated headers✅ HPACK✅ QPACK
Head-of-line blockingPer connectionPer TCP connection❌ None
Connection setup1 RTT1 RTT + TLS RTT0-RTT resume / 1-RTT new

Head-of-line blocking problem in HTTP/2: if one TCP packet is lost, ALL streams wait for retransmission. HTTP/3 streams are independent at the QUIC level — one lost packet blocks only that stream.

Android: OkHttp supports HTTP/2 automatically. Most Google, Meta, and Cloudflare APIs serve HTTP/2. This is why your app can make many API calls efficiently over one connection.

Q.Semaphore vs Mutex — the critical difference
🔥
+
MutexSemaphore
OwnershipOnly the locker can unlockAny thread can signal (release)
CountBinary (locked/unlocked)Counting (0 to N)
PurposeMutual exclusion — protect critical sectionLimit concurrency OR signal between threads
// Mutex — only one coroutine at a time
val mutex = kotlinx.coroutines.sync.Mutex()
mutex.withLock { sharedState++ }  // exclusive

// Semaphore — limit to 4 concurrent DB connections
val semaphore = kotlinx.coroutines.sync.Semaphore(permits = 4)
semaphore.withPermit { database.query() }  // max 4 at once

// Binary semaphore — signal between coroutines
val gate = Semaphore(permits = 0)  // starts closed
launch { gate.acquire(); processData() }   // waits
launch { loadData(); gate.release() }      // signals when ready
Analogy: Mutex = bathroom with one key (same person who took it returns it). Semaphore = parking lot counter (N cars allowed, any car can leave and free a spot).
Q.Process vs Thread vs Coroutine — hierarchy and cost
🔥
+
ProcessThreadCoroutine
MemoryIsolated address spaceShared heap, own stack (~1MB)Stackless (~hundreds of bytes)
Context switchVery expensive (TLB flush)Expensive (kernel scheduler)Near-zero (function call)
10,000 concurrent~10GB RAM~10GB RAM + OS limits~few MB
CommunicationIPC (Binder, pipes)Shared memory (sync needed)Channels, structured cancellation
Android exampleApp process, SystemServerMain, IO, RenderThreadviewModelScope.launch { }
Key insight: Coroutines run ON threads. A dispatcher (like Dispatchers.IO) manages which thread(s) coroutines run on. 1000 coroutines can share a pool of 4 IO threads — each suspends instead of blocking when waiting.
Q.Stack vs Heap — what lives where in Android
+
StackHeap
ContentsLocal variables, method frames, primitive values, object referencesAll objects (class instances, arrays)
SizeSmall (~1-8MB per thread, fixed)Large, dynamic (device RAM limit)
AllocationAutomatic LIFO — push on call, pop on returnGC-managed (Android ART)
SpeedExtremely fast (pointer move)Slower (GC overhead)
OverflowStackOverflowError (infinite recursion)OutOfMemoryError (bitmap, large objects)
fun example() {
    val x = 42           // x (Int) — on stack
    val name = "Rutul"  // reference on stack, String object on heap
    val user = User()   // reference on stack, User object on heap
}  // x, name, user references popped when method returns
   // User object on heap is GC-eligible if no other references remain
Q.DNS resolution — what happens before your HTTP request
+

Before any HTTP request, the OS resolves the hostname to an IP. This adds latency on first connection — typically 20-100ms.

  1. OS cache check — recently resolved, TTL still valid → use cached IP
  2. Router/ISP DNS — ask network's DNS resolver (e.g., 8.8.8.8)
  3. Recursive resolution — DNS resolver queries root → TLD → authoritative server
  4. Response cached — stored with TTL from DNS response

Android DNS behavior:

  • Caches per network (switching WiFi clears cache)
  • OkHttp caches resolved IPs for connection pool reuse
  • Happy eyeballs — tries IPv4 and IPv6 simultaneously, uses faster response

DNS pre-warming: force resolve your API endpoints at app start before the first real request needs them — eliminates the DNS latency from cold start.

Q.Context switching and OS scheduling — why it matters for mobile
+

Context switch: OS pausing one thread and resuming another — saves CPU registers, program counter, stack pointer, then loads the next thread's state. Takes microseconds but adds up with thousands of threads.

Why Android engineers care:

  • Too many threads → too many context switches → CPU overhead → battery drain
  • Android limits background process threads system-wide to reduce this
  • Coroutines eliminate context switches for waiting work — they SUSPEND instead of blocking, freeing the thread for other coroutines
// Set thread priority for background work
Thread {
    Process.setThreadPriority(Process.THREAD_PRIORITY_BACKGROUND)
    doHeavyWork()  // lower priority = less scheduling contention
}.start()

// Coroutine dispatchers handle priority automatically
// Dispatchers.Main = high priority (UI thread)
// Dispatchers.IO = bounded pool, lower priority
// Dispatchers.Default = CPU-count threads for compute
Q.TLS handshake — what happens on every HTTPS connection
+

Every HTTPS connection goes through a TLS handshake before data flows. On mobile, adds ~100-500ms on a fresh connection.

  1. TCP connect — 3-way handshake (1 RTT)
  2. ClientHello — TLS version, cipher suites, random nonce, SNI (server name)
  3. ServerHello — picked cipher, certificate, random nonce
  4. Certificate validation — verify chain to trusted CA, hostname match, expiry
  5. ECDHE key exchange — both sides compute shared secret without transmitting it
  6. Session keys derived — from shared secret + both nonces via HKDF
  7. Finished — both verify handshake integrity, encrypted data flows

Optimizations OkHttp does automatically:

  • Session resumption — skip full handshake on reconnect (0-RTT or 1-RTT)
  • Connection pool — reuse existing TLS connections (keep-alive)
  • ALPN — negotiate HTTP/2 inside the TLS handshake (no extra RTT)
★

SQL & Database Concepts

System design rounds always touch databases. ACID, JOINs, indexes, normalization, CAP theorem, N+1 problem — all missing until now. Know when to use SQL vs NoSQL.

Q.ACID properties — what they are and why they matter
🔥
+

ACID is the set of guarantees that make database transactions reliable.

PropertyMeaningExample
AtomicityAll operations succeed or ALL are rolled back. No partial state.Bank transfer: debit + credit both happen or neither happens
ConsistencyTransaction brings DB from one valid state to another. Constraints always hold.Balance never goes negative if constrained; foreign keys always valid
IsolationConcurrent transactions don't see each other's intermediate state.Two users booking the last seat don't both see it as available
DurabilityCommitted transactions survive crashes. Written to disk via WAL.After payment confirmation, server crash doesn't lose the charge

Isolation levels (trade isolation for performance):

LevelDirty ReadNon-repeatable ReadPhantom Read
Read UncommittedPossiblePossiblePossible
Read Committed (default PostgreSQL)❌PossiblePossible
Repeatable Read (default MySQL)❌❌Possible
Serializable❌❌❌ (slowest)
Android/Room: @Transaction wraps SQL in a SQLite transaction — atomicity guaranteed. SQLite default isolation is Serializable.
Q.Relational vs NoSQL — tradeoffs and when to use each
🔥
+
Relational (SQL)NoSQL
SchemaFixed — migrations for changesFlexible — schema-on-read
ACIDFullOften BASE (eventually consistent)
ScalingVertical primary; horizontal via sharding (complex)Horizontal — built-in sharding
QueriesPowerful JOINs, aggregationsLimited cross-collection queries

NoSQL types:

TypeExamplesBest for
DocumentMongoDB, FirestoreUser profiles, product catalogs, CMS
Key-ValueRedis, DynamoDBSessions, caching, rate limiting, leaderboards
Wide-ColumnCassandra, HBaseIoT telemetry, time-series, write-heavy analytics
GraphNeo4j, NeptuneSocial networks, recommendations, fraud detection
Samsara relevance: GPS telemetry → Cassandra/InfluxDB. Driver profiles → PostgreSQL. Real-time positions → Redis. Most production systems use polyglot persistence (multiple DB types together).
Q.CAP theorem — consistency vs availability vs partition tolerance
🔥
+

In any distributed system, when a network partition occurs, you can only guarantee 2 of 3:

  • Consistency — all nodes see the same data at the same time
  • Availability — every request gets a response (possibly stale)
  • Partition tolerance — system works despite network splits

P is non-negotiable — networks DO fail. Real choice: CP or AP.

ChoiceBehavior on partitionExamples
CPReturns error rather than stale dataHBase, ZooKeeper, PostgreSQL (strong mode)
APReturns possibly stale data rather than errorCassandra, DynamoDB, CouchDB, Redis
// Mobile implication — your app MUST handle AP backend behavior
// Optimistic UI: write locally, show immediately, reconcile on next fetch
val localResult = db.updatePost(post)   // show immediately
api.updatePost(post).catch { db.revert(post) }  // reconcile
Q.SQL JOIN types — INNER, LEFT, RIGHT, FULL, CROSS
🔥
+
-- INNER JOIN — only rows matching in BOTH tables
SELECT u.name, p.title
FROM Users u INNER JOIN Posts p ON u.id = p.user_id
-- Returns: users who have at least one post

-- LEFT JOIN — ALL left rows, matching right (NULL if none)
SELECT u.name, p.title
FROM Users u LEFT JOIN Posts p ON u.id = p.user_id
-- Returns: ALL users, even those with no posts (p.title = NULL)

-- RIGHT JOIN — all right rows. Rare; prefer LEFT JOIN instead

-- FULL OUTER JOIN — all rows from both, NULLs where no match
SELECT u.name, p.title
FROM Users u FULL OUTER JOIN Posts p ON u.id = p.user_id

-- CROSS JOIN — every combination (n*m rows). Used for combos/grids
SELECT color, size FROM Colors CROSS JOIN Sizes
// Room equivalent
@Query("SELECT u.name, p.title FROM User u INNER JOIN Post p ON u.id = p.userId")
fun getUsersWithPosts(): List<UserWithPost>
Q.Database indexes — B-tree, when to add, when not to
+

An index is a separate B-tree data structure that stores a sorted copy of column(s) with pointers to full rows. Turns O(n) table scan into O(log n).

-- Add index on frequently queried column
CREATE INDEX idx_post_user_id ON posts(user_id);
-- Now: WHERE user_id = 123 uses index — much faster at scale

-- Composite index — ORDER MATTERS (leftmost prefix rule)
CREATE INDEX idx_post_user_status ON posts(user_id, status);
-- Usable: WHERE user_id = 123 AND status = "published"
-- Usable: WHERE user_id = 123 (leftmost prefix)
-- NOT usable: WHERE status = "published" alone

When NOT to index: small tables (<1000 rows), low cardinality columns (boolean, status with 3 values), write-heavy tables (every write must update all indexes).

Room: @Entity(indices = [Index(value = ["userId"])]). Always index foreign keys and frequently-queried filter columns. Never index every column.
Q.N+1 query problem — what it is and how to fix it in Room
🔥
+

N+1 = 1 query to fetch a list, then N more queries for each item's related data. Causes serious DB overload at scale.

// ❌ N+1 — 1 query for posts + N queries for authors
val posts = dao.getAllPosts()           // 1 query
posts.forEach { post ->
    val author = dao.getUser(post.userId)  // N queries!
    display(post, author)
}

// ✅ Fix 1: JOIN query (single query)
@Query("SELECT p.*, u.name as authorName FROM Post p JOIN User u ON p.userId = u.id")
fun getPostsWithAuthors(): List<PostWithAuthor>

// ✅ Fix 2: Room @Relation (2 queries — but second is batched IN)
data class PostWithAuthor(
    @Embedded val post: Post,
    @Relation(parentColumn = "userId", entityColumn = "id")
    val author: User  // Room fetches all authors in one: SELECT * WHERE id IN (...)
)

// ✅ Fix 3: Batch IN query manually
val posts = dao.getAllPosts()
val userIds = posts.map { it.userId }.distinct()
val authorsMap = dao.getUsersByIds(userIds).associateBy { it.id }  // 1 query
posts.forEach { display(it, authorsMap[it.userId]) }
Q.Database normalization — 1NF, 2NF, 3NF in plain terms
+

1NF: each column holds atomic (single) values. No repeating groups.

-- ❌ Violates 1NF
orders: id=1, products="phone,case,charger"
-- ✅ 1NF
order_items: order_id=1, product="phone"
             order_id=1, product="case"

2NF: 1NF + every non-key column depends on the WHOLE primary key. Relevant when PK is composite.

-- ❌ Violates 2NF: product_name depends only on product_id, not full (order_id, product_id) PK
order_items: (order_id, product_id) PK, quantity, product_name
-- ✅ 2NF: split product to its own table
order_items: (order_id, product_id), quantity
products: product_id, product_name

3NF: 2NF + no transitive dependencies (non-key columns don't depend on other non-key columns).

-- ❌ Violates 3NF: zip_code → city → state (transitive)
users: id, zip_code, city, state
-- ✅ 3NF:
users: id, zip_code
zip_codes: zip_code, city, state
Denormalization trade-off: strict normalization = fewer anomalies, more JOINs. Denormalize intentionally for read performance. Analytics DBs often denormalize heavily (star schema / wide tables).
Q.Query optimization — EXPLAIN, indexes, and slow query patterns
+
-- PostgreSQL EXPLAIN ANALYZE
EXPLAIN ANALYZE SELECT * FROM posts WHERE user_id = 123 ORDER BY created_at DESC LIMIT 20;
-- Look for: "Seq Scan" (bad — full scan) vs "Index Scan" (good)
-- Look for: high estimated rows vs actual rows (statistics stale)

-- SQLite (Room debug)
EXPLAIN QUERY PLAN SELECT * FROM Post WHERE userId = 123;

Common slow patterns and fixes:

  • SELECT * — fetch only columns needed; avoids large row reads
  • No index on WHERE column → add index
  • LIKE '%search%' → leading wildcard kills index; use full-text search (Room FTS4)
  • WHERE LOWER(email) = x → function on indexed column skips index; store pre-lowercased
  • OFFSET 10000 LIMIT 20 → DB scans 10,020 rows then discards; use keyset pagination instead
  • N+1 queries → see dedicated question
// Room query debugging
Room.databaseBuilder(ctx, AppDb::class.java, "app.db")
    .setQueryCallback({ sql, args ->
        Log.d("SQL", "Query: $sql Args: $args")
    }, Executors.newSingleThreadExecutor())
    .build()
Q.Room database transactions — @Transaction and withTransaction
+
@Dao
interface TransferDao {
    @Transaction  // Room wraps in SQLite transaction — atomic
    suspend fun transfer(fromId: String, toId: String, amount: Int) {
        debit(fromId, amount)
        credit(toId, amount)
        logTransfer(fromId, toId, amount)
        // Any exception here → ALL three ops rolled back
    }

    @Query("UPDATE Account SET balance = balance - :amount WHERE id = :id")
    suspend fun debit(id: String, amount: Int)

    @Query("UPDATE Account SET balance = balance + :amount WHERE id = :id")
    suspend fun credit(id: String, amount: Int)
}

// Manual transaction for complex logic
db.withTransaction {
    val user = userDao.getUser(id)
    userDao.update(user.copy(points = user.points + 100))
    auditDao.log("points_added", id, 100)
    // Committed only if no exception; rollback otherwise
}
Why @Transaction matters in Room: without it, two concurrent reads between your two queries could see inconsistent data. With it, they're atomic and isolated.
Q.Choosing a database — decision guide for mobile backend scenarios
+
ScenarioRecommendedWhy
User accounts, profilesPostgreSQL / MySQLRelational, ACID, standard queries
Auth sessions / tokensRedisFast TTL expiry, in-memory speed
Real-time fleet GPS (Samsara)InfluxDB / TimescaleDBTime-series optimized, compression
Chat messagesCassandraWrite-heavy, time-ordered by conversation
Full-text searchElasticsearch / AlgoliaInverted index, relevance scoring
Social graph (follows)Neo4j or DynamoDBGraph traversal or denormalized at scale
Feature flags / configRedis or any K-VLow latency reads, simple structure
Analytics eventsClickHouse / BigQueryColumnar, fast aggregations at scale
LeaderboardsRedis Sorted SetsO(log n) insert + rank query built-in
★

TypeScript for React Native

Critical for Samsara and any RN role. Goes deep beyond the basics: generics, utility types, type narrowing, React component typing, common gotchas for Kotlin engineers.

Q.TypeScript type system — primitives, interface vs type alias
🔥
+

Primitives: string, number, boolean, null, undefined, bigint, symbol

interfacetype alias
Extendextends (opens for extension)& intersection (closed)
Merge declarations✅ Yes (declaration merging)❌ No
Union types❌✅ type A = X | Y
Primitive aliases❌✅ type ID = string
Use forObject shapes, APIs, class contractsUnions, primitives, function types, complex types
interface User { id: string; name: string; }
interface AdminUser extends User { permissions: string[]; }  // extension

type Status = "loading" | "success" | "error";  // union — must use type
type UserOrAdmin = User | AdminUser;             // union — must use type
type ID = string;                                // primitive alias
type Callback = (result: string) => void;       // function type
Q.Union types, intersection types, and discriminated unions
🔥
+
// Union — value can be one of several types
type StringOrNumber = string | number;
function format(val: StringOrNumber): string {
    if (typeof val === "string") return val.toUpperCase();  // narrowed to string
    return val.toFixed(2);  // narrowed to number
}

// Intersection — combine types
type Employee = Person & { department: string; salary: number; };

// Discriminated union — the key pattern for state machines
type ApiState<T> =
    | { status: "idle" }
    | { status: "loading" }
    | { status: "success"; data: T }
    | { status: "error"; message: string };

function render<T>(state: ApiState<T>) {
    switch (state.status) {
        case "loading": return <Spinner />;
        case "success": return <List data={state.data} />;  // TS knows data exists
        case "error":   return <Error msg={state.message} />;  // TS knows message exists
        // TS warns if a case is missing!
    }
}

Kotlin equivalent: sealed class → discriminated union. The when(state) exhaustiveness check works the same way.

Q.TypeScript generics — functions, interfaces, and constraints
🔥
+
// Generic function
function identity<T>(arg: T): T { return arg; }
const num = identity<number>(42);  // explicit
const str = identity("hello");      // inferred — T = string

// Generic with constraint
function getProperty<T, K extends keyof T>(obj: T, key: K): T[K] {
    return obj[key];  // type-safe property access
}
const name = getProperty({ name: "Rutul", age: 30 }, "name");  // string

// Generic React component (most common in RN)
interface ListProps<T> {
    items: T[];
    renderItem: (item: T) => React.ReactNode;
    keyExtractor: (item: T) => string;
}
function TypedList<T>({ items, renderItem, keyExtractor }: ListProps<T>) {
    return <FlatList data={items} renderItem={({ item }) => renderItem(item)}
        keyExtractor={keyExtractor} />;
}
// Usage: <TypedList<User> items={users} renderItem={u => <UserRow user={u} />} ... />
Q.Utility types — Partial, Required, Pick, Omit, Record, ReturnType
🔥
+
interface User { id: string; name: string; email: string; age?: number; }

// Partial<T> — all properties optional
type UserUpdate = Partial<User>;  // { id?: string; name?: string; email?: string; }
function updateUser(id: string, changes: Partial<User>) { /* ... */ }

// Required<T> — all properties required (opposite of Partial)
type FullUser = Required<User>;  // age is now required

// Pick<T, K> — take only certain keys
type UserSummary = Pick<User, "id" | "name">;  // { id: string; name: string; }

// Omit<T, K> — exclude certain keys
type PublicUser = Omit<User, "email">;  // { id: string; name: string; age?: number; }

// Record<K, V> — typed dictionary
type UserMap = Record<string, User>;  // { [key: string]: User }
const cache: Record<"loading"|"success"|"error", string> = { loading: "...", success: "ok", error: "fail" };

// ReturnType<T> — infer return type of function
function getUser() { return { id: "1", name: "Rutul" }; }
type UserShape = ReturnType<typeof getUser>;  // { id: string; name: string; }

// Readonly<T> — immutable
const user: Readonly<User> = { id: "1", name: "A", email: "a@b.com" };
// user.name = "B";  // ❌ Error
Q.Type narrowing and custom type guards
+
// typeof narrowing (primitives)
function process(val: string | number) {
    if (typeof val === "string") val.toUpperCase();  // string here
    else val.toFixed(2);  // number here
}

// instanceof narrowing (classes)
function handleEvent(event: MouseEvent | KeyboardEvent) {
    if (event instanceof KeyboardEvent) console.log(event.key);  // KeyboardEvent
    else console.log(event.clientX);  // MouseEvent
}

// in operator narrowing (object shapes)
type Cat = { meow: () => void };
type Dog = { bark: () => void };
function makeSound(animal: Cat | Dog) {
    if ("meow" in animal) animal.meow();  // Cat
    else animal.bark();  // Dog
}

// Custom type guard — is keyword
function isUser(obj: unknown): obj is User {
    return typeof obj === "object" && obj !== null &&
        "id" in obj && "name" in obj;
}
const data: unknown = await api.fetch();
if (isUser(data)) {
    console.log(data.name);  // TS knows it's User here
}
Q.Async/await with TypeScript — Promise typing and error handling
+
// Typed async function
async function fetchUser(id: string): Promise<User> {
    const response = await fetch(`/users/${id}`);
    if (!response.ok) throw new Error(`HTTP ${response.status}`);
    return response.json() as Promise<User>;
}

// Error handling — TypeScript catch is always unknown
async function safeLoad() {
    try {
        const user = await fetchUser("123");
        console.log(user.name);  // User is typed
    } catch (error) {
        // error is `unknown` in strict mode — must narrow
        if (error instanceof Error) console.error(error.message);
        else console.error("Unknown error");
    }
}

// Result type pattern (avoids try/catch everywhere)
type Result<T, E = Error> = { ok: true; data: T } | { ok: false; error: E };

async function safeFetch(id: string): Promise<Result<User>> {
    try {
        const data = await fetchUser(id);
        return { ok: true, data };
    } catch (error) {
        return { ok: false, error: error as Error };
    }
}
const result = await safeFetch("1");
if (result.ok) console.log(result.data.name);
else console.error(result.error.message);
Q.TypeScript with React Native — FC, hooks, props, events
🔥
+
import React, { useState, useCallback } from "react";
import { TextInput, TouchableOpacity, View, Text, StyleSheet } from "react-native";

// Typed props
interface SearchBarProps {
    placeholder?: string;
    onSearch: (query: string) => void;
    initialValue?: string;
    style?: ViewStyle;  // from RN — StyleProp<ViewStyle> for nested
}

// FC = FunctionComponent — includes children prop
const SearchBar: React.FC<SearchBarProps> = ({ placeholder = "Search...", onSearch, initialValue = "" }) => {
    const [query, setQuery] = useState<string>(initialValue);

    const handleSubmit = useCallback(() => {
        onSearch(query.trim());
    }, [query, onSearch]);

    return (
        <View>
            <TextInput
                value={query}
                onChangeText={(text: string) => setQuery(text)}  // typed event
                onSubmitEditing={handleSubmit}
                placeholder={placeholder}
            />
        </View>
    );
};

// Typed navigation (React Navigation)
type RootStackParams = {
    Home: undefined;
    Detail: { userId: string; showBack: boolean };
};
type DetailScreenProp = NativeStackNavigationProp<RootStackParams, "Detail">;

const DetailScreen: React.FC<{ navigation: DetailScreenProp, route: RouteProp<RootStackParams, "Detail"> }> = ({ route }) => {
    const { userId } = route.params;  // typed: string
};
Q.TypeScript enums — const enum vs enum vs union literals
+
enumconst enumUnion literal
Runtime object✅ Yes❌ Inlined at compile❌ Type only
Iterable✅❌❌
Bundle sizeLargerSmallestZero
Recommended❌ Mostly avoid🟡 Only if you need enum values by name✅ Preferred
// ❌ Regular enum — compiles to a JS object, harder to tree-shake
enum Direction { Up = "UP", Down = "DOWN" }

// ✅ Union literal — zero runtime cost, type-safe
type Direction = "UP" | "DOWN" | "LEFT" | "RIGHT";
const dir: Direction = "UP";  // only these 4 are valid

// ✅ const object — when you need the values at runtime too
const DIRECTION = { Up: "UP", Down: "DOWN" } as const;
type Direction = typeof DIRECTION[keyof typeof DIRECTION];  // "UP" | "DOWN"

// Why avoid regular enum:
// - Compiles to { "Up": "UP", "UP": "Up", ... } — reverse mapping
// - Not compatible with eslint @typescript-eslint/no-explicit-any rules
// - Breaks when using isolatedModules (required in many RN setups)
Q.Common TypeScript gotchas for Kotlin engineers
🔥
+
  • any vs unknown: any disables type checking completely. unknown is type-safe — must narrow before using. Always prefer unknown for API responses.
  • undefined vs null: TypeScript has both. undefined = declared but not assigned. null = intentionally absent. Use strictNullChecks: true always.
  • Non-null assertion (!): Same risk as Kotlin's !!. user!.name will throw at runtime if user is null. Use narrowing instead.
  • as casting: Not like Kotlin's as — TS doesn't actually verify at runtime. x as User tells the compiler "trust me", no actual conversion. Use type guards instead.
  • Structural typing: TS uses structural (duck) typing. If it has the right shape, it's compatible — no need to explicitly implement interfaces.
  • interface merging: Declaring the same interface twice MERGES them. Kotlin doesn't have this. Can cause unexpected behavior with third-party types.
  • Optional chaining: user?.address?.city — same as Kotlin user?.address?.city. Nullish coalescing: val ?? "default" — like Kotlin's Elvis val ?: "default".
// Kotlin  →  TypeScript
val x: String? = null        →  const x: string | null = null;
x?.length ?: 0               →  x?.length ?? 0;
if (x is String) x.length   →  if (typeof x === "string") x.length;
x!!                          →  x! (same risk, same bad idea)
Q.tsconfig basics — strict mode and key options for React Native
+
// tsconfig.json — React Native recommended base
{
  "compilerOptions": {
    "target": "esnext",           // output JS version
    "module": "commonjs",         // RN uses CommonJS modules
    "lib": ["esnext"],            // type defs available
    "jsx": "react-native",        // JSX transform for RN
    "strict": true,               // enables ALL strict checks below
    // strict mode enables:
    "strictNullChecks": true,     // null/undefined separate from other types
    "noImplicitAny": true,        // can't skip type annotations
    "strictFunctionTypes": true,  // function param/return type checking
    "strictPropertyInitialization": true, // class props must be initialized
    // Other important options:
    "noUnusedLocals": true,        // catch dead code
    "noUnusedParameters": true,
    "baseUrl": ".",               // allows absolute imports from project root
    "paths": {
      "@components/*": ["src/components/*"],  // import aliases
      "@screens/*": ["src/screens/*"]
    },
    "moduleResolution": "bundler"  // modern — understands exports field
  },
  "extends": "@react-native/typescript-config/tsconfig.json"
}
Enable strict: true immediately on new projects. Adding it later to an existing codebase surfaces hundreds of errors. Kotlin devs: strict TS feels like home — similar to Kotlin's default null safety and type inference.
★

Java for Android Interviews

Java fundamentals still tested at senior interviews. Kotlin compiles to JVM bytecode — you encounter Java APIs daily. Focus: differences from Kotlin, generics, collections, threading primitives.

Q.Java vs Kotlin — 10 most important differences
🔥
+
FeatureJavaKotlin
Null safetyNullPointerException at runtimeCompile-time null checks (?/!!)
Data classesManual equals/hashCode/toStringdata class generates all
Default argsMethod overloadingfun foo(x: Int = 0)
Extension fnsStatic utility classesfun String.cap() = ...
CoroutinesThreads/Executors/CompletableFuturesuspend fun + coroutineScope
Smart castManual instanceof + castAutomatic after is-check
PropertiesFields + getters/settersval/var with backing field
Sealed classesAbstract + limited subclasses (manual)sealed class enforced by compiler
Class final-by-defaultClasses open unless final keywordClasses final unless open keyword
String templates"Hello " + name"Hello $name"
Interview angle: Kotlin compiles to JVM bytecode — Java and Kotlin are interoperable. A Kotlin data class produces Java-compatible .class files with getters, equals(), hashCode(), etc. You call Kotlin from Java and vice versa seamlessly.
Q.Java generics — type erasure, wildcards, and why they matter
+

Type erasure: generic type info is removed at compile time. List<String> and List<Integer> are both List at runtime. This is why you can't do new T() or instanceof List<String>.

// Wildcards
void process(List<? extends Number> list) // upper bound — reads as Number
void add(List<? super Integer> list)      // lower bound — writes Integer
void print(List<?> list)                  // unknown — read-only, any type

// PECS: Producer Extends, Consumer Super
// If you read from it (produce) → extends
// If you write to it (consume) → super

Kotlin equivalent: out T = extends (covariant), in T = super (contravariant), * = wildcard. Kotlin handles this at compile time via declaration-site variance — cleaner than Java's use-site wildcards.

// Kotlin — declaration-site variance
interface Source<out T> { fun next(): T }  // covariant — T only comes OUT
interface Sink<in T> { fun put(item: T) }  // contravariant — T only goes IN
Q.Java 8+ features — what you encounter in Android codebases
+

Android supports Java 8 language features via desugaring (D8/R8). Key features that appear in Android code:

// Lambda expressions
Runnable r = () -> System.out.println("hello");
list.sort((a, b) -> a.compareTo(b));

// Method references
list.forEach(System.out::println);  // instance method ref
Stream.of("a","b").map(String::toUpperCase); // static ref

// Stream API (Android supports via desugaring)
List<String> filtered = names.stream()
    .filter(n -> n.startsWith("A"))
    .sorted()
    .collect(Collectors.toList());

// Optional — avoid null returns
Optional<String> name = Optional.ofNullable(getName());
name.ifPresent(n -> show(n));
String result = name.orElse("default");

// Default interface methods
interface Greeter {
    String greet(String name);
    default String greetAll(List<String> names) {
        return names.stream().map(this::greet).collect(Collectors.joining(", "));
    }
}
Android note: Java Stream API available from API 24+ via desugaring. Kotlin's standard library collections (map, filter, forEach) achieve the same on all API levels — prefer those in new code.
Q.Java inner classes — static nested vs inner vs anonymous vs local
+
TypeHolds outer ref?Use for
Static nested❌ NoLogically grouped helpers (Builder classes, ViewHolder)
Inner class✅ Yes — memory leak risk!Needs outer state. Avoid in Android — use static + WeakReference
Anonymous✅ Yes (if non-static context)One-off callbacks (deprecated with lambdas/coroutines)
Local✅ Captures effectively-final varsRarely used — prefer lambdas
// ❌ Inner class memory leak — holds Activity reference
public class MyActivity extends Activity {
    class MyHandler extends Handler {  // holds Activity implicitly
        public void handleMessage(Message msg) { ... }
    }
}

// ✅ Static nested — safe
public class MyActivity extends Activity {
    static class MyHandler extends Handler {
        WeakReference<MyActivity> ref;
        MyHandler(MyActivity a) { ref = new WeakReference<>(a); }
    }
}
Q.Java exception hierarchy — checked vs unchecked
+

Java has two branches: checked (must declare or catch) and unchecked (runtime errors, no requirement).

Throwable
├── Error             — JVM problems (OutOfMemoryError, StackOverflow)
│                       Do NOT catch normally
└── Exception
    ├── RuntimeException — UNCHECKED (bugs: NullPointer, IndexOutOfBounds,
    │                       IllegalArgument, ClassCast)
    └── IOException      — CHECKED (must declare: FileNotFoundException,
                            SQLException, ClassNotFoundException)

Rule: checked exceptions = caller must handle (IO, network, DB). Unchecked = programmer errors that shouldn't occur.

Kotlin difference: Kotlin has NO checked exceptions. All exceptions are unchecked. Java interop: @Throws(IOException::class) on Kotlin functions tells Java callers to handle it.

// Kotlin — no checked exceptions
suspend fun readFile(): String {  // no throws declaration needed
    return File("path").readText()  // IOException is unchecked in Kotlin
}

// For Java callers:
@Throws(IOException::class)
fun javaFriendlyRead(): String = File("path").readText()
Q.Java Collections — which implementation when
🔥
+
InterfaceImplementationOrdered?Unique?Use for
ListArrayList✅ insertion❌General-purpose list, O(1) get by index
ListLinkedList✅ insertion❌Frequent insert/delete at middle; also as Queue/Deque
SetHashSet❌✅O(1) add/contains/remove; no order needed
SetLinkedHashSet✅ insertion✅Unique + insertion order preserved
SetTreeSet✅ sorted✅Sorted unique elements, range queries
MapHashMap❌keysO(1) get/put; most common
MapLinkedHashMap✅ insertionkeysLRU cache (override removeEldestEntry), ordered iteration
MapTreeMap✅ key sortedkeysRange queries, floor/ceiling ops
QueuePriorityQueuepriority❌Min/max heap; top-K problems
DequeArrayDeque✅❌Stack + Queue; faster than Stack class
Kotlin note: Kotlin's listOf() returns an unmodifiable Java List. mutableListOf() returns ArrayList. mapOf() returns LinkedHashMap under the hood (insertion-ordered).
Q.equals() and hashCode() contract — why both must be overridden together
+

The contract: if a.equals(b) is true, then a.hashCode() must equal b.hashCode().

Breaking this contract causes bugs in HashMaps and HashSets — objects that are "equal" end up in different buckets and can't be found.

// ❌ Overrides equals but not hashCode — breaks HashMap
class Point(val x: Int, val y: Int) {
    override fun equals(other: Any?) =
        other is Point && x == other.x && y == other.y
    // Missing hashCode! Two equal Points → different hash buckets!
}

val map = HashMap<Point, String>()
map[Point(1,2)] = "A"
println(map[Point(1,2)])  // null!! — different hashCode used for lookup

// ✅ Override both
class Point(val x: Int, val y: Int) {
    override fun equals(other: Any?) = other is Point && x == other.x && y == other.y
    override fun hashCode() = Objects.hash(x, y)
}

// Kotlin data class handles this automatically — use data class
data class Point(val x: Int, val y: Int)  // equals + hashCode + copy + toString
Q.Comparable vs Comparator — when to use each
+
  • Comparable — the class defines its OWN natural ordering. Implement compareTo(). Single default sort order per class.
  • Comparator — external sorting logic. Multiple orderings for the same class. Pass as lambda to sort functions.
// Comparable — natural order IS part of the class definition
class Employee(val name: String, val salary: Int) : Comparable<Employee> {
    override fun compareTo(other: Employee) = this.salary.compareTo(other.salary)
}
employees.sort()  // uses natural order (by salary)

// Comparator — external, multiple orderings
val byName = Comparator<Employee> { a, b -> a.name.compareTo(b.name) }
val bySalaryDesc = compareByDescending<Employee> { it.salary }

employees.sortWith(byName)
employees.sortedWith(bySalaryDesc)

// Kotlin shorthand
employees.sortedBy { it.name }         // ascending
employees.sortedByDescending { it.salary }  // descending
employees.sortedWith(compareBy({ it.dept }, { it.name }))  // multi-key
Q.Java threading primitives — synchronized, wait/notify, volatile
+

synchronized: intrinsic lock — one thread at a time in the block. Uses object's monitor.

// Synchronized method
synchronized fun increment() { count++ }

// Synchronized block (finer granularity)
fun process() {
    synchronized(lockObject) { /* critical section */ }
    // other work continues outside lock
}

// wait/notify — thread coordination
synchronized fun waitForData() {
    while (queue.isEmpty()) wait()  // releases lock, waits
    processNext()
}
synchronized fun addData(item: T) {
    queue.add(item)
    notify()  // wake one waiting thread
    // notifyAll() — wake all waiting threads
}

volatile: ensures visibility across threads (write immediately visible to all readers). Does NOT guarantee atomicity.

@Volatile var running = true  // Kotlin @Volatile = Java volatile
// One thread writes: running = false
// Other threads see the update immediately (no CPU cache)
Modern Android: prefer coroutines + Mutex over raw synchronized/wait/notify. Java threading primitives are lower-level and more error-prone. Know them for interviews; use coroutines in production.
Q.Java memory — strong, soft, weak, and phantom references
+
TypeGC behaviorUse for
StrongNever collected while referencedNormal references — everything
SoftCollected only when JVM needs memoryMemory-sensitive caches (image cache)
WeakCollected at next GC regardlessCaches where stale is OK; avoiding retain cycles (WeakReference to Activity)
PhantomAlready finalized; for cleanup actionsPre-mortem cleanup notification (rarely used)
// WeakReference — Android classic pattern to avoid memory leaks
class MyHandler(activity: MainActivity) : Handler(Looper.getMainLooper()) {
    private val ref = WeakReference(activity)
    override fun handleMessage(msg: Message) {
        ref.get()?.updateUI()  // null if Activity was GC'd
    }
}

// SoftReference — in-memory image cache
val cache = HashMap<String, SoftReference<Bitmap>>()
fun getBitmap(key: String): Bitmap? = cache[key]?.get()  // null if GC'd under pressure
fun putBitmap(key: String, bmp: Bitmap) { cache[key] = SoftReference(bmp) }
19

Behavioral / STAR

Underrated at L5. Strong technical with weak behavioral = no offer. Your STAR story bank should cover every Amazon LP.

Q.What is STAR and how do you structure an answer?
🔥
+
  • Situation (1-2 sentences) — set context. What was the problem?
  • Task (1 sentence) — what was YOUR responsibility?
  • Action (60-70% of answer) — specific things YOU did. Use "I", not "we", for your contributions.
  • Result (1-2 sentences) — measurable outcome. Numbers if possible.

Total time: 2-3 minutes. Practice cold — record yourself.

Q.Your STAR story bank — what topics to prepare?
🔥
+

Have 1-2 stories ready for each:

  1. Technical challenge you solved → fault rate reduction observability, Widevine integration
  2. Conflict / disagreement → security risk reclassification, dependency disagreement with foundations team
  3. Leadership / influence without authority → Type with Alexa expansion, away-team coordination
  4. Failure / learning → initial encryption metric spike + dial-down, recovery
  5. Mentorship / teaching → onboarding 10+ engineers, design doc and process guides
  6. Cross-functional collaboration → PM/TPM/QA/Marketing on international launch
  7. Customer obsession / user impact → fault attribution leading to CX improvements at 100M+ scale
  8. Bias for action / ambiguity → AI tooling adoption (Kiro/Amazon Q context engineering)
Q."Tell me about a time you disagreed with a teammate"
+

Template for your story:

  • S: Encryption library scope expansion (~300KB APK increase) when app was already at 100MB
  • T: Convince foundations team / find alternative without sacrificing user trust
  • A: (1) Documented the tradeoff in a design doc with size impact, security tradeoffs, and alternative options. (2) Proactively researched MobileCryptoAndroid as a smaller alternative. (3) Aligned with senior engineers on the path. (4) Made the call to switch libraries after stakeholders weighed in.
  • R: Shipped encryption with minimal size impact; classification reduced from Critical to Highly Confidential; established a pattern for future similar reviews.
Q."Tell me about a time you had to deliver under pressure"
+

Template — Type with Alexa international launch:

  • S: Launch window for international expansion required clearing 35+ blocking tickets in under a month — locale-specific failures across 14 marketplaces, 20 locales
  • T: Drive triage and resolution to unblock the launch on schedule
  • A: (1) Triaged tickets by criticality and locale impact. (2) Partnered with PM/TPM/QA/Marketing daily for prioritization. (3) Identified a sanitization failure blocking 2 marketplaces — fixed and validated. (4) Coordinated phased verification across locales.
  • R: Launch shipped on time; opened US-only feature to 14 international marketplaces; downstream campaign added ~18.6% adoption.
Q."Tell me about a time you failed"
+

Template — initial encryption dial-up failure:

  • S: Q3'23 dial-up of encryption changes detected a spike in Load_Encrypted_Shared_Pref_Emp_Error metric
  • T: Diagnose root cause; restore safe state; re-ship with confidence
  • A: (1) Dialed down to protect customers. (2) Investigated multi-layer (SDK + foundation library + flag dial logic). (3) Identified edge cases in flag dial-up/dial-down handling and a decrypt SecurityException. (4) Simplified the encryption/decryption code path. (5) Authored CRs to fix flag handling and the security exception path. (6) Validated through bug bash before re-ramp.
  • R: Re-shipped encryption with substantially lower error rate; pattern of "ship → measure → fix → re-ship" reinforced as the team norm.
Q.What are the Amazon Leadership Principles to know?
+

Even outside Amazon, many ex-Amazon interviewers use the LP framework. The most-asked at SDE II/L5 level:

  • Customer Obsession — work that traces back to user benefit
  • Ownership — long-term thinking, not handing off problems
  • Dive Deep — knowing details, root-cause work
  • Bias for Action — making decisions with incomplete info
  • Earn Trust — admit mistakes, listen, treat others well
  • Deliver Results — focus on the right things, even with obstacles
  • Are Right, A Lot — strong judgment + open to data
  • Invent and Simplify — drive simplification, not just new features
